This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission that visited Mexico in June/July 1992.
The mission was composed of Mmes./Messrs. Michelle Riboud (Mission Leader/Sr. Economist,
LA2HR), Ana Revenga (Economist, LA2C1), Hong Tan (Economist, ENIN), Bo Dahlborg (Sr.
Technical Education Specialist, EC3HR), Mauricio Mathov (lnformation Tecinology Specialist,
ASTIF), Maria Elena Anderson (Operations Analyst, Consultant), and Muriel Aza (Consultant). Mr.
John Innes (LA2HR) finalized the report at headquarters. Messrs. Rainer B. Stbckhan (LA2DR) and
Kye Woo Lee (LA2HR) are the Department Director and Division Chief, respectively, for this
operation. Peer reviewers for this project were Messrs. Sweder Van Wijnbergen (EC2DR) and Neil
Roger (LA2C1). Messrs. Vinh Nguyen, Darren Dorkin, and David Warren (LA2HR) contributed to
the report at headquartrs.

MEXIC
LABOR MARKET AND PRODUCTIVITY ENHANCEMENT PROJECT
LOAN AND PROJECR
Br:wn~          Nacional Financiera, S.N.C. (NAFIN)
Gur:nt~r        United Mexican States
Secretariat of Labor and Social Welfare (STPS)
Ag=
Beiciaries:    Entrepreneurs and employees of micro, small and medium-size enterprises (MSMEs),
enterprise associations, unemployed and displaced workers, and rural migrants
Annl0t:         US$174.0 million equivalent
Terms:          Repayment in 15 years, including five years of grace at the standard variable interest
rate.
ProjectMThe objective of the proposed project is to assist the Government of Mexico in
Obectives:      promoting private sector-led growth and in easing the costs of labor mobility and labor
market adjustment by: (i) increasing the productivity and competitiveness of micro,
small and medium-sized enterprises; (ii) reducing costs incurred by employees and
employers of hiring and job search, and providing education and training opportunities
for displaced and unemployed workers, with special attention to women; and (iii)
improving the availability, timeliness and dissemination of labor market information and,
more broadly, the capacity to monitor labor market changes and to design, evaluate and
implement relevant policies and programs in the sector.
Projet          'The proposed project with a total cost of US$355.9 million would consist of three major
esaripti:       components: (a) Productivity Enhancement Comonent (28% of total costs, including
contingencies) would support activities aimed at increasing the productivity and
competitiveness of MSMEs by: (i) extending the coverage of the pilot in-service
training program (Multiple Support Service Program to MSMEs, CIMO) initiated under
the Manpower Training Project (Ln. 2876-ME in 1987) and expanding the scope of
support services provided to MSMEs; (ii) strengthening coordination between CIMO and
other institutions rendering support to MSMEs; and (iii) promoting the gradual
privatization of CIMO by increasing the involvement of enterprise associations in its
financing and operation and gradual transformation of Regional Productivity Support
Units (UPCs); (b) Labor Marklet Adustment Co   t (61% of total costs, including
contingencies) would improve the coverage and efficiency of State Employment Services
(SEEs) and provide education and training opportunities for displaced workers, through:
(i) strengthening the operation and management of the SEEs, and improving the quality
and availability of information on local and regional labor markets; and (ii) providing
improved retraining programs for approximtely 300,000 displaced and unemployed
workers, meeting stringent selection criteria; and (iii) introducing, on a pilot basis, an
education and training program for under-qualified rural migrants who are ineligible for



- iv -
the regular reraining program; (c)   n&   oi  and
CoXoneQ (11 % of total costs, including contingencies) would improve the availability,
timoliness and diswmtion of labor market information, and strengthen institutional
capacity to design, operate and evduate labor market policies and programs, including:
(i) supporting the development of an information system and the undertaking of in-depth
surveys to provide a basis for periodic and systematic labor market data generation and
analysis; (ii) promoting an extensive program of studies and resarch on labor market
issues, programs and policies; (iii) fostering regular publication and dissemination of
labor market information; (iv) instituting a systematic framework for program
evaluation; and (v) strengthening STPS's management and technical capacity through
human resource development and modernization of data management system.
frgjw           The project would increae productivity and competitiveness in MSMEs, creating higher
earnings and employment opportunities for Mexican workers. About 35,000 enterprises,
employing about 1.0 million workers, are expected to be reached. Project benefits,
however, would extend beyond the participating enterprises, as the project is intended to
have an important demonstration effect. Through the increased involvement of private
enterprise in MSME support programs and the promotion of closer inter-institutional
links, the project would also foster improvements in the quality of training services
supplied to enterprises and a faster rate of adoption of new technologies through
provision of a better trained labor force. The labor market adjustment process would be
eased as the improved quality of services provided to unemployed and displaced workers
would reduce the costs of hiring and job search, improve the job matching process and
thus provide better opportunities for employment stability and subsequent wage growth.
Approximately 2 million unemployed workers are expected to benefit from placement
services over the project life. About 300,000 unemployed workers whose current skills
fail to match those in demand would also benefit from the training to be provided.
The cost effectivenass of the retraining programs will be enhanced by targeting the
training to those groups (workers with at least completed primary education and some
work experience) where evaluation has demonstrated the highest rates of return to such
training. The results of the evaluation showed a 20 to 30% monthly salary increase for
male trainee with some prior work experience. For male target trainees, the evaluation
showed that benefits of program participation outweigh the costs of the program within 3
months of starting employment. For younger i--nex nced workers and rural migrants,
the project is expected to improve their entry conditions in the urban labor market. In
addition to sharing the general benefits of the project listed above, women would benefit
from the improvements to be introduced in the training programs and reduction of labor
market barriers which hamper the realization of the full benefits of training by females.
Finally, the project would enhance the Government's capacity to design, monitor and
evaluate labor market policies and programs, thus contributing to a more efficient use of
public resources.
The main risks are: (a) a possible decline in Government commitment to project
objectives after the change in administration in 1994. The risk is small given the
existing strong support for current economic policies and the importance given to
productivity and mobility issues in light of the North American Free Trade Agreement
(NAFTA); (b) insufficient budgetary allocations to guarantee program sustainability
beyond project completion. This risk will be minimized by the coverage of trainee
stipends and recurrent costs on a declining basis, by increased private sector
participation i the fcing and operation of the CIMO program, and by rigorous



- v -
monitoring of budgetary allocations during the annual project implementation reviews;
(c) slower than expected acceptance by enterprises of the productivity enhancement
actions. This risk quite small given growing support by private enterprises of
interventions initiated under the ongoing Bank-supported Manpower Training Project and
will be further mitigated by strengthened program management and availability of
services; and (d) project complexity and the fact that there are many implementing
agencies at different levels may limit the achievement of the project objectives. This
risk will be mitigated through the early identification and resolution of any problems
which develop through close supervision, and the mid-term and annual project reviews.

DEPINrIrIQS
Crude Death Rue                  The number of deaths per 1,000 population in a given year.
Infant Mortality Rate            The number of deaths of infants under one year of age in a given
year per 1,000 live births in that year.
Life Expetancy at Birth          The average number of years an infant would live if the current
age/sex-specific mortality trends prevailing at the time of birth were
to continue.
Total Fertility Rate             The average number of children who would be born alive to a
woman during her lifetime if she were to pass through her
childbearing years conforming to the age-specific fertility rates of a
given year.



I. INTRODUCTION
1.1.  Over the past six years, Mexico has successfully implemented a program of sweeping economic
reforms and made major strides towards a complete restructuring of its economy. Until 1985 the Mexican
economy was highly protected, dominated by state-run industries and heavily burdened by debt. As a result
of the policy changes and reforms that have taken place since then, the economy and Mexico's prospects
today are significantly different. A fast and far-reaching trade reform has turned Mexico into one of the
more open economies in the world. Domestically, the reforms have been equally comprehensive. The
financial sector has been restructured and liberalized, fiscal expenditures have been cut dramatically, and the
tax system has been overhauled. Key sectors, such as transport and telecommunications, have been
deregulated. Moreover, in a still ongoing process of privatization, more than two thirds of state enterprises
have been sold, closed or spun off. More recently, reforms have extended to the agriculture sector. The
rural financial system has been restructured, output pricing has been substantially liberalized and the ejido
system (Mexico's system of communal farming ownership) is being reformed.
1.2.   The Government's success in stabilizing, liberalizing and privatizing the economy has set the stage
for long-term economic development. However, sustaining private sector-led growth will demand more
than an adequate set of incentives; it will require finance for investment, improved physical infrastructure,
and, especially, an educated and skilled labor force. The latter is critical to the country's ability to increase
the productivity and living standards of its workers, to produce higher quality goods and to compete on
international markets. Moreover, full success of the adjustment program requires policies that are effective
in promoting social mobility and a more equal distribution of the gains from economic reform. These
objectives can be achieved only through sustained investments in human capital.
1.3.   Further liberalization of the economy through the expected North American Free Trade Agreement
(NAFTA) with Canada and the U.S. will accentuate the need for an educated and skilled labor force.
NAFTA will represent an unprecedented integration of large economies at very different stages of
development. Mexico's capability to compete with more developed U.S. and Canadian producers will hinge
on its ability to increase the productivity of its workers, adopt and improve new production processes and
technologies, train less-skilled workers, and develop new skills as the structure of demand evolves. It will
also require implementation of policies aimed at increasing labor mobility and facilitating the flow of labor
between expanding and contracting sectors and regions.
1.4.   Conscious of these needs, the Mexican Government is now giving high priority to the development
of human resources through investments in human capital both before and after entry into the labor market.
It is also making major efforts to reduce the cost of labor mobility and labor market adjustment. The
proposed project would assist the Government in meeting these goals.
II. TBE LABOR MARKET
A. OVERVIEW
2.1.   Over the last four decades, economic development has brought substantial changes in the structure
of the economy and the sectoral distribution of the labor force. The proportion of the labor force working
in the primary sector has fallen from about 58% in 1950 to 23% in 1990. Employment in the



- 2 -
manufacturing and service sectors has increased more than threefold to 74% of the labor force.-1 Output
from these sectors now accounts for 92% of GDP. In response to the emergence of new work
opportunities, important migration flows towards urban areas and high-income states have taken place As a
result, the proportion of the population living in rural areas has declined from 57% in 1950 to 29% today.
'Me skill levels of the population and labor force have increased significantly as illiteracy rates have fallen
from 52% in 1950 to 11 % in 1990. At present, 56% of the total population (10 and over) has at least
completed primary education (6 years of schooling) and 37% has 7 years of schooling or more (see Annex
2, Table 1). For the younger cohorts these proportions are much higher: 75% of the population 20-29
years of age has at least finished primary school, and 56% has 7 years of schooling or more. These
changes have been paralleled by an increase in the standards of living of the population: in spite of the
sharp economic contraction between 1982 and 1989, income per capita has more than doubled since 1950.
B.    LABOR MARKET ISSUES
2.2.   The main issues to be addressed in the labor market are related to: (a) labor productivity growth;
(b) unemployment and labor mobility; (c) the capacity of the labor market to absorb new labor force
entrants and migrant flows from rural areas; and (d) the monitoring and policy-making capacity of labor
market agencies and institutions.
1.    Labor Productivity Growth
2.3.   Labor productivity growth in Mexican manufacturing has been sluggish. Between 1982 and 1989,
output per hour of labor in manufacturing grew at a rate of 1.5% per year. In contrast, output per hour in
manufacturing in the U.S. and Canada, grew by 4.2% and 2.5% per year respectively.' Other OECD
countries also experienced higher productivity increases during the same period: output per hour grew by
4.1 % per year in Japan, 3.4% per year in France, and 5.5% in the U.K. Possible explanations for these
differences is that Mexican workers are less educated and incur substantially less on-the-job human capital
accumulation than do their counterparts in the group of industrialized and developing countries listed in
Table 2.1. Evidence for this hypothesis is provided by the fact that experience-wage profiles for Mexican
workers are flatter than those estimated for these countries. As shown in Table 2.1, the increase in wages
associated with an additional year of work experience for males is 3.8% in Mexico, as compared to 8.1% in
the U.S, 8.4% in Japan, and 9.1 % in France. This is low even when compared to countries at a similar
level of development and with comparable education indicators, such as Brazil (6.2%) and Colombia
(5.8%). In contrast, it is similar to what could be observed 15 years ago in the poorest region of Spain.
Given the correlation between wage growth, on-the-job training and productivity observed in most countries,
these differences can be associated with less post-school investment in human capital and lower productivity
growth.
2.4. Direct evidence on the proportion of workers receiving training reinforces the argument that
Mexican workers experience less post-school training than do their counterparts in other countries.
According to data from the training module of the 1991 National Labor Force Survey, only 24% of
Mexican workers report having received training to improve their skills on the job. In contrast,

2.5.   Sizeable differences exist in factor productivity growth according to firm size. Low labor
productivity appears to be particularly prevalent in micro, small and medium sized enterprises (MSMEs),
which account for a large fraction of employment in Mexico. MSMEs represent 71 % of total employment,
53 % of employment in manufacturing, 95 % of employment in the retail sector, and 73 % of employment in
services. Within manufacturing, micro and small enterprises account for 48% of employment in the food
industry, 68% of employment in textiles, shoes and leather, and 62% of employment in wood and paper
products. These are precisely the sectors experiencing lower labor productivity growth, and lower total
factor productivity growth. Moreover, within sectors, total factor productivity growth is positively
correlated with enterprise size (see Annex 2, Tables 2 and 3).
2.6.   Lower labor productivity growth in MSMEs is associated with less investment in worker training.
As Table 2.2 shows, only 9% of workers in micro enterprises and 15% of those in small firms report
having received any form of in-service training, as compared to 30%  of workers in large firms. Less
investment in training translates into lower on-the-job human capital accumulation, less ability to introduce
innovation and adjust to technological change, and thus, lower productivity growth. Several factors explain
why MSMEs invest less in the training of their workers:

(a)    Workers in MSMEs have less formal schooling than workers in larger enterprises (soe Table
2.2). Given that schooling and post-school training tend to be complementary invetments,
this would explain the lower demand for training in MSMEs;
(b)    MSMEs in Mexico are likely to be less innovative than larger firms and use more
standardized and outdated technology. Thus, skills learned on the job are likely to be less
firm-specific; this, in turn, diminishes the firm's incentive to provide training. Table 2.2
(column 4) shows that the proportion of training financed by the firm is much lower in small
than large firms;

(c)    MSMEs experience higher turnover than larger firms.5' High employment turnover
discourages the firm from investing in its workers, by reducing the probability of recouping
the investment. At the same time, lack of on-the-job-training is likely to exacerbate
employment turnoverY High labor turnover in MSMEs is thus both cause and
consequence of lower on-the-job human capital formation;
(d)    Scale economies, if they exist and are important, may limit the capacity of MSMEs to
provide training. If transaction costs linked to the organization and management of training
are substantial, firms needing to train only a few workers may find that the costs of
investing in training exceed the returns. Because of scale economies, MSMEs may be less
able to provide in-house training and rely more on external institutions than larger firms.
However, such external training may be less effective and more costly than in-house
training.1' Furthermore, the benefits of training may be harder to achieve in MSMEs
because of fewer in-firm rotation or promotion possibilities for trained employees;
(e)    More limited access to capital markets tend to prevent MSMEs from borrowing to finance
training. More stringent constraints imply less investment in training; AND

(f)    Although Mexican Labor Law mandates that training programs be included as part of
collective bargaining agreements, in practice it is difficult to enforce compliance. This is
particularly true for MSMEs, which are less likely to be caught deviating from the law than
larger enterprises.
2.7.   Lower labor productivity growth in MSMEs can also be attributed to other factors complementary to
training, such as the level of technological know-how, marketing and managerial skills, knowledge of export
markets, and coordinated relationships with suppliers and customers. Also economies os scale in
information usage may create an incentive for larger-scale operators to acquire more information than small-
scale operators.F
2.    Unemployment and Labor Mobiity
2.8.   A superficial review of unemployment figures in Mexico raises little concern about unemployment
levels. Official statistics drawn from employment surveys report a low open unemployment rate, which
currently stands at 2.8%. An analysis of recent trends indicates that even in the worst years of the
adjustment process and despite the rapid and far-reaching reforms, average official unemployment rates did
not increase beyond 6.1%. The stabilization and liberalization process has evolved without social unrest,
monitored by the Pact for Stability and Economic Growth (Pacto para la Estabilidad, la Competitvidad y cl
Empleo, PACTO), later renamed Pact for Stability, Competitiveness, and Employment (Pacto para la
Estabilidady cl Crecimiento Economico, PECE), a carefully engineered political agreement between labor,
business and the Government.
2.9.   However, a deeper analysis of the nature of unemployment suggests that these aggregate figures
hide important features of the functioning of the labor market, and thus raise doubts as to its capacity to
adjust rapidly to the profound structural changes that are expected to take place. Several issues stand out in
this regard.
2.10. The first has to do with the limited coverage and relevance of the official unemployment numbers.
The open unemployment rate is an average for individuals aged 12 and over in the 16 main urban areas; it
provides no information about unemployment in the rural areas, where the bulk of the poor are located.
Perhaps more important, it is based on a loose definition of employment that counts an individual as
employed if he/she works at least one hour per week. Data from the 1991 National Employment Survey
suggest that approximately 3.7% of employed workers work less than 15 hours a week. Counting these
workers as unemployed would yield an unemployment rate of 6.4%, more than double the official rate.
2.11. A second issue is the low degree of labor force attachment prevalent among many demographic
gtoups. As is common in developed and other developing countries, unemployment spells often end with
temporary withdrawal from the labor force (i.e., the unemployed stop searching for a job).2' For 24% of
unemployed men, unemployment spells end in withdrawal from the labor force. For young men between
the ages of 12 and 20, this percentage is much higher at 37%. For 53% of unemployed women,
unemployment spells end in withdrawal from the labor force; while for young women 12 to 20 years of age,
I/ See G. Wozniak, Human Capitl, Information, and the Early Adoption of New Technology,' Tho JOrnal of Hu
Row       Vol 22, No 1, Winer 1987.

Suuners, -Labor Market Dynamics and Un_yobyme: A  ada     in Brooldnn Pamn on Economc Actvitv 1979:1.



- 6 -
the corresponding proportion is 55 %. A substantial percentage of those who withdraw from the labor force
reenter within 3 months (55% of men and 41 % of women), yet while they are out of the labor force these
"discouraged" workers are not counted as unemployed. Surprisingly, an important group of discouraged
workers is comprised of able-bodied males, particularly among the young and less-educated, who appear to
be idle: these individuals are not working, report that they are not actively seeking a job (i.e., they are out
cf the labor force), but are able to work and are not engaged in other activities such as studying or
housework. If these individuals are counted as unemployed, the aggregate unemployment rate increases
from 2.8% to 5.5% in 1991.
2.12. A third issue that deserves attention is the structure of unemployment and how it affects different
socio-economic groups. An analysis of the determinants of unemployment in Mexico reveals that the
probability of being unemployed decreases with age (up to the age of 45 years) and with schooling.L0' The
bulk of unemployment is comprised of young individuals with relatively low levels of education. About
70% of unemployed males are between the ages of 16 and 30; approximately 20% have less than primary
education and 50% have 6 to 9 years of schooling. As regards the duration of unemployment, the mean
length of a spell of unemployment for men is 5 to 6 months. However, this average hides substantial
variation across individuals. Nearly 65% of unemployed males report spells of unemployment that end
within four months. On the other hand, about 20% report spells that have lasted over a year. In explaining
differences among individuals, age appears to be an important factor contributing to longer duration.
2.13. A fourth, and crucial, issue is the lack of employment stability for a significant fraction of the
population. Although the unemployment rate is low in Mexico, employment turnover is very high.
Individuals appear to suffer numerous, although short, spells of unemployment between jobs.
Approximately 10% of all male workers and 14.5% of female workers suffer at least one spell of
unemployment over the course of a year. These probabilities are much higher for young workers between
the ages of 12 and 20: 25% of males and 34% of females in this age group experience at least one spell of
unemployment during a one-year period. If idle male workers are counted among the unemployed, the
fraction of individuals reporting at least one spell of unemployment over the year climbs to 19% for all
males and 54% for males aged 12 to 20. In the U.S. the corresponding figure for youth aged 17 to 27 is
28 %. As these figures indicate, a substantial proportion of the labor force, and especially the young, has
little employment stability. Given the documented link between turnover and on-the-job training in most
countries, this low degree of employment stability is most likely both cause and consequence of lack of on-
the-job training.
2.14. This brief review of the main characteristics of unemployment suggests several conclusions. First,
although unemployment rates remain moderate compared to some other countries, perhaps because of the
absence of unemployment compensation schemes, they are higher than what official statistics indicate.
Considering as unemployed (or quasi-unemployed) both those with less than 15 hours of work per week and
those who are not actively seeking a job would raise the unemployment rate from 2.8% to about 9%.
Second, the analysis of the structure of unemployment suggests that the nature of the phenomenon varies
greatly by socioeconomic group. Those most at risk of being unemployed are the young and those with
relatively low levels of schooling. However, once unemployed, adult workers experience longer
unemployment duration. This suggests that unemployment programs must be designed to meet the
particular needs of their target groups. Third, the need for labor mobility is likely to increase substantially
10/ Sec A. Revenga ad M. Riboud (1992), *U_ntloyma in Maoo: An Andyms of its Chacterixtic and Dterninanw,'
World Bank mimoo.



- 7 -
in the future as the economy adjusts to NAFTA. The two key issues are: (a) absorbing NAFTA-induced
migration flows of largely uneducated workers from rural towards urban areas; and (b) facilitating the re-
allocation of labor between sectors and regions likely to result from the process of further trade
liberalization resulting from NAFTA. Lowering the costs of job search, improving the job matching
process and retraining labor for the skills that will now be required, are thus key to facilitating the necessary
employment adjustment with minimum social costs and enabling Mexico to take advantage of the anticipated
medium-term growth in employment prospects which NAFTA is expected to generate. These issues were
taken into account in designing the relevant project component through actions aimed at: (i) improving the
coverage and efficiency of the employment services; and (ii) providing training and education opportunities
for displaced workers (paras. 3.23-3.41).
3.    The Absorption Capacity of the Labor Market
2.15. The labor market has shown limited absorption capacity. Although non-agricultural employment
increased at a rate of 1.2% per year during the 1980-90 period, it has not been enough to fully absorb the
flow of new entrants and migrants from rural areas. Migration to the U.S. has provided an increasingly
important escape valve. The average annual flow of legal immigrants into the U.S. in the 1980s increased
by 11 % relative to the previous decade (see Table 2.3 below). The annual flow of undocumented
immigrants is estimated to have increased by 23 % during the same period.
Table 23: Average Annual Immigration Flows to the U.S.

2.16. The problem of absorbing rural labor flows into productive urban employment is likely to become
more acute with adjustment to the NAFTA. The further liberalization of the economy, and especially of
agriculture, combined with ongoing profound structural changes such as the reform of the ejido system, are
likely to result in a large flow of migrants from rural to urban areas. These rural migrants may be poorly
equipped to find jobs in the urban sector. About 20% of the rural population have no formal education at
all, and a further 30% have not completed primary schooling. A program of basic education for adults is
required to provide these workers with the most elementary skills necesary for occupational and
geographical mobility. Moreover, raising the educational level of these workers would complement efforts
to increase labor productivity and contribute to the emergence of new earnings opporunities resulting from
the growth process.

4.    MoEitorin and Policy-malng Capadty
2.17. The monitoring £ J policy-making capacity of labor market agencies and institutions has been
constrained by data limitations and by lack of analysis of labor market issues. Mexico has developed
various information sources and survey instruments that provide labor market information. These sources
include a population cesus, an industrial census, a household-based labor force survey, a household-based
income and expenditures survey, several enterprise-based surveys, and administrative records from social
security registers and from collective bargaining contracts filed through the Secretariat of Labor and Social
Welfare (STPS). Despite this wealth of surveys, however, the information obtained presents a number of
limitations.1 A key problem is that information is dispersed, generated by a number of different
institutions and for different purposes. As a result, data provided by the various sources are often
inconsistent, reflecting different definitions of the same concept, and/or limited or unrepresentative samples.
In addition, surveys are repeatedly overhauled and concepts/definitions changed, creating discontinuities in
the historical data. Another crucial weakness is the limited coverage of most surveys and data sources. For
example, the continuous household labor force survey which provides all key labor market monitoring
statistics (unemployment rate, labor force participation, etc.) is limited to the 16 main urban areas.
Similarly, the main enterprise surveys (the monthly and annual manufacturing surveys) do not cover
MSMEs, which account for the bulk of employment. Other deficiencies of the existing data sources
include: (a) lack of timely data dissemination, there is often a long lag between data collection and
dissemination; (b) limited publication, there is a gap between available information and published
information; and (c) restricted access to the data, not all data are systematically made available to research
and/or academic institutions, who could provide useful insights as to the functioning of the labor market,
nor are they always available to public agencies responsible for labor market programs and policies. These
deficiencies are addressed in the design of the information, policy and institutional strengthening component
of the proposed project (paras. 3.42-3.63).
C.    THE ROLE OF THE PUBLIC SECTOR IN PRODUCTIVITY ENHIANCENMENT AND
LABOR MARETS
2.18.  In order to compete on international markets, foster a more rapid rate of economic growth, and thus
raise the living standards of its people, Mexico needs to increase the productivity of its firms and workers.
Yet, the potential for productivity growth in many sectors of the economy, especially those with a high
concentration of MSMEs (which still represent 71 % of non-farm employment), remains constrained by lack
of investment in the human capital of workers, and by the limited ability of firms to adopt and develop new
technologies. As discussed in paras. 2.3-2.7, the roots of these problems can be traced back to: (a)
disincentives for firms to invest in training arising from the risk of losing the worker, and thus not
recovering the full proceeds of the investment; and (b) the existence of economies of scale in training,
market imperfections in information on training and other services, and the lack of a group (governmental
or private) to organize such training for MSMEs, until the Multiple Support Service Program to MSMEs
(CIMOs) was created in 1987. During periods of rapid ecowmic change, such as those engendered by
NAFTA, labor mobility is essential if Mexico is to take full advantage of the opportunities created. Labor
mobility can be facilitated by retraining programs where workers acquire enhanced or additional skills to
take up new employment and an efficient job placement and matching service which is central to a modern
labor market. Within the context of Mexico's commitment to private sector led growth, in three areas -
IV Tew ooeduionsu ae bhd on a mud, carried out a pat of proje preparalio whichb .ysaeacally reviewed al exidkg
labor maret surveys. See M. Pedro (Anex 9).



productivity enhancement throug training and assistance to MSMEs, labor market adjustment through
training to displaced and unemployed workers, and strengthening labor market policy formulation - there is
a need for a dose partnrship between the Government and the private sector. Taking each of these areas
in turn the role of the Government is as follows.
2.19. Productivity Enhancement. MSMEs tend to under-invest in training from a social point of view due
to a number of important factors. Firstly, workers are mobile, and cannot be bonded to the firm. The
enterprise may not capture the full flow of benefits from its investment - thus there is a disincentive to
invest in training. Secondly, MSMEs are involved in the day to day management of their enterprises and
neither have the skills nor the time to organize training for their employees. Thirdly, there are high
overhead costs in organizing training courses and significant economies of scale: the smaller the number of
trainees the higher the unit cost per trainee. Frequently, smaller MSMEs see one another as competitors
and do not combine forces unless an external facilitator assists. Finally, smaller MSMEs operating at a
lower level of organization and technology are often not even aware of the technological options available to
them nor their training needs. External technical assistance is required. Government is in a position to
equate the rate of return of the enterprise with the social rate of return by providing a subsidy equal to the
externality involved. Through CIMO and its Regional Productivity Support Units (UPCs) the Govenment
is able to broker and facilitate training courses, help to organize MSMEs, disseminate information and
provide technical assistance. Such support must be highly relevant to the needs of the MSMEs, thus strong
involvement of the private sector is essential. The Govenment's role is essentially that of a broker and
facilitator, fostering the growing independence and development of MSMEs and focusing technical support
and the limited training subsidies where it is needed most, in general the smaller MSMEs. MSMEs
currently pay for 56% of the training costs. This percentage will increase gradually to 85% over the project
period.
2.20. By reducing the costs associated with investing in training, and reducing uncertainty and therefore
the costs of adoption and development of new technologies, the Government can raise the return to these
investments and thereby induce their undertaking. Thus the Government role can be reduced over time as
MSMEs grow, become more organized and aware of the returns to such investments in training, and
underke more training directly themseves, with the Government maintaining a technical assistance role.
2.21. Labor Market Adjutment. Increasing the productivity of workers also implies facilitating the
reallocation of labor to its most efficient uses. In an economy undergoing profound structural changes, as is
the case for Mexico, the reallocation of resources between contracting and expanding sectors will entail
significant social costs. In particular, individuals who lose their jobs not only suffer from unemployment
and the accompanying loss in income, but also lose the returns to the specific human capital accumulated in
previous jobs. The costs of adjustment are likely to be largest for low-skill individuals, as demand for labor
shifts towards workers with higher-skill. In order to find new jobs, displaced workers may need to acquire
new skills or upgrade their old ones. This requires an investment in training. Although this investment
may be privately profitable, individuals may be unable to make thee investments for several reasons: (a)
the transaction costs of organizing training can be prohibitively high for an individual; (b) individuals are
unlikely to have adequate information on the skills demanded by the labor market; and (c) unemployed
individuals are likely to have limited access to capital markets, and thus may not be able to finance these
investments. On the other hand, both transaction and information costs of organizing training are much
lower for Govenment, as it can take advantage of economies of scale by organizing course for groups of
workers. This suggests that thern is a role for the public sector in organizing courses that upgrade the skills
of displaced workers, and assisting low income workers financially through providing a small stipend for the
duration of training, thereby facilitating the reallocation of labor. While in theory the cost of the training



- 10-
and the stipend could be recovered from the trainees through a loan scheme, in practice the administrative
and transaction costs would be very high as the amounts are small and it is difficult to keep track of the
trainees after training to ensure that repayments are made.
2.22. The Government's training role is complementary to that of disseminating information on job
availability and characteristics, to ease job search and facilitate the job matching process and thus reduce
these costs for both employees and employers.
2.23.  A public training program for displaced workers can also respond to equity considerations. In the
absence of public intervention in this area, only those individuals with enough resources to finance
investments in training would be able to take advantage of retraining opportunities. In these circumstances,
the burden of adjustment to an increasingly open economy would fall disproportionately on the poorer
segments of the working population, who are also those most likely to be hit by unemployment. By
implementing a public retraining program aimed at low-skill displaced workers, the Government can
facilitate job mobility for disadvantaged workers, and provide them with greater opportunities to increase
their lifetime earnings. Such a program would promote greater social mobility and a better distribution of
the gains from economic reform, and thus contribute to poverty alleviation.
2.24. Policy Formulation and Labor Market Information Systems. Finally, the public sector has an
important role to play in generating and disseminating information on the labor market, and in stimulating
research in this area. Benefits derived from these activities are not likely to be fully accounted for by
private institutions; as a result, the market may fail to produce the optimal amount of information and
research. The Government needs to be equipped to analyze labor market issues, including rigidities and
barriers, so as to formulate and implement policies leading to a more efficient and effective labor market.
D.  STATUS OF EXISTING LABOR MARXET PROGRAMS
1.    Support to Enterpise Tning
2.25. To date, Mexico's efforts to encourage enterprise training have been limited. Other than legislating
mandatory training for workers (with little enforcement of the law), the Government has provided few
incentives to promote employer interest in training or to reduce existing constraints to such training. In
contrast, other countries (such as Colombia, France, Germany, Japan, Korea and Singapore) have
implemented training policies, ranging from levy-grant schemes to encourage enterprise training to
subsidized technical assistance and industrial extension sevices.
2.26. The only significant program existing to date to support in-service training and skill upgrading in
small and medium sized firms is the in-service training program (CIMO) which was introduced, on a pilot
basis, under the Manpower Training project (Loan 2876-ME). The program established 29 small regional
in-service training support units linked to selected employer associations in priority economic sectors and
regions. Over a period of five years, these units worked directly with 18,486 small and medium sized
enterprises, and provided direct support for the organization and delivery of intensive, short-term training
for about 68,900 employees in those enterprises. The program also promoted in-service training through
dissemination of information on the labor market, productivity issues and training opportunities to 22,474
additional enterprises employing about 800,000 workers. The role of the regional support units was strictly
one of broker/facilitator, promoting interest in worker training and facilitating information and access to the
relevant instructors or services, rather than directly providing the services to enterprises themselves. A
large majority of these services (87%) were provided by private centers and/or instructors. A key feature of



- 11 -
the CIMO program is that it is co-financed with the private sector. Although there has not been a
systematic evaluation of the programUl, numerous case studies have been documented, showing reductions
in waste, productivity improvements, employment and profitability increases. Moreover, despite its still-
limited coverage, the program has enjoyed rapidly growing support from small and medium-sized firms, as
evidenced by the growth of their contribution to the financing of the program from an average of 45% in
1988 to 60% in 1991 (see details in Annex 3.3). Enterprises' willingness to participate in and contribute to
the financing of CIMO activities is evidence of the program's relevance and positive impact.
2.27. Few efforts have been undertaken to promote managerial and technological improvements. A
limited number of public institutions, the most significant being the National Science and Technology
Council (CONACYT), are providing technical and/or financial assistance to enterprises to encourage the
introduction of new technologies. These institutions have been constrained by lack of staff and financial
resources, as well as by limited sectoral or geographical coverage. They also have mostly attended the
requests of large firms. However, under the Science and Technology Project (Ln. 3475-ME), CONACYT
and the Bank are cooperating to improve the quality of technology services delivered to industry, and to
strengthen the role of the private sector in the provision of standardization and quality-assurance services.
As part of the development of a system for MSME support, the Government has also undertaken initiatives
to: (i) streamline administrative procedures so as to facilitate the creation and growth of small enterprises;
and (ii) provide financing opportunities to them. The most significant recent development is the increasing
role played by the National Financing Company (NAFIN) since 1988, in providing credit to MSMEs. In
1991, it attended requests from about 50,000 enterprises, four times as much as in 1990. This increasing
trend in provision of credit to MSMEs continued in 1992. However, a consultant studyl5O revealed that
despite these efforts, MSME support in Mexico is hampered by: (a) insufficient numbers of suitably trained
staff; (b) scarcity of informational materials; (c) lack of operative coordination between the different
agencies implementing support; and (d) excessive specialization of technical support. The study also found
that significant progress has been achieved in the areas of training and credit, but emphasizes that coverage
is still very limited (see Annex 3.2).
2.     Employment Programs
2.28. Since 1983, the Government has supported a number of programs aimed at facilitating labor
mobility and labor market adjustment. These programs are run primarily through the National Employment
Service (SNE) and its network of State Employment Services (SEEs), set up in coordination with the State
Governments. Services offered by the SEEs include registration of job seekers and vacancies, career
counseling, referral, and placement in jobs or training (a detailed description of the organization and

structure of the SNE is presented in Annex 4).*1  Programs are funded mainly under the federal budget,
supplemented with contributions from the state budgets (the latter comprise mainly staff salaries for about
one-half of total SNE personnel, and physical facilities). Although the SNE system has been in operation
for nearly 10 years, its capacity to effectively intermediate in the labor market is stil limited. The weakest
part of the program is job placement. The main issues in this regard are: (a) limited coverage of job
seekers and job opporunities; and (b) rudimentary and inefficient placement procedures. The SNE is also
plagued by a number of organizational problems, namely: (a) high SEE staff turnover; and (b) weak
monitoring and evaluation of SEE performance. Two studies conducted by external consultants (see Annex
9) analyzed the functioning of the SEEs and documented these issues. Their conclusions are summarized
below. These issues will be addressed under the Labor Market Adjustment Component of the proposed
project (paras. 3.24-3.32).
2.29.  Limited Coverage of Job Seekers and Vacncie. SEE coverage of local labor market job openings
and job seekers is low. Assessing the actual coverage of the existing SEE information is difficult because
data collected on job seekers and vacancies is limited to stock amounts, no information is collected on
flows. However, the stock numbers suggest that over the 1988-91 period SEEs have captured on average
230,500 job seekers and 228,500 vacancies per year. Given net annual non-agricultural employment growth
for this period of 1% (215,123 net jobs created per year) and an employment turnover rate of 17%,W
vacancies captured each year appear to cover roughly 6% of annual job openings. Similarly, given an
annual labor force growth rate of 1.3% for the 1988-91 period and employment turnover of 17%, job
seekers captured each year represent approximately 5.5% of the total. This coverage is substantially lower
than that observed in most industrialized countries, where the share of vacancies captured by public
employment services varies between 13% (Canada) and 33% (IJK). However, in most of those countries,
employment agencies also administer cash benefit programs for the unemployed, which may pardy account
for their larger coverage.i171 The main reasons for the limited coverage of SEEs in Mexico are: (a) capture
of vacancies is strictly passive, i.e. few SEEs use systematic phone calls and visits to enterprises to gather
vacancy information, but rely instead on the enterprises calling in vacancies; and (b) there is little
coordination and exchange of information with enterprises and other placement agencies operating in the
local market.
2.30. Rudimentarv and Inefficient Placement Procedures. Job placement procedures need to be upgraded
and modernized. Information on job seekers and vacancies is not recorded on a homogenous and systematic
basis-vacancies, for example, are registered exclusively by occupation; no information is recorded on skldls
demanded, wage offered etc. Matching between job seekers and job openings is not automated.
Information is filed solely by chronological order, preventing easy linkae between offers and demands on
the basis of the relevant variables. No mechanisms exist for avoiding double-counting of vacancies. Access
to labor market information on neighboring states is nonexistent or limited at best, as a result, SEEs
effectively function as if they were dealing with segmented markets. Follow-up of placements is limited to

2.31. Hifh SNE Staff Turnover. High turnover rates (15% of SNE staff rotate every year) limit program
effectiveness and continuity. Because of the high rates of turnover, valuable experience, contacts and skills
acquired on-the-job are lost every year and a significant amount of resources must be devoted to the training
of new staff. Turnover seems to vary with the quality of the facilities and the overall work environment. A
main problem is the lack of work incentives. No incentive scheme comprising additional training
opportunities, bonus pay or faster promotion exists to reward outstanding performance. As a result, top
performers with good employment opportunities elsewhere are likely to leave. Differences in pay and
benefits offered to state and federal staff tend to compound employment turnover. Unlike state employees,
federal staff do not enjoy social security coverage. This generates an important disparity in working
conditions between state and federal personnel, which leads to higher turnover among the latter. Finally,
working facilities are often inadequate: many employment offices are cramped, lack partitioned areas to
conduct confidential counseling interviews, and have insufficient phone lines, office equipment, and even
furniture.
2.32. Weak Monitoring and Evaluation of SEE Performance. Monitoring and evaluation of SEEs needs to
be strengthened although federal authorities exercise strong control over SEE operations (including
monitoring state compliance with negotiated agreements, authorizing the hiring of new staff, and approving
training courses prior to implementation) and although they require periodic performance reports from each
SEE. The main problem is that no criteria have been defined for evaluating SEE efficiency. Performance
reports are limited to providing information on numbers of job seekers and vacancies captured, number of
placement referrals (not actual placements) made, and number of trainee placed in courses. No systematic
attempt is made to evaluate each SEE's coverage of local labor market conditions, its efficiency in placing
workers or the success of its training programs. No incentive scheme exists to reward strong-performing
SEEs or motivate weaker performers to improve.
2.33. Retraining Programs for Unemployed and Displaced Workers. The Labor Retraining Program for
unemployed and displaced workers (Programa de Becas de Capacitaddn paw Trabajadores, or
PROBECAT) was established by the Mexican Govermnent in 1984 as a response to the growing economic
crisis. In 1987, the program received Bank support under the Manpower Training project, and its scope
and coverage significantly expanded. Since then, PROBECAT has provided short-term vocational training
to over 250,000 unemployed people. To participate in the PROBECAT program unemployed men and
women register at their local employment office where they are evaluated to determine their eligibility for
the program; based on available information of local enterprises needs for skilled workers, and taking into
account the schooling, experience, and interests of the unemployed, SEE staff refer them to a public or
private institution that offers relevant courses, previously evaluated by SEE staff. Under this program,
unemployed workers receive training of several months duration (on average, three months), during which
they receive a stipend equal to the minimum wage. Upon completion of the courses, the local state
employment office helps training completers find a job (see Annex 3.1 for a more detailed description).
2.34. A recent impact evaluation of PROBECAT found that: (a) trainees with prior work experience
have, on average, a shorter duration of job search than do comparable members of the control group; (b)
program participation appears to improve the likelihood of employment at three and six months after
training for males, and at three, six and twelve months after training for females with prior work
experience; (c) trauining increases the monthly earnings of male trainees, but this effect varies systematically
by level of schooling attinment; the largest effects (increases in monhly earnings of 28% to 37% following



- 14 -
training) are found for those with 6 to 12 years of schooling; (d) program participation appears to have no
effect on the earnings of females; (e) male trainees are more likely to find employment in large firms than
comparable controls; and (f) the monetary benefits of training outweigh the costs of the PROBECAT
program for certain groups of trainees (see Annex 3.1). These results have been taken into account in the
design of the new retraining program under the proposed project. Selection criteria for program
participation have been modified to target those for whom the program was found to be most cost-effective
(para. 3.35). In addition, follow-up evaluations of the program, incorporated in project design, would
attempt to identify the causes of differences in program impact between men and women to redesign the
program to improve the effectiveness of training provided to women.
3.    Labor Market Infonration and Analysis
2.35.  Some of the key deficiencies in the labor market information system were addressed under the
Manpower Training project, which included support for a number of surveys and studies. However, most
of the data generated from these surveys have not yet been thoroughly exploited and analyzed due to lack of
sufficiently qualified human resources. There is a clear need to promote further use of these data, and to
strengthen STPS's analytical capacity. This will increase STPS's understanding of the structure and
evolution of the labor market, and contribute to more effective policy design. This need will be addressed
under the Information, Policy and Institutional Strengthening component of the proposed project (paras.
3.43-3.50).
E.  GOVERNMENT STRATEGY
2.36.  The Education Modernization Program 1989-94, the Industrial Modernization and Ew=  Progrm
1990-94, the Modernization and Deveopment Program for Micro. Small and Medium Sized Industries
1991-94, and the National Training and Productivity Program 1990-1994, all of which expand on the
National Development Plan 1989-1994, reflect the strong commitment of the Government to build up
Mexico's human capital in the context of a consistent free-market oriented strategy, and to provide a clear
institutional framework for human resource development. While the Secretariat of Education (SEP) has
been made responsible for the implementation of education policies and programs, and the Secretariat of
Commerce and Industrial Development (SECOFI) for the implementation of policies related to technological
innovation and export promotion, STPS (by law, the leading institution in matters related to employment,
labor market regulations, in-service training, productivity, and working conditions) is the executing agency
for the National Training and Productivity Program.
2.37. The main focus of the NationalI  g ang     iLix1rogr  is to: (a) support private sector-
led growth by increasing the skills of the labor force, promoting productivity improvements and facilitating
labor mobility; and (b) broaden the basis for growth through a wider distribution of income-generating
opportunities within the population. These goals are made explicit in the Government's policy statement on
labor market and training policies, contained in draft form in Annex 1, which sets forth the general
direction, key objectives and implementation actions of the Government's labor market modernization
strategy.
2.38. As outlined in the draft sector policy statement presented in Annex 1, the Government has placed
high priority on education and training of the work force, and on the adoption of new technologies as a
means of increasing productivity and facilitating the modernization of the economy. The Government has
also emphasized the need to modify traditional forms of organization and management, including promotion
and wage schemes, to reward individual performance and foster productivity growth. The importance



- 1s -
attached to these issues has, furthermore, increased in light of the expected NAFrA agreement. This has
been reflected in the National Arreement for Increased Ouality and Productivity, signed in May 1992 with
representatives of business and labor, which aims to modernize traditional forms of production and
organization. Both the Nao         and Productiv Program and the National Agreement for
Increased Oualiy and Productivity stress the need for an active participation of the private sector in
education, training and productivity-enhancement activities. The draft sector policy statement sets forth
specific targets for private-sector involvement in these areas.
2.39. To accomplish its mandate, STPS would undertake or develop further the following specific actions:
(a) promote and finance programs to improve the knowledge and skills of working aged disadvantaged
population groups to allow them to participate in productive activities; (b) support training and retraining
programs for displaced workers to facilitate their adaptation to new technologies and different production
methods; (c) provide support and technical assistance to MSMEs to promote their productivity and growth;
(d) promote productivity, quality and efficiency through information and promotion campaigns; and (e)
improve information collection, analysis and distribution mechanisms to facilitate and support Government
policy-mak.ing. The proposed project would support and help implement these strategies. At negotiations,
the Government presented a signed statement on sector policies, satisfactory to the Bank, including: (i) the
participation of the private sector and public institutions in the area of job placement and productivity
enhancement activities and the corresponding regulatory framework; and (ii) the objectives and implications
of the recently signed National Agreement for Increased Quality and Productivity. To ensure integration of
project interventions with Government strategies, during negotiations. aIgreem=e was reached on a final
sector olcv statement w%uch includes a broad uew of the Government's labor market strategies for the
project implementation period and beyond and their linkages with the DroDosed Droject (para. 7.2(a)).
F.    BANK EXPERIENCE AND LESSONS LEARNED
2.40. The lessons from experience derive from three Bank operations in the training sector in
Mexico-the First and Second Technical Training projects (CONALEP, I and U), which were completed in
1984 and 1991, respectively, and the Manpower Training project, which is about to be completed and for
which a draft PCR was prepared in June 1992W-as well as from training sector experience Bank-wide.
1.    Taning Sector Experience in Mexico
2.41. The CONALEP I and H projects focused on the creation and strengthening, respectively, of a new
and large training system (the National System for Technical and Vocational Education, or CONALEP121)
through two loans (2042-ME in 1981 and 2559-ME in 1985) totaling US$171.0 million. A third loan for
CONALEP (3358-ME) was approved by the Board in June 1991 to consolidate earlier achievements,
modernize and improve the quality and flexibility of program offerings, and improve its articulation with the
productive, education, and public sectors. Disbursements have begun to accelerate under this Loan. In
October 1987, the Manpower Training project was approved by the Bank. It sought to improve
employment services and provide training to unemployed and displaced workers, promote

industrial/technical training with the involvement of private enterprises, upgrade existing training institutions
through provision of complementary equipment, and strengthen STPS.
2.42.  Lans leri. All three projects accomplished their objectvs, met or surpassed their key
targets, and did not suffer serious implementation problems. Implementation of the Manpower Training
project was consistently ahead of forecasts. The main implementation lessons of these projects are:
(a)    A labor retraining program, such as the one supported under the Manpower Training
project, can be effective in improving the employment and earnings opportunities of
trainees, provided it is properly targeted. The selection criteria for trainees are important
determinants of the impact and cost-effectiveness of the program (para. 3.35 and Annex
3.1);
(b)    To allow continous assessment of the impact and efficiency of training efforts, programs
should have a carefully designed, built-in monitoring and evaluation mechanism, which
should include a control or comparison group. The data collecton strategy, methodology
for the evaluation and periodic review mechanisms should be agreed upon at negotiations
(para. 3.57);
(c)    Support programs should reach out to the enterprises, and involve industrial and business
associations in program activities, to ensure that they respond in quality, output and
relevance to labor market needs. As experience with the Manpower Training project shows,
gradually increasing cost-sharing arrangements can contribute to this (see Annex 3.3);
(d)    Moreover, since experience with the CIMO pilot suggests that trning alone is seldom the
only problem that needs to be addressed, technical support to enterprises should cover a
range of services complementary to training (management, process consulting, industrial
engineering services) (para. 3.9);
(e)    A highly competent and commited project management team that is deeply involved from
the beginning of sector work and project definition is of critical importance (para. 5.3); and
(f)   In order to ensure continuity in the implementation process, short seminars should be
conducted by the Bank whenever there is a change in project management/coordination staff
to familiarize such new staff with Bank norms and procedures. Start-up seminars can also
be very useful when the instution in charge of the project has no previous experience with
Bank procedures.
2.    Lawns Learned from Traini  Setor Ixpe m  Dank-Wide
2.43.  Iuinin  Lessons learned from training sector experience Bank-wide include the following.'
(a)    Economic analysis of labor market issues should be improved for use in the design of
training policies and investments (para. 5.1);
