(b)    Programs should encourage private sector training, which is likely to be the most effective
and efficient way to develop the skills of the work force (pam. 3.22, 3.35-3.36, and 3.41);
(c)   Interventions in the area of public training should seek to improve the responsiveness and
efficiency of such programs. Experience shows that training is effective only if it responds
to labor market needs. The linkage must be direct: employers must be involved in the
definition of training needs and the design of courses; also, instiutional links with industry
are required to enhance the opportunities for staff industrial exposure and research (para.
3.41); and
(d)    Finally, experience indicates that the introduction of cost-recovery schemes is feasible for in-
service training (para. 3.15).
2.44.  Mam        Administration. Project experience Bank-wide confirms that continuity of staff within
project units is crucial to successful project implementation (para. 5.3). Such continuity allows the
development of close cooperation between the Government and the Bank, which helps provide the flexibility
required to effect necessary project changes. Recent experience also suggests that systems for monitoring
and evaluating the intermal and external effectiveness of training can fail because little attendon is paid to
these areas during implementation. Also, staffing constraints in the education/training sector agencies may
have to be addressed in order to avoid relying too heavily on costly technical assistance, which may be used
even for simple administrative and accounting duties.
2.45.  Finaing and Countpart Funig. Successful implementing agencies are those with considerable
financial and administrative independence. In many cases, revenue shortfals or lack of
availability of counterpart funds due to adverse economic conditions cause delays in implementation (paras.
4.5 and 5.12).
3.    Incorportion of Lsons into Project Design
2.46. During project design, the Government and the Bank considered these lessons. First, the proposed
project supports secor policies and strategies that seek to further strengthen the links between enterprise
and public institutions (paru. 3.3-3.12). Formal institutional arrangements are also in place to encourage
private sector training and involve industrial business associations in program activities. Second, successful
cost-sharing arrangements between the Government and enterprises for in-service training of workers will
continue and services to enterprises will be expanded (paras. 3.9 and 3.22). Third, the results of the
evaluation of the labor retraining program supported under the Manpower Training project have been taken
into account to improve the design of the new retraining program. Selection criteria for program
participation have been modified to target those for whom the program was found to be most cost-effective
(para. 3.35). Fourth, the proposed project includes monitoring mechanisms and formal evaluations of the
retraining program and of the MSME support program, based on comparisons with control groups (paras.
3.54-3.56). Fifth, an experienced team that successfully managed the Manpower Training project designed
the project, taking into account their experiences with the first operation and the results of its mid-term
review. Sixth, institutional strengthening activities supported by the project aim to: (a) improve policy-
making capacity by supporting improvements to the existing labor market information sytem, and carrying
out of studies and surveys (par.. 3.42-3.50); (b) provide greater technical support, training and information
to the project implementation units to improve their capacity as service providers; and (c) improve intra and
inter-institutional coordination (paras. 3.13-3.18). Finally, adequacy of countepart funding would be



- 18 -
ensured through the analysis of the work plan and budget allocation for the following year during annual
project implementation reviews (para. 5.12).
G.    RATIONALE FOR BANK INVOLVEMIET
2.47. The project's objectives are fully consistent with the Bank's country assistance strategy to: (a)
support the shift in the Government's focus toward long-run development issues; and (b) provide support to
private sector-led growth through the provision of social infrastructure and a well-structured set of
incentives. By supporting productivity enhancement activities in the labor market, the project would
contribute to human resource development, which is key for long-term growth and poverty alleviation. By
improving the efficiency in the use of public resources and by inducing an increased involvement of the
private sector in training activities, the project would support the Government's efforts to rationalize the role
of the public sector and promote private-sector led growth. By targeting specific actions on more vulnerable
socio-economic groups, the project would also promote social mobility and a more equal distribution of the
gains from economic reform. The project is justified on economic efficiency and equity grounds (paras.
2.18-2.24).
2.48. The Government has been showing increasing interest to involve the Bank more closely in policy
and program development in the human resource sector. Following the good experience of the CONALEP
I, CONALEP 11 and Manpower Training projects (Loans 2042-ME in 1981, 2559-ME in 1985, and 2876-
ME in 1987, respectively), the Bank has been expanding its support to human capital development in
Mexico, through the Basic Health, CONALEP HI, Primary Education, and Science and Technology projects
(Loans 3272-ME in 1991, 3358-ME in 1991, 3407-ME in 1992, and 3475-ME in 1992 respectively), and
through the preparation of an Initial Education Project recently approved by the Board (September 1992).
The Bank has undertaken studies which contributed to the design of the proposed project and these are listed
in para. 5.1 and Annex 9. It is also currendy carrying out a labor market and training study which will
help identify future lending operations in the sector. The Bank is the only aid agency in Mexico involved in
large-scale human resource development programs, and would be able to transfer to Mexico lessons learned
from Bank experience in other countries. The Bank has thus a comparative advantage to provide the
necessary support for the proposed project,
2.49.        In the future, Bank strategy in the fields of education, training and productivity enhancement
will focus on three main areas:
(a)       al and basic education - over the last 4 years, federal budget allocations to education
have increased from 2.9% to 4.2% of GDP, despite this there are still unacceptable
deficiencies in the system in relation to acces, drop-out rates, quality and resource
utilization efficiency. The Bank will support interventions to deal with these issues
particularly in pre-school and primary education. The recently approved Initial Education
Project, the Initial Education Strategy (ESW FY93), the proposed Basic Education 11 (FY94)
and National Teacher Training (FY94) Projects are all key elements in the Bank strategy to
strengthen the foundation of human resource development in Mexico;
(b)   Trinn and roductiv                - in support of the private sector led growth strategy
which Mexico has fully adopted, the skills and frequently the basic education of the labor
force must be enhanced to tWe advantage of the economic opportunities which develop.
The careful implementation of this project, and the current Training and Labor Market
Study, will define any further areas of action requiring Bank intervention. Future projects



- 19 -
in this area are likely to emphasize highly targeted training programs with maximum private
sector participation and the further strengthening of labor market policy formulation but
would not provide any further financing of the costs of trainee stipends; and
(c)    Nutritio - adequate nutrition is both a key determinant of early child development and has a
direct effect on the productivity of the labor force. Nutritional status and the improved
targeting and cost-effectiveness of nutritional programs are expected to be addressed by the
Bank through the Food Security and Nutrition Study (FY93) and the proposed Nutrition
Project (FY96).
HI THE PROJECT
A.    PROJECT OBJECTIVES
3.1.  The objective of the proposed project is to assist the Government of Mexico in promoting private
sector-led growth and in easing the costs of labor mobility and labor market adjustment by: (i) increasing
the productivity and competitiveness of micro, small and medium-sized enterprises; (ii) reducing costs
incurred by employees and employers of hiring and job search, and providing education and training
opportunities for displaced and unemployed workers, with special attention to women; and (iii) improving
the availability, timeliness and dissemination of labor market information and, more broadly, the capacity to
monitor labor market changes and to design, evaluate and implement relevant policies and programs in the
sector.
B.    PROJECT COMPONENTS
3.2.   The project would consist of three major components:
(a)    Productivity Enhancement (28 % of total costs including contingencies) would support
activities aimed at increasing the productivity and competitiveness of MSMEs by: (i)
improving the coverage of the pilot in-service training program (CIMO) initiated under the
Manpower Training Project and expanding the scope of support services provided to
MSMEs; (ii) strengthening coordination between CIMO and other institutions rendering
support to MSMEs; and (iii) promoting the gradual privatization of CIMO by increasing the
involvement of enterprise associations in its financing and operation, and the gradual
transformation of UPCs;
(b)    Labr Mk  Adjustment (61 % of total costs including contingencis) would improve the
coverage and efficiency of State Employment Services (SEEs) and provide education and
training opportunities for displaced workers, through: Ci) strengthening the operation and
management of the SEEs, and improving the quality and availability of information on local
and regional labor markets; (ii) providing improved retraining for approximately 300,000
displaced and unemployed workers meeting stringent selecdon criteria; and (iii) introducing,
on a pilot basis, an education and training program for under-qualified rural migrants who
are ineligible for the regular retraining program; and
(c)   Information Policy and Institudonal Strengthening (11% of total costs including
contingencies) would improve the availability, timeliness and dissemination of labor market



- 20 -
information, and strengthen institutional capacity to design, operate and evaluate labor
market policies and programs, including: (i) supporting the developme  of an information
system and the underking of in-depth surveys to provide a buis for periodic and
systematic labor market data generation and analysis; (ii) promoting an extensive program of
studies and research on labor market issues, programs and policies; (iii) fostering regular
publication and dissemination of labor market information; (iv) instituting a systematic
framework for program evaluation; and (v) strengthening STPS's management and technical
capacity through human resource development and modernization of data management
system.
C.    PROJECT DESCRIPTION
1.    Pructity    anLm oaM  (US$99.1 million including contingencies)
3.3.  Under the Productivity Enhancement Component, the project would support activities aimed at
increasing the productivity and competitiveness of MSMEs, while inducing increased involvement of private
sector enterprise associations in the financing and operation of program activities. The Government's role
would thus evolve to be essentially a provider of technical assistance, with the private sector paying for the
costs of training. These activities would consist in the consolidation and extension of the CIMO program
initiated under the Manpower Training project, which was designed to promote in-service training among
small and medium sized enterprise in selected sectors and regions (see description of the CIMO pilot in
para. 2.26 and Annex 3.2).
3.4. The expanded CIMO program would maintain the key features of the pilot, namely: (a) the support
units delivering CIMO services (UPCs) would play strictly a broker/facilitator role, promoting intert in
worker training and in improved product quality, organization and management, and disseminating
information and facilitating access to the relevant instructors or services, rather than providing the services
themselves; the majority of services are provided by private instructors or centers; (b) assistance would
remain demand-driven; and (c) services would continue to be co-financed with the private sector. The
consolidation and extension of the CIMO program would be achieved through:
(a)    Improvig and Expanding Coveae of Ihe CIMO Propm
3.5.  The project would provide the required resources to extend the geographical and sectoral coverage
of the program and reach new target groups. It is expected that by the end of the project, the total number
of enterprise training and support events organized would reach 100,000 (twice those organized under the
previous project). Approximately 35,000 different enterprises, employing about 1 million workers would be
reached (about two-thirds of enterprises are expected to participate in more than one training/support
activity). The 35,000 enterprise represent about 30% of enterprise employing 10 to 250 workers.
3.6.  Geoaphical Coveroe. Mmhe program currntdy has 29 established regional units (UPCs) with about
three staff per unit. The project would support the establishment of five new UPCs in 1993 and 1994 and
promote improved coverage of enterprises from each of the exist  units by increasing the number of their
staff up to a maxiunm of five or six per unit. The now locations have been chosen either as a response to
an existing demand from enterprise ociatons and/or to reach unateded regions in which strong
economic changes are expected to occur. With 34 opeatng units established within or linked to enterprise
associations, the overal national territory would be practically covered while maintainin a light
administrative structure. Tbe number of promoters would gradually increase by about 20% per year, from



- 21 -
100 during the first year to a maximum of 170 by the end of project life. All promoters would be hired as
fixed-term temporary staff with time-limited contracts and their fees would be financed under the project on
a declining basis (paras. 4.5 and 4.14).
3.7.   Sectora Crg. The program has so far focused its attention on manufacturing and the tourism
sector. Under the new project, it would not only extend its coverage within manufacturing but would also
cover the service sector and agro-industry, both of which have a large fraction of micro and small firms that
are expected to benefit substantially from the program (see Annex 2, Table 4). As the work plan of each
UPC would respond to the demand by private sector enterprises, the relative emphasis put on each of these
sectors would reflect the specific characteristics of the region.
3.8.  ITUmaGroups. While most CIMO interventions in the past were geared towards small- and
medium-sized enterprises (from 15 to 250 workers per firm), the program would now also seek to attend the
needs of micro enterprises (under 15 workers), which represent about 35% of total employment in Mexico.
Promotion and information campaigns would be targeted especially towards smaller enterprises and those
located in urban marginal areas and in the agro-industrial and craft sectors.
3.9. Broadening the Scope of Services. Although originally designed as a program promoting mainly in-
service trainig, the experience with the CIMO pilot (para. 2.26) indicates that the impact of CIMO
interventions can be enhanced by complementing direct training support with actions aimed at improving
product quality, enterprise organization and management. To this end, CIMO would gradually enlarge the
scope of the services it facilitates under the proposed project. In-service training programs would be
expanded to cover the overal occupational structure of the firm, particlarly supervisors (US$49.0 million),
and to allow for training of in-plant instructors (US$4.7 million). CIMO would also facilitate access to
other types of consulting services (US$10.3 million), such as: (a) management or process-consulting
services; (b) industrial engineering; (c) business management, including marketing, accounting, personnel
management, etc.; and (d) industrial, technology and labor market information services. The variety of
services to be provided would respond to local enterprise needs and gradually increase as each individual
UPC gains experience in its respDective region. In all cases, the UPC would play a double role: that of
promoting productivity improvements and that of reducing the costs of organization and access to the
required services. The project would finance the costs of these services on the basis of cost-sharing
arrangements with private sector enterprises (para. 3.22). Over the project's life, CIMO would organize
about 56,000 enterprise training events and about 44,000 interventions involving other types of services (see
Annex 8). After the first two years of project implementation, a first evaluation of the impact of the
different types of CIMO interventions would be made (para. 3.55 and Annex 5.4). Results would be
discussed during the mid-term review and satisfactory adjustments in program design would be made
accordingly (paras. 5.13 and 5.14).
3.10. Ouality Control Laboratories for MSMEs. The project would promote high quality standards
among MSMEs and would thus support, with co-financing from enterprise and enterprise associations, the
creation of five quality control laboratories. Thes laboratories would provide standardization, certification
and quality control services for a fee. Direct cost-recovery of at least 30% of investment costs in the first
two years would be ensured through contracts with the quality control labs for provision of standardization,
certification and quality control services at preferential rates. Project support to the quality control labs
would be fully consistent with the Government's objective of promoting the establishment of a tier of
secondary certification and control labs to help increase quality standards and improve productivity in
industry, as outlined under the ongoing Science and Technology Project (Ln. 3475-ME). CIMO's function
would be to educate MSMEs on the role and advantages of standards and quality assurance and to provide



-22 -
training and incentives to encourae private sector participation in the development and management of
quality control systems. The project would provide minimum financial support to cover part of the design
(US$85,000) and equipment cost (up to a maximum amount of US$100,000 per lab). Selection criteria for
the quality control laboratories were discussed and agreed upon during appraisal. It was agreed that STPS
would invite, through a published nouent, submission of proposals for participation in this program.
Proposals would be selected, by STPS, based on the following criteria: (a) centers benefitting from support
must be located within an area of operation of CIMO, and must be willing to tailor their standardization,
certification and quality assurance services to the specific needs of MSMEs; (b) centers must have suitable
infrastructure and equipment to accommodate the complementary equipment, or must be able to obtain said
infrastructure and equipment through co-financing from private enterprises; (c) centers must present a
satisfactory feasibility study; and (d) centers must agree to offer services to MSMEs at discounted prices, to
allow for cost-recovery of at leut 30% of the investment costs over an initial two-year period. This
percentage is based on experience acquired for start-up activities under the CIMO pilot program. The type
of contract for cost-recovery would be identical to that described in para. 3.15. Dirfn nege&9m.
agreement was reach  on the sekcton criteria for qualty control laboratories and that Droosals for
creation of agalitv control laboratories. Incuifn, feasdbfty stdes. would be sent to the Bank for review
and ogrovrl (para. 7.2(b)).
3.11.  Industrial Imaio System for MSMEs. To strengthen CIMO's productivity-oriented support
services, the project would promote the development of an industrial information system for MSMEs. The
objective would be to provide MSMEs with improved access to local, national and intrnational information
on production technologies, market conditions, human resource management policies, legal and regulatory
requirements, and accounting procedures. The system has been designed on the basis of a pilot project,
conducted by STPS between June 1991 and February 1992, which identified the most pressing information
requirements for MSMEs, the databases available to address these requirements, and the most cost-effective
ways to access those databases.WIV As conceived, the system would operate in a decentralized fashion and
would comprise three levels: (a) the UPC level, which would operate a basic database containing relevant
sectoral and regional information and inormation from all periodic national publications (official bulletins,
business newsletters, busine periodicals, etc.); (b) the central level, which would manage key national
database and information sources, accessible to all UPCs through a communications network; and (c) the
external level, to be used for specialized information requests. MSMEs would have access to these
databases and information sources through the UPC promoters. Coordination of these activities would be
carried out by the Directorate for Employment Studies and Policies (DEPE). The project would finance the
costs of CIMO's specialized information requests and subscriptions to specific databases and publications.
Other costs related to access to national information sources, required equipment and training are included
in the institutional strengthening subcomponent (para. 3.62).
3.12. Economies of Scale. Efforts to reduce transaction costs linked to the organization of training and
management courses and to the access to relevant information would be accompanied, whenever possible,
by a search for economies of sale. Trainin and information costs, in particular, can be substantially
reduced by providing access to a group of firms rather to a single one. TIis is likely to be most significant

under the pilot program showed this to be effective and less costly, in particular when attending the needs of
various firms within the same sector, or when working with a group of common suppliers to a large firm.
(b)    Sgthing Coordination between CIMO and Other Institutions
3.13. The proposed project would strengthen coordination between CIMO and: (a) the training sector; (b)
the set of institutions providing support to MSMEs; (c) the SEEs and workers' associations.
3.14. Training Sector. To improve the availability and quality of training services provided to the firms,
and to ensure that they respond to enterprise needs, the project would finance the training or retraining of
independent instructors or of instructors working in training institutions (US$890,000) that have been active
in modernizing their course offerings (according to a survey conducted with the existing UPCs, 87% of
training centers that have been working with CIMO are private). STPS would invite, through a published
announcement, training centers and/or instructors to present proposals for participation in this program.
The selection, by STPS, of the centers and/or instructors would be based on the following criteria: (a)
centers and/or instructors must have participated or be willing to participate in the organization of training
courses within the CIMO program; (b) they must be actively modernizing their course offerings in response
to local enterprise needs; (c) they must have past experience with, or be in the process of developing,
course offerings in critically demanded specialties (in particular, in the areas of quality control, production
engineering, enterprise organization and management, and accounting/financial control); (d) they must be
wiiling to tailor these offerings to the specific needs of MSMEs; and (e) training centers must have suitable
infrastructure and equipment for course delivery. A modest amount of financial support for complementary
equipment (between US$10,000 and US$100,000) would be provided to 5 training institutions when training
for a critically needed specialty is not available in the area. The equipment would be procured by STPS
using Bank procurement guidelines (para. 4.8) and would be made available to the centers through a
contract whereby STPS retains ownership and the training institution is responsible for the costs of operating
and maintaining the equipment. Trainers would receive specialized courses organized by STPS and
developed and delivered through contracts with private institutions.
3.15. Direct cost-recovery from private training centers that would receive complementary equipment
support would be ensured through contracts for training services to be provided at preferential rates that
allow recovery of at least 30% of equipment investment costs during the initial two-year period. Firms
whose workers receive training would pay for services provided by these centers at reduced rates which
would be reflected in a corresponding reduction of the Government's contribution to the trainees' firms.
For individual independent instructors who receive specialized training, contracts would provide for a cost-
sharing scheme whereby participants would contribute at least 30% of the training cost and STPS would
cover the remainder. During appraisal, the selection criteria for the instructors and training centers were
discussed and agreed upon between the Government and the Bank. Draft agreements for the financing
schemes were also reviewed and agreed upon. The selection of the cost-recovery percentage is based on the
experience of STPS with start-up activities under the CIMO pilot program. At negotiations, the
Government presented satisfactory finalized standard legal contracts for the cost-recovery scheme. During
negotiaions. agreement was reached on the selection criteria for centers and/or lndcendeM instructors who
would =ncfitfrom ClMAO  IwpIr (para. 7.2(c)).

3.17. State Emnloyment Services (SEEs) and Workers' Organizations. Closer links would be established
with the SEEs and with workers' organizations, both of which provide placement services to workers. The
objective would be to improve the information flows regarding job seekers, vacanies and demand for
specific skills in the region and thus facilitate the job matching process. Closer coordination with the SEEs
would also allow better preparation of retraining programs for workers displaced by massive layoffs in areas
undergoing strong economic restructuring.
3.18. Workers' organizations have traditionally run training programs for their affiliates. Some of them,
including the Mexican Confederation of Labor (CTM), have an organized educational strucure which
includes regional units. Most training programs, however, have exclusively dealt with labor law and labor
relations. In order to promote workers' participation in the enhancement of enterprise productivity, the
project would support training services for instructors belonging to workers' associations (US$210,000).
Training of instructors would be co-financed with the workers' organizations. STPS would reimburse 70%
of costs and worker's organizations the remaining 30%. During negotiations, the Government presented

3.19. The CIMO program would be operated through the regional network of UPCs coordinated and
supported by a central unit located within the Directorate for Occupational Analysis (DAO). The project
would strengthen the capacity of the UPC network to increase the coverage and efficiency of the program
by financing: (a) a maximum of 170 UPC promoters (US$19.1 million) (para. 3.6) and 25 fixed-term
temporary saff (US$3.2 million) at the central level; (b) the provision of relevant documentation to UPC
staff (small libraries); (c) the development of an industrial information system (para. 3.11); (d) computer
equipment (para. 3.61); (e) continuous upgrading of skills of UPC staff in all areas in which technical
ssistnce is to be provided to enterprises (pan. 3.59); (f) specialized technical asstance to support

financing and operation of the program. The Government would ultinately maintain only an information
and technical assistance role. The privatization process would be achieved through the gradual
transformation of UPCs into Multiple Service Support Centers (USIs) established within the enterprise
associations and partially supported by them. It is expected that the costs of at least one-fifth of existing
UPC staff would be privately financed by the end of project life (at least one promoter per UPC would be
paid by the enterprise associations). This process would be closely linked to the consolidation and extension
process. UPCs which have already gained regional experience and extended both their coverage and their
services would be privatized more quickly and to a larger extent.
3.21. To promote private sector involvement, the project would provide various types of incentives to
enterprise associations: (a) technical assistance regarding program operation, financing and legal
arrangements; (b) provision of didactic materials; and (c) training of associations' key personnel in areas of
management and organization.
3.22. The privatization process would be accompanied by an overall increasing cost-sharing scheme. For
activities already initiated under the Manpower Training Project (in-service training of workers and
supervisors), the percentage of costs financed by enterprises would increase, on average, from 65% to 85%
by the end of project life. For newly introduced activities, two types of cost-sharing arrangements would be
used: (a) for the training of instructors in private and public centers, the percentage of costs financed by
enterprises would be a flat 30% because these are one-time only events for each participant; and (b) for
technical assistance services to enterprises, the percentage would increase from 30% to 60% by the end of
the project, to ensure that enterprises that receive various types of services time absorb a greater share of
the cost of these services over time. Overall, the percentage of costs financed by enterprises would increase
from 56% in 1993, to 64% in 1995, to 66% in 1996, to finally reach 77% by the end of project life.
Enterprises' total financial contribution to program operation costs is estimated to grow from about US$5.0
million during the first year of the project to US$12.0 million by 1997. This estimate of total private sector
contribution does not include the value of time of program participants (see Annex 3.2). The Government's
financial contribution for CIMO services would remain flat at about US$4.0 million throughout the life of
the project. The contribution of the private sector would be ensured through the payment mechanism, under
which the private party pays for 100% of the costs of services obtained, and then gets reimbursed by STPS
for the agreed percentage. At appraisal, agreements were reached on cost sharing and cost-recovery
arrangements with the private sector and on specific, time-bound targets for private sector participation to be
monitored during project implementation (see Annexes 1 and 8). These targets are included in the
monitoring indicators, and were confirmed at negotiations (para. 5.10).
2.    Labor Market Adjustnent Component (US$218.1 million including contingencies)
3.23. Under the Labor Market Adjustment component the project would provide the resources required to
improve the coverage and efficiency of employment services and provide education and training
opportunities for displaced ankd unemployed workers.
(a)    S gthening Operation and Manage t of Employment Services
(US$41.4 million including contingencies)
3.24. The project would improve the functioning of the SNE and its network of SEEs by: (i)
strengthening the operation and management of the SNE; and (ii) increasing the quality and coverage of
information on local and regional labor markets. Overall responsibility for this component would rest with



- 26 -
the Directorate for Employment Services (DSNE). At the local level, operational responsibility would be
held by the SEEs, with support from the DSNE.
3.25. Strengthenig Q ational Procedures. The project would upgrade procedures for job placement
and training referral and improve the recording and use of information on job seekers and vacancies.
Current operational procedures would be revised and reformulated, with technical assistance support
(US$1.0 million), and new operational manuals developed (US$80,000) and distributed to staff
(US$570,000) (currently numbering 851) in all 32 SEEs and their operating units during the first year of the
project (see Annex 4 for a breakdown of units and staff per SEE). Information recorded by the SEEs would
be systematized and computerized to allow easy linkage between job offers and job demands on a number of
variables (e.g., occupation, qualifications demanded/offered, sector). The project would also augment and
strengthen contacts with local firms through the formation of local committees with employer representation
and through intensified personal visits to firms. By supporting closer coordination with employers, the
project would improve coverage of job opportunities, assure a better match between training courses offered
and skills required, improve follow-up of employment referrals, and ultimately contribute to better job
placement with fixed-term staff support (US$7.8 million). To expand the geographical coverage of the
employment service network, the project would provide 24 'mobile" employment service units to the main
SEEs. The design and operation of the mobile units would be based on the experience of the six pilot units
supported under the Manpower Training project. Support for these units was conditioned on the preparation
of an evaluation of the experience, expected to be completed and sent to the Bank by the end of 1993.
Dunng negotiations, agreement was reached that the Government woudd submit to the Bantk the revsed
operational manuals for SEEs by June 30. 1993 (para. 7.2(e)). As a condition for disbursement for the 24
mobilen mlovment unwts. the Government would present an evaluation. satisfactorv to the Bank. of the

3.26. To ensure effective implementation of these actions and to eliminate constraints to the operation of
SEEs arising from inadequate working facilities, the project would support the provision of essential
computing and office equipment, the installation of additional telephone lines (three per SEE), the repair
and/or redesign of existing office space, and the modernization of existing information technology (para.
3.61). The goal would be to: (a) allow each SEE to computerize its basic program operations; (b) provide
the basis for the systematic recording of information on job seekers, vacancies and training needs at the
local level; and (c) provide each SEE with fast and easy access to local, regional and national information
relevant to its operations. Implementation of new operational procedures will also require substantial
training and upgrading of personnel at the state and central levels. This would be provided under the
project as part of a comprehensive training program for STPS staff (para. 3.59).
3.27. Monitoring and Assessment of SEE Performance. The capacity for continuous system-wide
monitoring is essential in assuring the quality and effectiveness of services delivered through the
employment centers. The project would improve DSNE's existing monitoring capacity, with the help of
consultants and fixed-term staff (US$2.2 million), by establishing a set of homogenous and systematic
efficiency criteria to measure SEE performance. The new criteria would emphasize placements as the
appropriate variable to measure SEE efficiency. The efficiency criteria were discussed and agreed upon
during appraisal (see Annex 5.3). The most important among them have been incorporated into the set of
key monitoring targets for project implementation (para. 5.10 and Annex 8).
3.28. Provision of Work Incentives to Starf. The project would introduce a performance-based bonus
compensation system for employees. The objective is to reduce the high turnover of staff in the SEE



- 27 -
network. The performance-based compensation scheme would aim to link individual compensation to
performance of that individual's operating unit and/or SEE. SEE and/or unit performance would be
assessed based on the set of efficiency indicators referred to in para. 3.27 and found in Annex 8. The
evaluation of SEE performance would be carried out by an Evaluation Committee comprising the Director
General of Employment, the Director and three Sub-Directors of the SNE, and three SEE Directors chosen
at random. The top 30% of the operating units would receive a compensation bonus equal to one to two
months of salaries, to be distributed among their employees. Costs generated by the staff incentive system,
which are part of recurrent costs, would be financed on a declining basis (para. 4.5). During negoiations.
the Government provided assurances that the precse guidelines for the ogeration of the work incentve

March 31. 1993. and that the Evaluation Committee would be established bv June 30. 1993 (para. 7. 1(b)).

3.29. The incentive program would be managed by STPS, in coordination with the State Governments.
The coordination structure already exists in the form of annual agreements between STPS and the State
Governments, which determine the financial contributions of each, as well as the norms and legal
framework for the year's activities.P1 Under the agreements for the current year, which were reviewed
during appraisal and found satisfactory, the contribution of the State Governments covers the salaries of
approximately one-half of the employees of the SEEs, the costs of office space and equipment, and all
associated maintenance and operating costs. In future annual agreements, STPS would also negotiate the
gradual incorporation of all SNE federal staff into the social security regime, with charge to the State
Governments. Targets for the incorporation of SNE federal staff into the social security regime were
discussed and agreed upon during appraisal, and would be used as monitoring indicators for project
implementation (see Annex 8). Duning negotiations, the Government provided assurances that the annual
agreements with the State Governments for subseauent years would be revewed with the Bank during the
annual reviews. so as to verify the adeauaca of State financial contributions for the operation of the SEEs
(para. 7.1(c)).
3.30. Reorganization of Central Level Unit. The project would strengthen the management capacity of the
SNE by financing technical assistance to support the coordination, monitoring and evaluation activities of
the central level unit. Functions and responsibilities of the central units and the need for technical assistance
were discussed and agreed upon during appraisal.
3.31. Improving the Information on Local and Regional Labor Markets. The project would improve the
quality and coverage of information on local labor markets through: (a) coordination and exchange of
information with private placement agencies, business representatives and CIMO; (b) elaboration of periodic
regional studies on occupational needs; (c) improved data collection and processing at the operating unit
level, leading to better use of information recorded by the SEEs themselves; (d) improved communication
between SEEs and the central level, allowing for increased access to regional and national labor market
information collected at the central level (paras. 3.43-3.46); and (e) a more decentralized use of the
information, allowing for greater dissemination to local employers, training institutions, and other placement

3.32. By promoting better information on local and regional labor market conditions, these actions would
directly contribute to improved operation of the SEEs, better job placement, improved training referral and
course design, and more accurate career counseling. Parallel activities aimed at strengthening operational
procedures and organization (paras. 3.24-3.30) would, in turn, foster better use of available information.
Responsibility for coordinating the regional information system and for updating and developing the central
database would rest with the DEPE. Operational responsibility for collecting and managing local and
regional information would rest with the SNE.
(b)   Training and Education Programs for Unemployed and Displaced Workers
(US$176.7 million including contingencies)
3.33. The project would support an extensive training program aimed at upgrading the skills of
unemployed and displaced workers. The main objective is to provide training for those workers whose
skills fail to match those in demand and who otherwise would be unable to qualify for existing vacancies.
Based on the evaluation results of the PROBECAT program supported under the Manpower Training project
(see Annex 3.1), the project would continue to support existing STPS retraining programs for displaced
workers who meet improved selection criteria (paras. 3.35-3.36 and Annex S. 1). The project would also
introduce, on a pilot basis, an alternative basic education and training program for under-qualified rural
migrants, who are not eligible for regular retraining. Over the five years of the project, approximately
300,000 persons would be trained. Overall management responsibility for retraining programs would rest
with the DSNE. At the local level, operational responsibility would be held by the SEEs.
3.34. The project would support two alternative training delivery systems: (a) classroom training,
implemented in accordance with existing procedures for STPS retraining under contracts with over 400
public and private training institutions; and (b) in-plant training, implemented jointly with enterprises which
contact the SEEs to hire new workers and manifest interest in participating. In the case of classroom
training, the project would provide financing for trainee stipends and training costs (training materials and
instructor salaries). In the case of in-plant training, the project would provide financing only for trainee
stipends. All other training costs would be covered by the enterprise. Classroom trainees would receive a
small supplement to the regular stipend, calculated on the basis of a survey of transport costs in 10 urban
areas. In-plant trainees would receive similar assistance directly from the training enterprise. Although
enterprises would not be obliged to hire all training completers, in the past the majority of in-plant trainees
have been hired. Approximately 80% of all trainees would receive classroom training, while the remaining
20% would be trained through in-plant programs.
3.35. Retraining for Displaced Workers (classroom training). The project would support continuation of
the retraining program along the PROBECAT model for those types of workers for whom it has proved
effective, namely unemployed workers with prior work experience and who have either completed primary
or some secondary education. The selection criteria would be based on these two main characteristics. The
age bracket for eligible trainees would be in the 18-55 range, although the condition of prior work
experience would restrict significantly the number of eligible young workers aged 18 to 24. In addition,
trainees have to be registered as a job applicant at a local SEE and have either complete primary or some
secondary education. All trainees without prior work experience would be eligible only for the in-plant
training modality (para. 3.36). These selection criteria for program participation (detailed in Annex 5.1)
have been designed, based on the results from the evaluation of the PROBECAT program, to target those



- 29 -
individuals for whom training has proven most cost-effective, namely, trainees with prior work experience
(para. 2.34). Particular attention will be paid to addressing the issue as to why the benefits of training for
women is apparently low. Possible explanations include inappropriate courses for females and other
rigidities and barriers in the labor market. This issue will be fully investigated in the evaluation (para.
3.37) and an action plan, including measures to remove rigidities and barriers to female employment,
developed to deal with it (Annexes 1 and 5.4). Participants in the program would receive short-term
training in tailor-made courses in specialties required by enterprises in the respective region, varying in
length from one to six months, with an average of three months, during which time they would receive a
stipend equal to the regional minimum wage. The rationale for the stipends is to cover part of the
opportunity cost of attending the course (which is an investment cost) to allow individuals with minimum
resources and greater family responsibilities to participate in the program. This rationale is identical to that
used to justify financial assistance for any educational investment. The level of the stipend is very low
relative to the average wage of Mexican workers; as much as 93 % of Mexican workers report earning more
than the minimum wage. According to the National Employment, Salary, Technology and Training Survey
conducted in December, 1991, the average salary for production workers in the lowest salary scale was
35% higher than the PROBECAT stipend, including the transport supplement and the average salary of
administrative workers in the lowest salary scale was 52% higher. Thus, the stipend is only intended to
cover part of the opportunity cost of program participation and does not constitute an incentive for workers
to reject viable job opportunities in favor of attending training. Cost-recovery for this program would have
very high transaction costs that would undermine the economic benefits of the program (para. 2.21).
3.36. Training for Young Inexperienced Workers (in-plant training). This program would be targeted to
young workers with no work experience, for whom classroom training along the PROBECAT model has not
proven to be effective (see Annex 3.1). The program would have the dual-purpose of providing skills to the
most disadvantaged youth and unemployed and giving them the opportunity to obtain the necessary work
experience to become active labor force participants. Course content is determined by the enterprise needs
for specific skills and includes a combination of classroom and on-the-job training. Course duration varies
between two and six months with the average duration being three months. Under this program, enterprises
cover the costs of instructors and materials, provide the necessary physical training facilities, and usually
cover the trainees' transportation and meal costs while in training. The Government provides a stipend
equal to one regional minimum wage to each participant. Cost-recovery options would not be feasible due
to the high transaction costs involved (para. 3.35 and Annex 5.2).
3.37. The project would support the development of a systematic framework for data collection and
evaluation (paras. 3.54-3.57 and Annex 5.3). Several evaluations of all of PROBECAT's training
modalities would be conducted over the project life to measure the impact of alternative training programs
and delivery systems on the employment and earnings of program participants and to assess the cost
effectiveness of the programs. The first evaluation would take place in the second and third quarters of
1994, because sufficient time is required to observe trainee cohorts, and its results would be made available
to the Bank by March 31, 1995. Bank financial support beyond this date would be conditional upon the
satisfactory results of this evaluation and agreement on a plan of corrective reform, if called for. Durng
negadions. aireement was reached on the terms of referce and timinp of the PROBECAT evaluations

3.38. Basic Education and Trainia. As discussed in para. 2.16, Mexico is undergoing important
structural changes; the reform of the traditional ejido and the NAFTA agreement are foremost among them.
Both are likely to result in a large flow of rural migrants to urban areas. To facilitate channelling some of
the expected flow of rural migrants into productive employment in urban areas, the project would support,
on a pilot basis, the introduction of a Basic Education and Training program for individuals with little or no
formal education who are not eligible for participation in the retraining program.
3.39. The design and implementation of this pilot program would be done jointly between STPS and SEP
through its National Institute for Adult Education (INEA). INEA would provide the format for the basic
education courses, training materials and equipment. It would also support STPS in the selection and
training of instructors, and in the monitoring and evaluation of the program. STPS would cover the costs of
program operation and be principally responsible for the selection of trainees, the design and organization of
the skills training component, and the monitoring and evaluation of the program. Participants would enroll
in courses lasting approximately six months, which would combine training modules in basic literacy with
additional skill/occupational training. While enrolled in the programn, trainees would receive a stipend equal
to the regional minimum wage for the same reasons as stated in para. 3.35. Selection criteria for the pilot
program participation were discussed and agreed upon during appraisal (see Annex 5.1).
3.40. Implementation of the program would be gradual, with focus in the first five months of the project
on organization and design of program contents and operational procedures. Following this, a pilot
program would be implemented in three urban areas during June-December, 1993. Evaluation would take
place in September of the following year; during this period the program would continue with its current
level of trainees and would not expand. Based on evaluation results, the pilot program could be expanded
into a regular training modality within the STPS Labor Retraining Program. As for the other training
modalities, the project would support the development of an extensive and systematic program evaluation
framework. During negotiations. the Government rovided assurances that: (i) theDrecise guidelinesfor
the operation of the pilot Basic Education and Training program. including contents of the pro gram courses
and detailed operational Drocedures. would be gresented to the Bank by May 31. 1993 for review and
aq=roval: rii) the results of the evaluation Qf the pilot in line with terms of referce accptabek to the Bank
would be submitted to the Bank for review no later than March 31. 1994 and that based on those results.
atreement would be reached on the continuation and Dossib&e redesign of the groram. fii) a satisfactory
agreement between STPS and INE1 for the cDansion of the Basic Education and Training Propram beyond

3.41. Training Institutions. To ensure the quality and relevance of the training offered under the program,
the project would support a number of actions aimed at modernizing and upgrading existing training
institutions. First, as discussed in paru. 3.25 and 3.31, the project would promote greater coordination
between the different agents involved in the local labor market-including employers, job seekers,
placement agencies and training institutions-and would seek to improve the availability and dissemination
of information on local labor marcet needs and conditions. This would help training institutions adapt their
offer to rapidly changing training requirements. Second, over the five years, the project would provide a



- 31 -
modest amount of complementary training equipment such as electronic boards, sets of wood and metal
working tools. This equipment would help improve the quality of the training provided to MSMEs and to
PROBECAT trainees. An average of US$23,000 per program for about 300 technical training programs
would be made available to private and public training institutions involved in providing retraining and in-
service courses. Eligibility for equipment support would be open to all public and private training
institutions who have participated in the Labor Retraining and CIMO programs in the past or desire to do so
in the future (with the exception of CONALEP participants, which benefit from support under Ln. 3358-
ME). Following a general announcement requesting proposals, beneficiary training institutions would be
selected on the basis of agreed criteria, which include presentation of institutional plans for strengthening the
offer of courses in critically demanded specialties, visits to evaluate the condition of existing equipment, and
availability of suitably qualified instructors. The equipment would be procured by STPS and would be
made available to the centers through a contract whereby STPS retains ownership and the training institution
is responsible for the costs of operating and maintaining the equipment. The impact and use of equipment
provided under the project would be monitored and evaluated annually by STPS. A portion of the
investment costs would be directly recovered from private training centers through contracts for training
services at preferential rates. Agreements for cost-recovery schemes would follow those outlined for CIMO
(para. 3.15).
3.    Information, Policy and Institutional StrengtheinLg Component
(US$38.7 million including contingencies)
3.42. Under the Information, Policy and Institutional Strengthening component, the project would support
a set of activities aimed at improving the availability, timeliness and dissemination of labor market
information and at siengthening the institutional capacity to use this information to better design, operate,
monitor and evaluat, libor Siarket policies and programs.
(a) * Laber Market Information and Program Evaluation
' (US$A1.8 million including contingencies)
3.43.  Informationa vem . ,he project would support the development of an information system that
would allow for sys*matic analysis of the labor market and would help design, monitor and evaluate labor
market policies and programs. The project would finance technical assistance (US$800,000), survey design,
data collection and processing, and analysis. The information system would cover data on employment,
wages and non-wage benefits, education, training and technology, as well as data on collective agreements
and other aspects of labor market regulations. It would be made accessible to policy makers and other
agencies and institutions involved in labor market issues.
3.44. The developed information system would be based on: (a) the review, gathering and integration of
all available information from various sources (surveys, administrative registers on insured workers and
existing databases); (b) the promotion, through agreement with the main information generating institution,
the National Institute of Statistics (INEGI) of increased coverage and representativity of existing surveys,
improvements in questionnaires and data quality, greater use of and greater access to existing information;
(c) the regular and timely production of short-term statistics to allow for the monitoring, on a quarterly
basis, of changes in employment, labor costs and productivity; (d) the definition of a basic set of statistics to
reflect medium and long-term structural changes; and (e) an extensive program of specific surveys and
studies (paras. 3.47-3.50).



- 32 -
3.45. These tasks would be carried out by the staff of the General Coordination Unit for Labor Market
Policies, Studies and Statistics (CGPEET), with support from a technical committee, formed in June, 1992,
chaired by the General Coordinator of CGPEET and comprised of representatives from all technical areas of
STPS, including the General Directorate for Employment (DGE) (paras. 5.3-5.7). This committee, with the
assistance of working groups and external consultants, would also be responsible for setting priority needs
and coordinating the implementation activities to be carried out by the various technical areas. Durin
negotiations. the Government rovWied assurances that: (f) the set of short. medium and long-term statistics
to be used for labor market monitoring would be defined and submitted to the Bank for review no later than
ADril 30. 1993: and rii) once ddfied. these statistics would be updated and Dublished on a regular basis
beginnwng in June 1993 (paras. 3.53 and 7.1(e)).
3.46. The information system would also encompass the industrial information system and the regional
labor market information system, which directly support the operation of the CIMO program and the
functioning of the SEEs (paras. 3.11 and 3.31-3.32). Both the industrial and the regional labor market
information systems would be under the responsibDlity of DEPE in coordination with the DSNE and DAO.
3.47. Surveys. To allow for a deeper analysis of particular labor market issues, specific surveys would be
undertaken. The design of these surveys would be carried out by the CGPEET with the assistance of
external consultants (US$5.7 million). Data collection would be contracted out to INEGI and other research
centers. The most important surveys would be: (a) a national employment survey with a specific module
on training activities; (b) an enterprise survey with an attached worker questionnaire; (c) a retrospective
survey of individuals that would set the basis for creation of a longitudinal data set; (d) a migration survey;
and (e) a survey of micro-enterprises. These surveys, to be conducted every two years, in accordance with
a detailed implementation schedule (Annex 8, Schedule 1) to be agreed upon at negotiations, would expand
the coverage of existing INEGI surveys and would fill important information gaps (para. 2.17). Terms of
reference for these surveys were discussed and agreed upon during appraisal (see Annex 5.7). Dund
negotiations. agreement was reached on terms of rcference and Iementato schedule for the survevs and
on the definition of the content and purpose of additional specc survevs would be discussed during the
nnual rviews (para. 7.2(g)). During negotiations. the Government vrovaded assurances that. ri) all
survev questionnaires would be sent to the Bank for review: and di) basic survn results would be publgshed
and the nucro-data be made avilable to external research institutes, academic institutions and the Bank
within a year of data collection. so as to stimulate timch research on labor issues (para. 7. 1(f)).
3.48. Studies. The increased availability of raw data on the labor market would be accompanied by an
extensive study and research program. Two types of studies would be undertaken: (a) short "diagnosis"
studies which would be carried out on a regular basis and would consist of a simple description of the most
important characteristics of the labor market (active population, skills of the labor force, wage levels, etc.);
and (b) studies and research aimed at providing a deeper analysis of the functioning of the market,
identifying its determinants and predicting the impact of behavioral and institutional changes. These studies,
which would be carried out either by qualified STPS staff and/or fixed-term temporary staff (US$2.8
million), would largely be based on the analysis of existing and newly created labor market data,
systematically gathered or made accossible through the development of the information system and the
surveys program (paras. 3.43-3.47). Tbey would serve to improve STPS's understanding of changing labor
market conditions, provide direct feedback into policy decisions, and allow for continuous monitoring and
evaluation of programs supported under the project.
3.49. The list of the main studies to be financed under the project was discussed and agreed upon during
appraisal (Annex 8). Studies wo'lld be carried out in the following areas: (a) the analysis of the



- 33 -
determinants of employment, wages and productivity; Q,) the relationship between openness, technological
change and growth; (c) the structure and dynamics of labor demand and supply; (d) the informal sector; and
(e) impact of the regulatory framework on the functioning of the labor market. The study program would
also include a number of studies designed to directly support the other project components, such as the
evaluation of education and training programs for the unemployed (para. 3.56) and the evaluation of the
impact of the CIMO program (para. 3.55), as well as regional studies targeted towards specific areas or
sectors. Finally, the study program would include an in-depth mid-term review to assess project
performance and initial impact (para. 5.13). Results and conclusions of these studies would be discussed
with the Bank during the supervision missions and annual implementation reviews. For those closely linked
to the programs supported under the project (CIMO and retraining), the implementation of recommendations
satisfactory to the bank, would also be discussed and agreed upon during the annual reviews (para. 5.12).
Detailed terms of reference for first year studies, implementation schedules (see Annex 8) and consultant
selection processes for the studies to be carried out during the first year of the project were discussed and
agreed upon at appraisal (see Annex 5.8). In particular, it was agreed that the consultant selection process
would involve a general announcement in academic and research institutions requesting proposals in
accordance with the Terms of Reference for the studies. During ncgotiations, agreement ws reached on
the terms of rference. schedules and consultant selection processes for the first-year studies and that the
terms of referenac. reiew and implementation schedules for the studies to be carried out during each
subsequent Year would be discussed and aged Wonwith the Bank ex-ane during the annual
LniMOelntation reWews (para. 7.2(h)).
3.50. Responsibility for the design and management of most of the survey and study programs would also
rest with the CGPEET, with support from the technical committee and relevant technical units of STPS, and
with technical assistance and fixed-term staff support (US$5.7 million). The evaluation of the education and
training programs for the unemployed and of the CIMO program, as well as specific sector regional studies
would be carried out by DEPE.
3.51. Documentation Center and Publications Unit. The project would support the strengthening and
extension of the Information Center on Labor Issues (SIAL), created in 1983 under CGPEET and directly
managed by its General Directorate for Institutional Programs and Documentation (SPID). The proper
functioning of this center, which is both a library and a Publications Unit, has been impaired by information
gaps and lack of resources. This has led to a wide dispersion of data between different institutions and
great difficulties in timely access to them. Strengthening this center would thus have as main objectives the
consolidation and centralization of information flows and the provision of easy access to up-to-date labor
market information to all technical areas of the STPS as well as to other institutions involved in the sector.
3.52. The development of this center would be achieved through: (a) the constitution of catalogues and a
unified access system; (b) the rationalization and computerization of information-gathering procedures; (c)
the creation of a network system to gain access to existing databases in Mexico and abroad; (d) the
extension of the existing stock of documentation through subscriptions, acquisitions, and transfers/loans; and
(e) the production of various types of publications. These actions have been defined on the basis of a 1990
inventory of the existing stock of library volumes (around 100,000) and of a June 1992 diagnosis study
which reviewed the functioning of the editorial programs (see Annex 9).
3.53. Publications to be financed under the project would include various periodic statistical bulletins, a
journal published three times a year and occasional monographs. The statistical bulletins would aim at
ensuring the dissemination of the short-, medium- and long-term statistics generated through the information
system (paras. 3.43-3.45). The journal and monographs would serve to disseminate the results of studies



- 34 -
carried out by STPS and external researchers. The content of each issue would be decided by an editorial
board comprising the members of the technical committee and at least two outside experts in the labor field.
Services from external referees would be requested as needed. Publications would be distributed to the
different areas within the sector, to research centers and to university libraries, and would be available for
sale in specialized bookstores.
3.54. Program Evaluation. Continuous monitoring and evaluation of programs is essential to assure
effective and efficient use of public resources. The project would improve STPS's evaluation capacity by
instituting a systematic framework for data collection and evaluation of all programs supported under the
project. Responsibility for the evaluation would rest with the Directorate of Employment Studies and
Policies (DEPE), in coordination with the directorates responsible for program implementation.
3.55. CIMO Program. A first objective of the evaluation would be to monitor the implementation of
CIMO interventions on a continuous system-wide basis, so as to assure the quality, timeliness and
effectiveness of CIMO services. A second objective would be to measure the impact of CIMO interventions
on the performance and productivity of enterprises, and to assess the efficacy and overall cost-effectiveness
of the program and of its different support modalities. The evaluation would be based on periodic
operational reviews, detailed case studies and time-series data systematically collected from participating
enterprises.
3.56. Retraining and Basic Education and Training Programs. The main objectives of the evaluation
would be to assess the impact of program participation on employment and incomes. This would be
complemented with a cost-effectiveness analysis of the overall program and of each training modality
(including the Basic Education and Training program for under-qualified rural migrants). These evaluations
would be based on longitudinal data gathered on several trainee cohorts. Control groups would be drawn
for the corresponding periods from the quarterly National Urban Employment Survey (ENEU) by exploiting
the panel structure of that survey.
3.57. For all program evaluations (para. 3.37), the project would finance data collection and analysis and
technical assistance (US$1.7 million). Terms of reference for the evaluation systems and methodology,
including implementation schedules, were discussed and agreed upon during appraisal (see Annexes 5.3 to
5.5, and Annex 8). The first set of evaluation results for the retraining program would be made available to
the Bank by March 31, 1995 and would condition Bank's further financial support for the program (para.
3.37). The first set of evaluation results for the CIMO program would be ready by the project's mid-term
review and would be used to adjust program design, if necessary. Later evaluations results would be
discussed with the Bank during annual reviews to allow for further program improvements. Dunea
n=e=tiadons. areement was reached on the terms of reference and completon dates of the evaluations of
the CaMO and retraing grograms and that the Government would: riJ submit to the Bank the results of
these evaluations on the rcfied dates for review: ai) discuss their implications for program desgn. and
(fiiJ develop a satiswiactor action Dan. as ma be needed. to incorporate the reqred changes (para.

the Bank, that would guarantee STPS access to INEGI's labor market micro-data, which is essential for
program evaluation and labor market monitoring.

(b)   Institutional St
(US$16.9 million including contingencies)
3.58. This subcomponent would strengthen decision-making processes by improving management and
technical capacity at the central and local levels and modernizing equipment and working methods. It would
also strengthen STPS capacity to implement project activities by financing consultants (US$1.3 million),
fixed-term staff (US$2.8 million), and other expenditures related to project coordination and management.
Activities are grouped under three major areas: (a) human resource development; (b) modernization of the
gathering, processing, and transferring of data; and (c) project coordination and management.
3.59.  Human Resource Developmn. STPS' main strategies for human resource development consist of:
(a) training of selected staff as trainers of new staff and at the central and local levels; (b) continuous
development and updating of training materials for in-house training; and (c) involvement of local and
foreign universities and other institutions, in the development and delivery of specialized training for
managerial, technical and support staff and fixed-term temporary staff. Areas for knowledge and skill
improvement of staff are determined through STPS-wide needs analyses, which are revised and updated
annually. In line with STPS' long-term human resource development strategies, activities supported by the
project include: (a) technical assistance for needs analyses and course development; (b) in-service training
for staff involved in the administration and management of SEE and CIMO support programs; (c) training
for trainers; (d) long-term continuing education programs for upgrading skills at the central level, with
involvement of universities and other specialized institutions, in particular the Bureau of Labor Statistics;
and (e) short-term study tours and scholarships for STPS managerial and technical staff.
3.60. The proposed project would finance: (a) training of 851 staff of the SEEs and 170 internal of the
UPCs, including short-term study tours; technical training for about 155 managerial and technical STPS
staff; training in the use of software; general administration, accounting and statistics courses for about 300
STPS staff; and short-term scholarships for 28 STPS staff; (b) technical assistance for course development at
the central and local levels; (c) audiovisual equipment for selected SEEs and for the General Directorate of
Human Resources; and (d) reproduction or purchase of training materials. STPS has prepared a detailed
training program, including course objectives, duration, instructional techniques, and training providers for
the five years of project implementation. Each year, training plans for the following year of implementation
would be updated and reviewed during the annual reviews (para. 5.12). RmdagzgQda&nL agreement
wLs reached that the Government would submit during the annual reviews, soilsfactord updated training
Dlans. revised on the basis of the annual needs analsis. for the foUowing year of rogram ifmpementation
(para. 7.2()).
3.61. Modernization of the Gathering. Processing and Transferring of Data. To facilitate and modernize
data management at the central and local levels, to integrate information from various technical units of
STPS and to avoid duplication of efforts within the institution, the proposed project would support the
consolidation of the labor market information system (paras. 3.43-3.45). Actions would comprise: (a)
provision and installation of the required computer and communications hardware and software needed to
link the technical units of STPS (including SEEs, UPCs and USIs) and provide access to external
users/generators of labor market data; (b) development of norms and guidelines for the use, updating and
access to information databases; and (c) planning for equipment procurement, installation, maintenance and
software upgrading. The design of the information system was based on a detailed needs analysis of
computer and communications hardware and software. This design, together with detailed lists of
equipment and the implementation plan, were reviewed by an information technology expert and agreed
upon at appraisal (see Annex 5.9).



- 36 -
3.62. The proposed project would finance technical assistance to: (a) select the best alternative to
integrate information and establish communications links among prospective users at the central level; (b)
assist in the preparation of equipment lists and evaluation of bid proposals; (c) assist in system development;
and (d) provide tehnical support to users. It would also finance computer and communications hardware
and software for the central and local levels, subscriptions to externa databases, and maintenance of
equipment. Overall responsibility for the development of the information system, including database design,
determination of hardware/software requirements, and definition of procedures for data entry and database
queries, would rest with the General Directorate for Labor Statistics (SET), which would use its own
information experts supported by outside consultants.
3.63.     ect Coordination    nng      t. Experience under the Manpower Training project showed
that STPS staff, with assistance from consultants, managed and coordinated the project quite effectively.
However, experience also showed that administrative work generated by the project required greater
resources than anticipated, especially given the continuous decrease in permanent staff experienced by
STPS. Thus, the proposed project would support the creation of small administrative units, for the project's
duration, in the offices of the General Coordinator for Labor Market Policies, Studies and Statistics and of
the General Director of Employment that would provide project-related adminis&ative support to all STPS
units involved in project implementation activities. The proposed project would finance: (a) fee for fixed-
term temporary staff who would staff the units; (b) travel expenses of STPS staff responsible for supervision
of project activities; (c) basic office equipment; and (d) other incremental operating costs such as office
supplies and equipment maintenance, which would be financed, as all other recurrent costs, on a declining
scale.
A.    PROJECT COSTS
4.1.   The total project cost, including physical and price coningencies, and tax  and duties of about
US$8.1 million, is estimated at US$355.9 million equivalent, of which an estimated US$14.1 million would
be foreign exchange (Fable 4.1). The baseline cost is estimatd at US$322.1 million. Investment costs
amount to 81 % of base costs and recurrent costs amount to 19% of base costs. Project costs were estimated
at July 1992 price levels and include physical contingencies of 5% for equipment, minor office
rehabilitation, and for technical assistance for studies and surveys. Price contingencies were calculated at
3.7% per year for 1992-97. These rates have been applied to local and foreign costs as well, as periodic
exchange rate adjustments based upon purchasing power parity are assumed to compensate for the difference
between projected US dollar inflation and local inflation rate.
4.2. Project cost estimates are based on rocent experience with training and stipend costs. Estimated
costs for minor office rehabilitation are based on detailed cost anysos of five typical State employment
offices. Estimated costs of computer, training, and office equipment are based on local market prices.
Estimates for technical assistance and studies are based on current rates for local and foreign experts. Cost
estimat for travel allowances and other operating costs are bsed on current costs or costing standards
used by STPS.

B.    RECURRENT COSIS
4.3.  Incremental recurrent costs of about US$13.6 million annually, on average, would be generatin by
the project. About one half of these costs, or an average of US$6.4 million per year correspond to i
term temporary staff who would be contracted as UPC promoters and program specialists and manageli. It
is expected that by the end of the project period at least one-fifth of UPC promoters' costs would be
absorbed by enterprises. About one fourth of the costs, or an average of US$3.6 million per year,
correspond to operating costs of the SEEs that would continue to be covered by state budgets as currently
formalized in the annual agreements signed between the Federal and State governments. The agreements
for the current year, signed in September, were reviewed during appraisal, those for subsequent years would
be presented to the Bank during the annual reviews (paras. 3.29 and 5.12). Incremental recurrent costs of
US$1.4 million per year, on average, would be generated by activities of the productivity enhancement
component of the project. A portion of these costs would be gradually covered by enterprise associations as
the program becomes institutionalized within these associations. Recurrent costs of about US$320,000 per
year, on average, would be generated by tie establishment of an incentive system for SEE employees; these
costs are expected to be absorbed, over time, by State Governments. The remainder corresponds to
icremental operating costs of the STPS due to the increased level of activities. It is expected that
implementation and sustainability of the programs are feasible with a modest increase in the sector budget of
about two percent per year, even without further participation from enterprises.
