C.    FINANCING PLAN
4.5.   The project would be financed by the Bank, the Federal and State Govermments and private
enterprises. The proposed loan of US$174.0 million would finance 50% of total project costs net of taxes
(or 49 % of total project costs). The loan would be made to NAFIN, which would make the funds available
to STPS for the execution of the project. The Federal Government would finance US$114.9 million, or
32% of total project costs. The contribution of private enterprises participating in in-service training and
retraining and benefitting from CIMO technical assistance provided under the project would be about
US$49.0 million, or 14% of project costs. State Governments would finance the remainder, US$18.0
million, or 5% of total project costs. Assurance of the adequacy of annual budget allocations by the Federal
and State Governments would be sought during the project annual implementation reviews (para. 5.12).
The contribution of private enterprises would be ensured through the cost-sharing arrangements described in
paras. 3.19-3.22. The Bank loan would finance 100% of foreign exchange expenditures and 47% of local
expenditures. The relatively high percentage of local cost financing under the project is the consequence of
the project's small foreign exchange component. It is justified by the need to ensure a significant impact on
the sector's development and to facilitate desired policy changes. Incremental recurrent costs and stipends
to unemployed workers would be financed by the Bank on a declining basis. Most MSME support activities
would be financed on a declining basis since cost-sharing by enterprises would increase gradually. Also
fixed-term temporary staff services would be financed on a declining basis (para. 4.14).

D.  PROCURENMNT
4.6.   Procurement for goods, office refurbishing, and services would be done following procedures

providers and bear a continuously increasing share of the costs; (b) local private and public training
providers for the retraining program, who would be selected by SEEs based on specific selection criteria
and on their ability to meet training course requirements; and (c) equipment for a maximum of five private
quality control laboratories where the Government would contribute not more than US$100,000 in each
case. Standard bidding documents agreed upon between the Bank and the Mexican Government would be
used during project implementation. The project executing agency, STPS, has substantial experience with

4.7.   Minor Office Rehabilitation. It is expected that about 47 State employment offices would undergo
minor rehabilitation. Because of their modest size and scattered locations, it would not be justified to



- 40 -
procure these works through ICB procedures. Therefore, rehabilitation and refurbishing works costing
US$50,000 or less and up to an aggregate of US$1.4 million would be contracted on the basis of price
quotations obtained from at least three contractors. LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank would be used
when rehabilitation and refurbishing works can be grouped in contracts estimated to cost more than
US$50,000; however, such cases are not expected. Individual contract values are expected to range
between US$15,000 to US$30,000.
4.8.   Equipment and Mobile Units. Equipment to be procured under the project amounts to US$17.2
million. Unless otherwise agreed by the Bank, audiovisual and office equipment, computers, including
software and maintenance, supplemental training center equipment and the mobile units would be procured
through ICB. The 24 mobile units for the SEEs amount to US$0.9 million. Equipment valued at more than
US$50,000 but no more than US$250,000 would be procured through LCB procedures acceptable to the
Bank, up to an aggregate value of US$2.0 million. Contracts for office and other equipment valued at
US$50,000 or less and not exceeding US$1.2 million in aggregate would be awarded on the basis of
comparison of price quotations obtained from at least three eligible suppliers.
4.9.   Publications and Printed Materials. Contracts for printing promotion and information materials and
publications, totaling US$1.4 million, are expected to average below US$50,000 and would be procured
through local shopping or, in cases where the materials are proprietary and obtainable only from one
source, through direct contracting. Should the cost of any package exceed US$50,000, LCB procedures
acceptable to the Bank would be used; however, such cases are not expected.
4.10. Technical Assistance and Studies. All the surveys and some of the labor market studies would be
contracted with private or public sector institutions and with individual . The smaller studies would be done
by STPS's staff. Publicity campaigns would be contracted with private specialized firms. Technical
assistance would be required mainly for institutional strengthening of SEEs and STPS, and long-term
consultants would be contracted to provide key services at the central and local levels. The selection and
appointment of would be consistent with the August 1981 Bank Guidelines for the Use of by World Bank
Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency.
4.11. Prior Review. All procedures, documents, bid evaluation and contract awards for ICB procurement
of equipment, mobile units, other goods and services would be subject to prior review by the Bank. LCB
bid packages would be too small for effective ex-awe review by the Bank, and therefore only the first LCB
procurement for equipment and for printing of promotion and information materials would be reviewed ex-
ante, regardless of their value. For consultant contracts valued at less than US$50,000 the Bank's prior
review would cover only the Terms of Reference, with the exception, inter alia, of consultant assignments
of a critical nature and for amendments to contracts raising the original contract value above the specified
threshold. These will require prior review by the Bank, in accordance with Bank guidelines. An ex-post
review of contracts disbursed against statements of expenditures (SOEs) would be conducted during field
supervision on the basis of random sampling covering at least one in five contracts. Such review would
verify compliance with Bank's consultant guidelines and the use of an agreed upon model contract
document. The proposed prior review levels cover about 70% of contracts for goods financed under the
loan.
4.12. Recording of Procurement Information. The Borrower and the Bank would continue to record
procurement information in a format agreed under Ln. 2876-ME, including completing Forms 384. The
timing of works, goods and service contracts for the first year of project implementation was agreed during
appraisal. The timing of procurement for subsequent years would be agreed during the annual project



- 41 -
implementation reviews and would be based on implementation progress and budgetary resource availability
(para. 5.12).
4.13. Incremental Salaries and Other Operating Costs. The project includes US$67.7 million of
incremental operating costs (see Annex 6) that would not be subject to special procurement regulations.
E.    DISBURSEMENTS
4.14. The proceeds of the loan would be disbursed as follows:
(a)    Computer and office equipment, audiovisual, laboratory and training equipment, and mobile
units, 100% of foreign expenditures, 100% of local expenditures (ex-factory cost), and 90%
of local expenditures for other items procured locally, excluding taxes (paras. 3.12, 3.19-
3.22, 3.25-3.26, 3.41, 3.60-3.63);
(b)   Office rehabilitation, 90% of total expenditures (para. 3.26);
(c)    Technical assistance services, studies, surveys, and staff training, 100% of expenditures,
excluding taxes (paras. 3.19, 3.26-3.27, 3.30, 3.43, 3.45, 3.47-3.48, 3.53, 3.57, 3.60,
3.62);
(d)    Temporary, fixed-term staff, at a rate of 100% until an aggregate amount of US$4.5 million
has been reached, thereafter, disbursements for this category would be made at a rate of
80% until an aggregate amount of US$14.0 million has been reached, thereafter,
disbursements would be made at a rate of 40% until an aggregate amount of US$16.0
million has been reached, and the remainder would be disbursed at a rate of 25 % (paras.
3.6, 3.19, 3.48, 3.63);
(e)    Training of private and public institution trainers, 70% (paras. 3.12, 3.18);
(f)   In-service training of workers and supervisors, at a rate of 35% until an aggregate amount
of US$4.5 million has been reached, thereafter, disbursements for this category would be
made at a rate of 25 % until the aggregate amount of US$9.5 million has been reached, the
remainder would be disbursed at a rate of 15% (paras. 3.6-3.8);
(g)   Technical assistance to enterprises, at a rate of 70% until an aggregate of US$1.7 million
has been reached; thereafter, disbursements for this category would be made at a rate of
60% until the aggregate amount of US$3.0 million has been reached, thereafter, at a rate of
50% until the aggregate amount of US$4.0 million has been reached, and the remainder
would be disbursed at a rate of 40% (paras. 3.9 and 3.12);
(h)    Stipends to trainees, at a rate of 55% until an aggregate amount of US$16.0 million has
been reached; thereafter, at a rate of 40% until an aggregate amount of US$25.0 million has
been reached, thereafter, at a rate of 25% until the aggregate amount of US$35.4 million
has been reached, and the remainder would be disbursed at a rate of 10% (paras. 3.36-
3.38);



- 42 -
(i)    Training programs for the unemployed, at a rate of 100%, excluding taxes (paras. 3.36-
3.38);
(j)    Promotion and information materials, publications and related services, at a rate of 100%,
excluding taxes (paras. 3.8, 3.19, 3.25, 3.45, 3.52, 3.60, 3.62); and
(k)   Incremental operating costs, travel expenses and staff incentives, at a rate of 100% until an
aggregate amount of US$3.0 million has been reached, thereafter, at a rate of 80% until the
aggregate amount of US$7.5 million has been reached, thereafter, at a rate of 40% until the
aggregate amount of US$9.0 million has been reached, and the remaining funds would be
disbursed at a rate of 25% (paras. 3.28, 3.63).
The disbursement schedules for (f) and (g) respond to the need to gradually increase the cost-sharing by
enterprises of MSME support activities. No disbursements would be made against operating costs of the
SEEs financed by the State Governments.
4.15. Proceeds of the proposed loan would be disbursed over a period of 5½h years against withdrawal
applications, to be fully documented for expenditures under contracts valued at more than US$50,000 for
consultants and US$250,000 equivalent for goods and office rehabilitation. Claims for expenditures under
contracts of lesser amounts as well as those related to stipends for trainees, fixed-term temporary staff,
training, and all incremental operating expenditures, would be disbursed against presentation of Statements
of Expenditure (SOEs), for which the supporting documentation would be retained by NAFIN for periodic
inspection by the Bank and by external auditors. The Government would set up a Special Account at Banco
de Mexico in US dollars with an initial deposit of US$12.0 million representing an estimated four months of
Bank-financed expenditures. During negotiations. agreement was reached on the Special Account and on
retroacfive financing of up to USSI1.0 million to cover eligible ependitures incurred after Jul I . 1992
(para. 7.2(k)). Retroactive financing of eligible expenditures for key activities of the productivity
enhancement and labor market adjustment components would be required to avoid interruption of technical
support to enterprises and training of unemployed and displaced workers between the loan closing of the
existing project (Ln. 2876-ME) and effectiveness of the proposed project.
F.    ACCOUNTIG AND AUDITING
4.16. STPS would maintain records and accounts adequate to reflect, in accordance with sound accounting
practices, the resources and expenditures in connection with the execution of the project. Central records
would reflect the resources and expenditures in connection with the execution of the entire project. Other
sets of records, one for each operating unit (UPCs and SEEs) would be maintained by each unit, under
supervision from the central level, and would reflect the resources and expenditures in connection with
project implementation in or for their respective programs. Data from these operating units would be sent

V. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION AND MANAGEMENT
A.    PROJECT READINESS FOR IMLEMENTATION
5.1.  The project was prepared by STPS staff belonging to the DGE and the CGPEET, placed under the
Undersecretary "B," with periodic inputs from Bank missions. Experience with the current project and
STPS's desire to have continuity in project activities and improved services for enterprises and the
unemployed resulted in an accelerated and well-focused project design process. Project preparation is in an
advanced state of readiness for implementation. Key regions for establishing new UPCs have been selected
and designs for in-service training and technical assistance to enterprises have been completed. SEEs are
expected to deliver improved retraining program services. Staff training course design is being developed,
the design of the information system is completed, and the list of surveys and main studies to be completed
under the project has been prepared. Also, standard bidding documents to be used under the project are
completed, as well as standard equipment lists for the first year of project implementation. These were
reviewed and found satisfactory. The project design has also benefitted from incorporation of experience
with pilot programs and from several studies undertaken to: (a) evaluate the impact and cost-effectiveness
of the existing retraining program; (b) assess the role of CIMO in relation to other ongoing support
programs to MSMEs; (c) diagnose the main operational weaknesses of the SEEs; (d) analyze the importance
and nature of unemployment in Mexico; and (e) review the coverage and quality of existing labor market
statistics (Annex 9). The main findings of these studies were discussed in chapter I (paras. 2.2-2.35).
B.    PROJECT ORGANIZATION
5.2.   The project organization would have two levels: the central level, represented by STPS, which
would have overall responsibility for project coordination and implementation; and the local level, through
operating units of the SEEs and UPCs, which would be responsible for the implementation and delivery of
services to unemployed and displaced workers and to enterprises. This organizational setup is similar to the
one used under the previous project and proved to be effective.
C.  PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND COORDINATION
5.3.   Central Level. Project implementation would be carried out within the existing organizational
structure of STPS through units whose operational and administrative responsibilities are directly linked to
project components. Overall authority for project management would be vested in the Undersecretary of
Labor (Undersecretary "B"). The Undersecretary would be assisted in the general management and
coordination of the project by the CGPEET and by the DGE (see Annex 5, Figure 1). The DGE has
already demonstrated its ability to implement effectively and efficiently a Bank-financed project (Ln. 2876-
ME). Institutional strengthening of the DGE would be mostly directed at modernizing its administrative and
operational systems, and at updating the skills of existing and new staff. By contrast, the CGPEET, while
having very qualified staff at the managerial level, is weak in technical areas; thus, institutional
strengthening activities would focus on the provision of specialized technical assistance, to help develop core
groups of staff with technical skills (see technical assistance requirements in Annex 6, Table 7. During the
annual reviews, implementation progress will be assessed and agreements would be reached on further
institutional developmental plans (para. 5.12).



- 44 -
5.4.   CGPEET would be responsible for coordinating the Information, Policy and Institutional
Strengthening component of the project and implementation of some of its activities through its three
General Directorates (Szbcoordinaciones): (a) liaising and entering into agreements with public and private
organizations that would conduct surveys and studies under the project; (b) conducting specialized studies,
analyzing and publishing the results; (c) issuing periodic publications with labor market information and
reviewing technical STPS publications; (d) managing and improving the labor market information system
and the labor market documentation centers; (e) coordinating delivery of technical training for staff; and (f)
coordinating the installation of computers in central offices of STPS.
5.5.   DGE would be directly responsible for the coordination and implementation of the Productivity
Enhancement and the Labor Market Adjustment components of the project. It would also have direct
responsibility for specific activities under the Information, Policy, and Institutional Strengthening component
of the project: (a) managing, updating, and improving DGE's human resources and labor market
information database; (b) providing technical support to STPS units in the implementation of computerized
data collection and processing in the SEEs and UPCs, and training of staff in the use of the systems; (c)
carrying out studies and basic data analysis to support DGE's policies and programs; (d) monitoring
implementation of project activities and evaluating the impact of the productivity enhancement and retraining
activities of the project; and (e) coordinating staff training activities of DGE and the SEEs and UPCs.
5.6.  To facilitate coordination between CGPEET and DGE and to support decision-making related to
studies, surveys, and publications, a high-level technical committee was set up in June 1992. It is chaired
by the General Coordinator of the CGPEET and its members are the General Directors of the technical
areas of STPS. The committee would be supported by outside experts when needed. Its recommendations
would be presented to the Undersecretary "B" for approval.
5.7.   The CGPEET General Coordinator and the DGE General Director would be assisted and supported
by staff and consultants from their directorates and other STPS administrative units to coordinate and
implement project activities, manage personnel, procurement, consultant contracting, accounting and
financial activities under the project. STPS, having successfully implemented the Manpower Training
project through DGE, already has adequate support systems in place to manage and monitor the project's
physical, financial and human resource activities. The use of additional fixed-term temporary staff and
technical assistance would reinforce its capacity to manage and implement the new project activities (para.
3.63). STPS is also familiar with Bank procedures.
5.8.   Local Level. Responsibility for assisting in project implementation at the local level would rest with
the operating units of the SEEs and the UPCs, with support from DGE at the central level. The local
operating units would be responsible for implementing project components and for providing assistance to
unemployed and displaced workers, as well as technical assistance and support services to enterprises within
the area of their jurisdiction. Both UPCs and SEEs have acquired considerable experience in implementing
the CIMO and retraining programs through their involvement in the Manpower Training project.
D.  IMPLEMNTATION SCHEDULE
5.9.   The proposed project would be implemented over a period of about five years and is expeted to be
completed by December 31, 1997, with the closing date set for June 30, 1998. The cost estimates prepared
for the various components (Annex 6) and implementation schedule (Annex 8) would serve as a basis for
project implementation planning and would be updated annually by STPS in preparation for the annual
project implementation reviews (para. 5.12). During the annual reviews with the Government, the Bank



- 45 -
would pay particular attention to the effectiveness and efficiency of implementation of project components,
and changes would be agreed as needed. In addition, an in-depth mid-term review (para. 5.13) would
provide an opportunity for a preliminary assessment of project impact and performance and for the
definition of necessary adjustments.
E.    MONITORING TARGETS
5.10. To assist in the monitoring of project implementation, particularly in the context of the annual and
mid-term reviews, a comprehensive and well-focused list of monitoring indicators would be used (Annex 8).
It includes a detailed implementation schedule for all surveys and studies. This list was discussed and
agreed upon during appraisal. During negotiafions. a=reemce was reached on the list of peCrfrance

F.    LOAN SUPERVISION, ANNUAL AND MID-TERM REVIEWS
5.11. Project supervision is expected to take place at least twice a year, to monitor progress with the
proposed project. Supervision missions at the appropriate point of the annual budget cycle would seek
assurance of the adequacy of budget allocations for each component, which would be confirmed in the
annual reviews. For the surveys, studies and program evaluation subcomponents, supervision would need to
include experts in labor economics. Overall supervision needs for the proposed project are expected to be
somewhat higher than those required by the Manpower Training project. They are estimated at about 125
staff weeks, or an average of 25 staff weeks per year, over the project life. The required supervision team
would be a combination of Bank staff and consultants with experience in the operation of similar projects in
Latin America and other regions. Their main areas of expertise would be labor economics, training,
business administration, and administration of employment services. The mid-term review, which would
rely on a similar team of experts, would require about 12 staffweeks in addition to regular supervision
requirements.
5.12. Specific annual reviews, no later than November 30 of each year, would provide an opportunity for
an assessment of project impact and performance, and for an agreement of necessary adjustments. During
these reviews, progress in compliance with the actions and policies specified in the sector policy statement
(Annex 1) would be assessed. The cost estimates prepared for the various components (Annex 6) and the
key monitoring indicators and implementation targets (Annex 8) would serve as a basis for project
implementation planning, and would be updated annually by STPS in preparation for the annual reviews.
The annual project implementation review would: (a) analyze previous year performance as measured by
the set of monitoring indicators and targets, and review budget allocation and expenditures; (b) analyze and
agree on the proposed work plan and budget level for the following year to ensure the adequacy of
counterpart funding; (c) review the surveys and studies carried out the previous year and discuss their
implications for project design, as well as discuss and agree on the Terms of Reference for studies to be



- 46 -
undertaken in the following year; (d) verify that the timing of completion of the survey and study program
complies with the agreed upon schedule; (e) assess the effectiveness of project management and coordination
and discuss adjustments to be made in the project's implementation schedule or targets; (f) review the
annual agreements with the State Governments; and (g) consider progress in implementing the Government's
policy statement. The proposed work plan and budget for the following year would have to be previously
defined at the central and local levels and contain a detailed description of targets and planned activities.

VI. PROJECT BENEFITS AND RISKS
A.    PROJECT BENEFTIS
6.1.  The project would increase productivity and competitiveness in MSMEs, creating higher earnings
and employment opportunities for Mexican workers. About 35,000 enterprises, employing about 1.0
million workers, are expected to be reached. Project benefits, however, would extend beyond the
participating enterprises, as the project is intended to have an important demonstration effect. Through the
increased involvement of private enterprises in MSME support programs and the promotion of closer inter-
institutional links, the project would also foster improvements in the quality of training services supplied to
enterprises and a faster rate of adoption of new technologies through provision of a better trained labor
force. The labor market adjustment process would be eased as the improved quality of services provided to
unemployed and displaced workers would reduce the costs of hiring and job search, improve the job
matching process and thus provide better opportunities for employment stability and subsequent wage
growth. Approximately 2 million unemployed workers are expected to benefit from placement services over
the project life. About 300,000 unemployed workers whose current skills fail to match those in demand
would also benefit from the training to be provided. The cost effectiveness of the retraining programs will
be enhanced by targeting the training to those groups (workers with at least completed primary education
and some work experience) where evaluation has demonstrated the highest rates of return to such training.
The results of the evaluation showed a 20-30 % monthly salary increase for male trainees with some prior
work experience. For male target trainees, the evaluation showed that benefits of program participation
outweigh the costs of the program within 3 months of starting employment. For younger inexperienced
workers and rural migrants, the project is expected to improve their entry conditions in the urban labor
market. In addition to sharing the general benefits of the project listed above, women would benefit from
the improvements to be introduced in the training programs and reduction of labor market barriers which
hamper the realization of the full benefits of training by females. Finally, the project would enhance the
Government's capacity to design, monitor and evaluate labor market policies and programs, thus
contributing to a more efficient use of public resources. The project is not expected to have any significant
environmental impact.
B.  IPACT ON WOMEN
6.2.   Women hold about one third of all jobs in the manufacturing sector and nearly balf in the service
sector. They would thus comprise a significant fraction of workers benefitting from project-supported
assistance to MSMEs. Women also suffer more frequently from unemployment than men, and when
unemployed, suffer spells of longer duration. They would therefore largely benefit from job placement
assistance and retraining programs. Agreement has been reached with Government that a full investigation
will be carried out and an appropriate action plan developed to deal with the issue of effectiveness of
training provided to women (para. 3.35 and Annex 1).
C.    PROJECT RISKS
6.3.   The main risks are: (a) a possible decline in Government commitment to project objectives after the
change in administration in 1994. The risk is small given the existing strong support for current economic
policies and the importance given to productivity and mobility issues in light of the North American Free
Trade Agreement (NAFTA); (b) insufficient budgetary allocations to guarantee program sustainability
beyond project completion. This risk will be minimized by the coverage of trainee stipends and recurrent
costs on a declining basis, by increased private sector participation in the financing and operation of the



- 48 -
CIMO program, and by rigorous monitoring of budgetary allocations during the annual project
implementation reviews; (c) slower than expected acceptance by enterprises of the productivity enhancement
actions. This risk quite small given growing support by private enterprises of interventions initiated under
the ongoing Bank-supported Manpower Training Project and will be further mitigated by strengthened
program management and availability of services; and (d) project complexity and the fact that there are
many implementing agencies at different levels may limit the achievement of the project objectives. This
risk will be mitigated through the early identification and resolution of any problems which develop through
close supervision, and the mid-term and annual project reviews.

7.1.  During negotiations, the Government provided assurances that:

(d)   (i) the precise guidelines for the operation of the pilot Basic Education and Training
program, including contents of the program courses and detailed operational procedures,

the evaluation of the pilot in line with terms of reference acceptable to the Bank would be

(f)   (1) all survey questionnaires would be sent to the Bank for review; and (ii) basic survey

incorporate the required changes (para. 3.57);
()    that the Government would submit during the annual reviews, satisfactory updated training
plans, revised on the basis of the annual needs analysis, for the following year of program
implementation (para. 3.60);
(k)    on the Special Account and on retroactive financing of up to US$11.0 million to cover
eligible expenditures incurred after July 1, 1992 (para. 4.15); and
1I)    on the list of performance indicators and project progress review criteria to be used
including for the annual and mid-term reviews (para. 5.10).
7.3.   The conditions for disbursement would be that:
(a)   for the 24 'mobile" employment units, the Government would present an evaluation,
satisfactory to the Bank, of the experience with the six pilot units supported under the
Manpower Training project (Ln. 2876-ME) (para. 3.25); and
(b)   for the work incentive program, the Government would present the guidelines for the
operation of the work incentive scheme for SEE staff, including an implementation schedule,
satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.28).
7.4.   An event of suspension of disbursements for the retraining program for the unemployed beyond
US$25.0 million would arise if: (i) the Govenment fails to present to the Bank, within 60 days of the time
such amount is reached, a satisfactory evaluation that: (a) measures the impact of altenative training
programs and delivery systems on the employment and earnings of the trainees; (b) identifies possible
improvements in program targeting; and (c) assesses the cost-effectiveness of the program and its different
modalities; and (ii) the Govenment fails to present a plan of action, satisfactory to the Bank, for improving
such retraining program in a manner that takes into account the results of the evaluation. The timetable for
the completion of the evaluation and agreed action plan is by March 31, 1995. Inter alia, the number of
trainees would not exceed 55,000 per annum until such satisfactory evaluation has been completed (para.
3.37).
7.5.   Rsomm         . Subject to the above conditions, the proposed project would constitute a suitable
basis for a Bank loan of US$174 million equivalent to the United Mexican States, to be repaid over a period
of 15 years, including a five-year grace period.

LABOR SECTOR
POLICY STATEMENT
MCO
LAIIOR MAUAND PRDClYEN}IANCEMEN PROJCT
SER CTOR POLICY STATENT
November 19, 1992
Mr. LeWis Proston
President
The World Bank
1818 H Street, N.W.
Washington D.C., 20433
Dear Mr. Preston:
1.    Over the past decade the Mexican economy has undergone a major transformation. It was
initiated by a profound macroeconomic adjustment process, aimed at eliminating major imbalances
that led in 1982 to a break in the country's development process. At the same time, a set of
structural reforms was put into effect in order to transform a highly regulated and protected economy,
redefine the role of the state and create the necessary conditions both to foster sustained growth of
productive employment and wealth and to respond more effectively to the most pressing needs of the
Mexican people.
2.    The adjustments encompassed a comprehensive tax reform, the successful renegotiation of the
foreign debt and a stabilization strategy based on consensus gathering and a strong fiscal and
monetary adjustment policy. These macroeconomic changes were accompanied by a far reaching
trade reform that turned the Mexican economy into one of the most open among the countries of the
developing world, a deregulation of key sectors such as transport and communications, an extensive
privatization program, and a substantive reform of the ejido system (Mexico's system of communal
farming ownership). In parallel, the Government has financed an unprecdented social policy
program which focuses on the neediest people and is based on community initiatives and participation
in project decision-making.
3.    The macroeconomic stabilization policies, together with the set of structura reforms
implemented during this period, have set the stage for long-term economic development. However,
meeting the demands of a rapidly changing and challenging world and responding more effectively to
the social needs of the population will require sustained action in ongoing reform, complemented by
adequate finance for investment, improved physical infrastructure and, especily, an educated and
skilled labor force. The latte is critical to the country's ability to increase the productivity and living
standards of its workers, to produce higher quality goods and to compete on international markets.
Moreover, full succes of the adjustment program requires policies that are effective in promoting
social mobility and a more equal distribution of the gains from economic reform. These objectives
can be achieved only through sustained investments in human capital.
4.    Further liberaization of the economy through the expected Free Trade Agreement with
Caunda and the U.S. (NAFTA) will accentuat the need for an educated and skilled labor force.



- 54 -                                Annex I
LABOR SECTOR
POLICY STATEMENT
NAFTA will represent an unprecedented integration of large economies at very different stages of
development. Mexico's capability to compete with more developed U.S. and Canadian producers will
hinge on its ability to increase the productivity of its workers, adopt and improve new production
processes and technologies, train less-skilled workers, and develop new skills as the structure of
demand evolves. It will also require implementation of policies aimed at increasing labor mobility
and facilitating the flow of labor between expanding and contracting sectors and regions.
5.    Conscious of these needs, the Mexican Government is now giving high priority to the
development of human resources through investments in human capital both before and after entry
into the labor market. It is also making major efforts to reduce the cost of labor mobility and labor
market adjustment. Success in this endeavour requires close collaboration and understanding between
Government, the private sector and the labor movement. In this context, the Government has
committed itself to the following:
National Policy for the Modernization of the Labor Market
6.    In an increasingly interdependent and competitive international environment, Mexico's ability
to increase the living standards of its people depends critically on its capacity to increase the
productivity of its workers and firms, adopt and improve new technologies, produce higher quality
goods and improve the use of its resources. Conscious of this, the present administration has
committed itself to the modernization of the Mexican economy. In this process, the Government has
given highest priority to the development of human resources through investments in education and
training.
7.    As part of its modernization strategy, the Administration established in the National
Development Plan 1989-94 general criteria governing the education and training policy, which are
rooted in principles of equity and efficiency. Based on this policy, the Federal Government has
formulated the National Training and Productivity Program (Programa Nadonal de Capacitaci6n y
Produclvldad 1991-94, PRONACAP). On May 25, 1992, in line with its goal of stimulating the
participation of workers and private-sector firms in the modernization process, the Federal
Government signed an agreement with representatives of business and labor, the National Agreement
for Increased Quality and Productivity (Acuerdo Nadonal para la Elevaci6n de la Producnvidad y la
Calidad, ANEPC).
8.    Recognizing that the modernization of the economy can only be brought about through a
collaborative effort involving all participants in the productive process, the Goverment has sought
the active participation of business, workers and unions in the design of its development policies.
One outcome of this emphasis on dialogue and collaboration is the new Pact for Stability,
Competitiveness and Employment (Pacto para la Estabilidad, La Competitividad y el Emplco,
PECE), replacing the former Pact for Stability and Economic Growth (Pacto para la Estabilidad y cl
Crecmiento Economico), which has served since December 1988 as an instrument for the
Government and the productive sectors to agree on concerted actions to abate inflation, promote
economic growth and structural change. PRONACAP, ANEPC and PECE are described below.
National        aned Ftoduivitv BroX= (PRONACAP)
9.    PRONACAP sets forth the general direction, objectives and characteristics of the
Government's training and productivity strategy. The purpose of the program is to promote a greater



-55-                                   Anex I
LABOR SECTOR
POLICY STATEMENT
efficiency in the use of resources, so as to increase productivity and thereby the standards of living of
Mexican workers. PRONACAP recognizes the importance of technological change, access to
information, and improvements in organization and management in achieving greater efficiency in the
use of resources. However, its primary focus is on the development of human resources, which are
viewed as the key element in increasing productivity and sustaining growth.
10.   The main objectives of PRONACAP are to: (i) increase the skills of the labor force as a
means for raising productivity and improving the living standards of Mexican workers; (ii) foster skill
upgrading and retraining of workers necessary to support the adoption of new technologies and rapid
adjustment to changing structures of demand; (iii) promote a work environment that fosters efficiency,
quality and productivity; and (iv) broaden the basis for growth through a wider distribution of
income-generating opportunities within the population.
11.   To achieve these objectives, the Government has undertaken actions to improve the
effectiveness, quality and relevance of both pre-service and in-service training programs. To this end,
PRONACAP has sought to strengthen the linkages between the education and training system and the
productive sector, by encouraging the active participation of business organizations, worker
associations, educational institutions and research centers in the implementation of the program.
12.   In parallel, in the realm of education, the Government has signed the Agreement for the
Modernization of Basic Education on May 18, 1992 which seeks to: (i) adjust the educational system
so that it is more responsive to the needs of the labor market; (ii) increase the coverage and quality of
education for socially disadvantaged groups; and (iii) strengthen math and science education,
beginning at the level of basic education.
National Agreement for Increased Ouality and Productivity (ANEPC)
13.   The National Agreement for Increased Quality and Productivity (ANEPC) was jointly signed
on May 25, 1992 by the Federal Government and representatives of workers' and employers'
associations. It falls within the framework of the PECE and thus is based upon the principles of
dialogue and collaboration between the various participants of the production process. The main
objective of ANEPC is to eliminate structural inefficiencies in the use of resources and barriers to
productivity growth. ANEPC reflects the consensus between the signing parties on the need to foster
a more efficient use of resources, a faster rate of adoption of new technologies in the Mexican
economy, and the need to develop incentives to promote an active participation of the labor force in
the process.
14.   ANEPC emphasizes that sustained increases in productivity cannot be brought about by the
efforts of a single factor of production, be it labor or physical capital; rather, it views productivity
growth as the result of the interaction of a multiplicity of elements involving the planning and
organization of production, the skills and motivation of the worklforce, the availability of financial
resources, an efficient public administration, and an increased capacity to adopt and adapt to
technological change. In consequence, ANEPC underscores the need to Ci) involve all participants in
the production process - workers, managers, administrators, business and labor associations - in a
common effort to achieve sustained increases in quality and productivity; and (ii) ensure equity in the
distribution of benefits which will arise from these productivity and quality gains.



-6 -                                  Annx 1
LABOR SECTOR
POLICY STATEMENT
15.   To achieve these objectives, the Federal Government and the representatives of worker and
business associations have set forth the following action plan:
2    Modemizgaion of Organizational Structures of Production. The primary objective of
this strategy is to remove rigidities in the organization and management of production, to
increase the rate of adoption of new technologies in the enterprise, and strengthen firms'
ability to adapt to changes in consumer preferences and fluctuations in worldwide demand.
To this end, efforts will be aimed at promoting more streamlined organizational and
hierarchical structura within the enterprise, through: (i) fostering a greater flow of
information to and within the firm; (ii) developing efficiency indicators for monitoring
enterprises improvements in productivity, quality and consumer satisfaction; (iii) promoting
increased linkage between firms and their suppliers; and (iv) increasing the participation of
workers at all levels within the enterprise, and promoting their understanding of the whole
organizational structure of the firm.
f                  _.A                            The goal is to strengthen
administration and management in the private and public sectors, to facilitate the
modenization of organizational structures of production in the context of an increasingly open
and competitive economy. To this end, actions will focus on: (i) upgrading the skills of
administrators and managers; (ii) improving the efficiency and quality of public services; and
(iii) creating a work environment that emphasizes quality and productivity, and encourages
innovation.
()    Increased Emphasis on Human Resources. ANEPC emphasizes that labor must be
viewed as a valuable and essential factor of production, complementary to physical capital and
technology. It recognizes that increasing productivity requires sustained investments in the
human capital of workers, suitable working conditions and work environment, and incentives
for workers to reward individual performance. This will be achieved through: (i) promotion
of in-service raning for the whole occupational structure of the enterprise, including
retraining of workers to adapt their skills to changing technological requirements; (ii)
improvements in work conditions through better enforcement of existing regulations and
promotion programs for occupational safety and health; (iii) the development of a well
structured set of incentiveg for workers through an increased flow of information between
workers and their supervisors, enhanced opportunities for self-development and job
satisfaction of workers, and the recognition and reward of good performance; and (iv) the
revision of existing promotion and wage schemes to reward individual performance.
f)               g of Labor Relations.    ANEPC highlights the need to: (i) strengthen
the dialogue betwen workers, managers and enterprise owners to minimize conflict; and (ii)
promote the participation of worker associations in the definition of new reward systems, and
in the enforcement of regulations governing safety, health and worker protection in the
enterprise. These objectives will be promoted through a strengthened system of employer-
worker dialogue and improved conciliation procedures.
Z  Technological IXrovement Raesrch and Develgpment. ANEPC stresse that
technology research and the adoption of new technologies are key to improving the quality,
producdvity and international competitiveness of enterprises. The parties agree to: (i)
strengthen science education within the established educational system, beginning at the level



- 57 -                                Annex I
LABOR SECTOR
POLICY STATEMENT
of basic education; (ii) promote investments in R&D within firms; and (iii) develop training,
information systems and consulting services to induce a faster rate of adoption of new
technologies within the private sector.
(fl    Maintaining a Favorable Macroeconomic Environment. ANEPC underscores the
Government's commitment to maintaining a macroeconomic environment that fosters price
stability and real income per capita growth. To simulate a more efficient use of resources,
increased competitiveness and productivity growth, the Government also commits itself to:(i)
attend to the needs for physical and human infrastructure; (ii) improve the efficiency of public
services; (iii) ensure that the regulatory framework promotes efficiency and competitiveness;
and (iv) develop information systems that facilitate the decision-making process and the
monitoring of productivity performance.
Pact for Stability. Compdetitivenes and Floymept (PCE)
16.   The PECE, an agreement signed betwe :n the Federal Government, business, agricultural
sector and labor on October 22, 1992, is intended to strengthen the Government's strategy to involve
the private sector and workers' associations in consolidating the country's economic stability,
furthering its competitiveness, and creating employment. A principal objective of the PECE is to
support actions that will lead to increased productivity and competitiveness, generate employment
while at the same time maintaining price stability.
17.   The North American Free Trade Agreement (NAFTA) between Mexico, the United States and
Canada and the decline of global economic activity will require a more strengthened effort to improve
productivity and thus competitiveness. The NAFTA will result in more opportunities for economic
expansion, increase in exports, expansion in productive investments, employment generation and a
more equitable distribution of income.
18.   The main points embodied in the PECE are: (i) maintaining strict fiscal discipline through the
Government's proposed Income Law Initiative and Budget, currently being reviewed by Congress; (i)
achieving single digit inflation; (iii) setting the minimum wage, on the basis of the PECE, that will be
in effect on January 1, 1993; (iv) revising contractual agreements between labor and business to
encourage economic growth and employment generation, and improve workers' incomes; (v)
businesses will absorb, as much as possible, the cost increases derived from the agreement without
increasing the prices of their products and services and will maintain their adequate supply; (vi)
establishing agricultural sector support programs that would benefit the producers without increasing
consumer prices; and (vii) expanding the range of the floating exchange rate (banda de flotacion). A
single Committee for PECE Monitoring and Evaluation will coordinate with State Committea to
develop concrete action plans to increase productivity, competitiveness and employment generation in
all sectors.
Govenmnent PrgM= and Polid-e
19.   To implement the strategies desaibed in the National iing nd Pductivty Program and
in the National Agreement for Increased Ouality and Productivity as described above, the Federal
Govenment has undertaken several policy actions directed at (i) increasing the skills and productivity
of workers and firms; and Cii) retrAining displaced and unemployed workers. These actions were
supported by the Manpower Trairing Project. The Federal Government now intends to develop these



- 58 -                                 Annex 1
LABOR SECTOR
POLICY STATEMENT
actions further through several policy instruments which will be supported by the Labor Market and
Productivity Enhancement Project. These policy instruments and the supporting project activities and
targets are outlined below.
Consolidation and Extension of the CIMO Program
20.   The Multiple Support Service Program to Micro, Small and Medium Size Enterprises (CIMO)
program, operated by the Secretariat of Labor and Social Welfare (STPS), is directly linked to the
PRONACAP and the ANEPC. Its primary objective is to eliminate inefficiencies in the use of
resources and barriers to productivity growth. The program aims to: (i) modenize management and
organization of production through the provision of technical assistance to enterprises, and improved
information flows; (ii) improve the quality of human resources and induce the adoption of new
technology through the promotion of in-service training, and through improvements in work
conditions and reward systems; and (iii) strengthen the operation and efficiency of support programs
for the private sector through upgrading the skills of managers and administrators.
21.   To achieve these objectives, the program would provide training, process-consulting services,
and access to industrial and technological information to micro, small and medium size enterprises.
A fundamental characteristic of this program is that the role of the Government is strictly one of
coordinator/facilitator, promoting interest in worker training and improved product quality,
organization and management, and disseminating information and facilitating access to the relevant
instructors or services.
22.   In line with the approach set forth in the ANEPC, one of the objectives of the Federal
Government is to increase steadily the share of the participation of the private sector in the operation
and financing of the program. Ultimately, the Government would maintain only an information and
technical assistance role in its support to MSMEs. To this end, cost-sharing arrangements for
services facilitated under the CIMO program will be agreed upon with the private sector according to
the following targets: (i) for training activities, the share of costs financed by the private sector will
gradually increase from 65% in 1993 to 85% in 1997. During these five years, in which 11,250
training courses are planned, enterprises would contribute an average of 75% of training costs; (ii) for
process-consulting services, the share of costs financed by the private sector would increase from
30% to 60% over the same period. On average, enterprises would cover approximately 45% of costs
associated with this service. A total of 3,000 consultancies are planned over the five year period
1993-1997; and (iii) for industrial and technological information services, the private sector would
finance 30% of the costs. It is anticipated that there will be 3,000 enterprise utilizations of this
service over the five year period 1993-1997.
23.   The Government would also support actions aimed at improving the quality and relevance of
training and consulting services supplied to enterprises through the improvement of consultants' skills.
The consultants will bear 30% of these training costs, the program 70%. Additionally there will be a
net increase of 70 promoters in the Regional Productivity Support Units (Unidad Promotora de
Compeddvidad, UPCs) in 1993, recruited through a stricter selection process, rising to 170 by 1997.
24.   The Federal Government intends to assimilate the UPCs into business organizations of each
region over the medium term, in coordination with each local executive committee of the UPCs.
Several actions will facilitate this assimilation process. On the technical side, the UPCs will be
transformed into Multiple Service Support Units (Unidades de Sercidos Integrados, USI), which is a



-59-                                   Annex I
LABOR SECTOR
POLICY STATEMENT
new concept to provide services to enterprises. On the legal side, the local executive committees of
the UPCs will assume a new legal status allowing them to receive funds from other sources. It is
expected that by the end of 1997, at least 20% of the staff will be financed by the private sector.
25.   According to the decreasing government share of the costs proposed in the cost-sharing
structure for 1993-1997, the CIMO program will ensure the institutionalization of the services beyond
the end of the program. This process will be assisted through: (i) the increasing understanding by
enterprises of the knowledge they need and how to gain access to it; (ii) the decreasing amount of
government support to promote training, that will decline to only 15% at the end of the program,
permitting a natural transition; (iii) growth in the supply of local consulting services as the econmy
developes; and (iv) the information service will be provided primarily through local technical
resources by 1996, thereby reducing its cost. All of these capacities will be assimilated by business
organizations and will be institutionalized through appropriate legal schemes.
26.   Overall, the contribution of the private sector is estimated to grow from US$5.0 million (56
percent of total costs) in 1993 to about US$12.0 million (76 percent of total costs) in 1997. The
Government contribution is estimated to decline slightly from US$ 3.9 million in 1993 to about USS
3.8 million in 1997, however this represents a major decline in the share of the costs covered by
Government from 44 percent to 24 percent over this period.
27.   To promote a more integrated private-sector development, the Government would seek
improved coordination between the CIMO program and other institutions providing support to micro,
small and medium-sized enterprises. To this end, work is underway to reach an umbrella agreement
of collaboration between STPS, the Secretariat of Commerce and Industrial Development (SECOFI),
and Nacional FYnanciera (NAFIN) by March 31, 1993.
28.   With regard to SECOFI, the agreement seeks to ensure the participation of CIMO
representatives in the Joint Commission for the Modernization of the Micro, Small and Medium Size
Industry (Comisi6n Mixta part, la Modernizaci6n de la Industria Micro, Pequefla y Medana). The
objectives are to: (i) identify potential business groups to participate in the CIMO program; and (ii)
to facilitate the access of enterprises receiving CIMO support to SECOFI's single window mechanism
established to speed up the various administrative requirements for the operation of micro, small and
medium-sized enterprises.
29.   With regard to NAFIN, the agreement seeks to enable the CIMO program to take advantage
of NAFIN's Program for Managerial Development (Programa de Desarrollo Empresarial). This
program was designed to provide basic training to managers of micro, small and medium-sized firms.
In addition, NAFIN will help enterprises served by CIMO gain access to the different types of
financing it provides, as well as to specialized technical assistance. NAFIN will also help identify
potential enterprises that can be supported through CIMO.
30.   The Banco Nacional de Comercio Exterior (BANCOMEXT) would support CIMO in the
following areas: access to information on business opportunities, characteristics and requirements for
exports, technical assistance and financing for suppliers of export enterprises, direct exporters and
enterprises with export potential. Modalities already exist for this support and the pace of
implementation will be accelerated in 1993.



- 60 -                                Annex 1
LABOR SECTOR
POLICY STATEMENT
&U_1af o I_  Maket Adjumet MWeab_
31.   To facilitate labor market adjustment, increase labor mobility and thus increase efficiency in
the use and allocation of labor resources, the Government has improved the coverage, quality and
efficiency of placement services and increased the flow of information on local and regional labor
markets through enhanced employee incentives linked to productivity, stronger coordination with the
private sector, improved telecommunications links and a rationalization of the information system.
32.   The Government will strengthen the National Employment Service and promote the creation
of a framework for a closer and more effective coordination between public and private placement
agencies.
33.   The general legal foundation for the operation of private employment services is given by
Section XXV of Article 123 of the Mexican Constitution, which stipulates that employment services
may be offered by public or private organizations, but must be done so free of charge to the job
seeker. The Federal Labor Law, Articles 538, 539 and 539 A, B, C, D, and F reiterates the
complementary character of placement services, and the legality of its provision by either public or
private agencies.
34.   Although the legal framework allows the existence of private placement agencies, their
operation was hampered in the past by the Mexican Government's subscription to Part I of
Agreement No. 96 of the International Labor Organization, which ratified the monopoly power of the
State Employment Agency. However, as of March 1, 1991, Mexico has rescinded its signing of Part
11, and subscribed to Part Im of Agreement No. 96 of the ILO, which ratifies the legal guarantees of
the Mexican regulations that allow the operation of private agencies. Thus, there are no longer any
legal obstacles to the operation of private placement agencies.
35.   In order to strengthen the operation of the National Employment Service (SNE) and its
network of State Employment Services (SEEs), the Government will seek the increasing financial
participation of the State Governments in the operation of the SEEs. The State Governments will
integrate federal staff working in the SEEs into their State social security system over the period
1993-1996. The targets are that at least 25% of the federal staff will be so incorporated each year
such that all are covered by the end of 1996. This participation is ensured through annual agreements
signed between the Federal and State Governments within the context of the Convenmos de DesarroUo
Social. Compliance by the states will be monitored closely and compliance will be a requirement for
continued federal financing.
36.   In line with its goals of upgrading the skills of the labor force and widening the distribution of
training opportunities within the population, the Government will improve the cost-effectiveness of the
retraining program for unemployed and displaced workers (PROBECAT) through improved targeting.
From December 1992, more rigorous selection criteria will be applied to trainees. Workers should
have significant prior work experience, be between 18 and 55 years of age and have completed either
full primary or at least some secondary education. Workers between the age of 18 and 25 years
without significant work experience could qualify for in-plant training only. In order to provide more
time for a fuller evaluation of PROBECAT, the number of trainees will be frozen at the 1992 level of
55,000. A full evaluation will be completed by March 31, 1995, and only the cost-effecdve elements
of training will be continued, or where justified, expanded. As part of its policy for establishing links
with the private sector, the Federal Government will increase the participation of private enterprises



-61 -                                  An    I
LABOR SECrOR
POLICY STATEMENT
in the operation and financing of these training activities through outreach and dissemination. Many
enterprises are already participating and financing the costs of materials and trainers from their own
resources. About 75 percent of the trainees have even been able to find employment with the
enterprise where the training has taken place.
37.   The Government intends to fully investigate the reasons for the apparent low benefits for
female trainees under PROBECAT. The problems may be less to do with the training per se, but
rather other rigidities or barriers in the labor market for women. This issue will be fully reviewed
and appropriate corrective actions taken. In order to assist labor mobility anticipated as a result of
the full implementation of NAFTA, a pilot training program for under-qualified rural migrants will be
established. The pilot will be subject to a full review also. The World Bank will be invited to
discuss the outcome of these reviews and to make recommendations on the actions to be taken.
lngroed Womution. Polig and bInsitutional Slrenthaftn
38.   As evidenced in the ANEPC, the Federal (Government recognizes that the modernization of
the country requires an effort to strengthen and ;estructure the operation of the Public Administration;
in regard to the modernization of the labor market, this implies a strengthening, in particular, of the
technical areas of STPS. At the same time, the Federal Government also acknowledges that in the
context of Mexico's reform process, the availability of timely and accurate information on the labor
market. is a pressing need. To address this growing demand for information and support its objective
of strengthening the Administration, the Government has begun the process of rationalizing labor
market information systems, improving the coordination of the many agencies involved (eg. STPS,
SEEs and UPCs), and developing the skills of the staff working in these organizations through
appropriate training. Much remains to be done in all these areas to provide a modern, effective and
accessible labor market information system and to improve policy formulation for the sector, however
the Government is committed to modernizing and strengthening its labor market information system
and policy formulation. Additional computer equipment and an enhanced training program for staff
in the STPS, UPCs and the SEEs will commence in 1993. A program of high priority labor market
surveys and studies has been developed for the period 1993-97 covering such areas as employment,
wages, productivity, migration trends and incentives, and an evaluation of micro-enterprises. All
these studies and surveys are designed to provide policy-relevant information for policy makers in the
sector and will be completed according to a specified schedule rer the period 1993-97. The World
Bank will be invited to discuss the results of these studies and surveys and to make recommendations
on the actions to be taken. The training and computer equipment will both facilitate the gathering and
analysis of data and equip the staff to implement labor policies and *erve the labor market more
effectively.

-68 -                               Annex 3.1
PROBECAT & CIMO PROGRAMS
EVALUAIION OF THE fCO LBR R33tAMG PROGRAM:
SARY OF R13SULTS
In the early 1980s, Mexico entered a period of economic crisis when rising world interest
rates, falling oil prices and a refusal by external creditors to roll over Mexico's short-term debt put an
end to its expansionary policies. Against this background, the Government of Mexico established a
retraining program in 1984 for unemployed and displaced workers (Programa de Becas de
Capacitacion para Trabajadores, or PROBECAT). Its objective was to dampen the social costs of
major economic restructuring and rising unemployment during this period of macroeconomic turmoil.
In 1987, the PROBECAT program was incorporated into the Bank-supported Manpower Training
Project, and its scope and coverage expanded. Since that time, PROBECAT has provided short-term
vocational training to over 250,000 unemployed people-approximately 50,000 trainees per year.
Although originally viewed as a transitional measure, the PROBECAT program is currently
being considered for continuation over a longer period of time. Several reasons exist for this. The
most important is concern about the impact of the proposed North America Free Trade Agreement
(NAFTA) on migration flows, especially from rural areas, and on unemployment. Second, while the
adjustment process to date has taken place with relatively little impact on observed unemployment
rates, sizeable labor reallocations between expanding and contracting sectors still remain with further
liberalization and privatization of the Mexican economy. The third reason is that the PROBECAT
program is the only unemployment scheme currently in place in Mexico. In making decisions about
the future of the PROBECAT program, policymakers will need information about the impact of
retraining on target populations and the cost-effectiveness of the program.
This annex summarizes the main findings of an evaluation of the PROBECAT program.Y'
The objective of said evaluation was to determine the impact of participation in the PROBECAT
program on the post-training employment and incomes of trainees. We sought to address four
questions: (i) what is the impact of training on post-training employment of trainees; (ii) does
training increase the speed with which they move from unemployment to employment?; (iii)
conditional on their finding employment, what effect does training have on monthly earnings, hours
worked, and hourly wages of trainees?; and (iv) do the monetary benefits from program participation
outweigh the costs of providing retraining?
In answering these four questions, we compared the post-training labor market experiences of
trainees with those of a control group-a matched sample of unemployed individuals who were eligible
for, but did not participate in, the PROBECAT program. Since individuals are not selected randomly
to join the training program, the evaluation methodology must account for any systematic differences
in the attributes of trainees and the control group. Otherwise, group differences are likely to lead to
biased estimates of program effects, because of sample selectivity, or because trainees are
overrepresented in the data relative to their true weight in the unemployed population. Both kinds of
methodological problems were addressed in our evaluation. Our evaluation also sought to estimate
program effects for specific groups of trainees-by sex, age, schooling completed, prior work
j1 For the detailed evaluation see A. Revenga, M. Riboud and H. Tan, 'Mexico Labor Retraining
Program: An Evaluation of its Impact on Employment and Wages," World Bank Report No. 10972-
ME, revised September 1, 1992.



-69 -                               Annex 3.1
PROBECAT & CIMO PROGRAMS
experience, and degree of attachment to the labor force-to provide policymakers with guidelines on
specific groups of unemployed individuals who should be targeted for retraining.
The evaluation was conducted using retrospective data on post-training experiences of the
1990 cohort of PROBECAT trainees, collected by the STPS in February 1992. For a control group,
we developed a matched sample of unemployed individuals from the 1990-91 quarterly urban labor
force surveys (ENEU). The two datasets permit an evaluation of the PROBECAT program that
substantially improves upon previous evaluation efforts. The earlier studies were constrained by lack
of an appropriate comparison group (outcomes were compared for training completers and those who
dropped out of training), and by relatively crude wage data (wage information was bracketed, and
reported in reference to the minimum wage). Both of these limitations have been largely overcome in
our evaluation.
Our evaluation of the PROBECAT program yields several main finding:
(a) On average, the duration of job search is shorter for trainees than for controls. Post-training
job search for male PROBECAT trainees averages 4 months, as compared to an average job search
spell of 6.5 months for the control group. Thus, participation in PROBECAT reduces the mean
duration of search time by 2.5 months. For the average male trainee, this shorter search period
nearly compensates for the length of the training period: total time spent out of a job for the average
trainee (equal to time spent in training plus post-training search time) is only 12 days longer than for
the average control. Moreover, for certain groups (those with work experience and those over 25
years of age), the shorter search spell more than compensates for the time spent in training. Total
time spent out of a job for those two groups of male trainees is shorter than for controls (see Table
1). In contrast, trainees without work experience suffer longer spells of joblessness than do controls.
For women, the picture is fairly similar. Compared to the control group, female trainees with work
experience and those over 25 years of age spend less without a job: total time to first job (including
time spent in training) for these groups of trainees is approximately 1 month shorter than for the
controls.
(b) Male trainees are more Ikely to be employed three and six months after training than the
controls. However, this effect does not persist: at twelve months, the probability of employment is
the same for trainees and controls. Taken together with (a), the implication is that for certain groups
of trainees (those with work experience and/or those over 25 years of age), PROBECAT training
shortens the time to first job, and thus increases the amount of time trainees spend in employment in
the first year after training.
(c) Female trainees with work expeuience are more hlkly to be employed three, six, and twelve
months after program completion as compared to the controls. However, women without prior
work experience are less likely to be employed at comparable intervals after completing training.
(d) Male trainees are more likey to find employment in large ru than comparable controls.
Large firms are Inown to pay higher salaries, and provide more stable employment, more training
and more promotion opportunities. For females, no similar effect is found. This issue will be fully
investigated to establish whether there are labor market rigidities or other barriers to female
employment. This will form part of the full review of PROBECAT to be completed by March 31,
1995.



-70 -                               Annex 3.1
PROBECAT & CIMO PROGRAMS
(e) Trining naes the monthly earnings of male trainees, but this effect varies systematically
by levd of schooing attainment. Large and statistically significant effects (increases of 28 % to 37 %
in monthly earnings following tratiing) are found for males with 6 to 12 years of schooling. To a
large extnt, this earnings effect among males can be attributed to increased weeldy hours worked
rather than as a result of a higher wage per hour, possibly because they are more likely than the
control group to find employment in large firms where monthly earnings and work hours are higher.
For females, training does not appear to raise their monthly earnings. While there is some evidence
of positive effects on hours worked, this does not translate into higher monthly earnings for them.
(f) The monetary baenits of trining outweigh the costs of the PROBECAT program for cetain
groups of trainees. Considering only the employment effects, the benefits associated with finding a
job more quickly compensate the costs of training for males over age 25 and for females with prior
work experience. When earnings effects are incorporated into the calculations, benefits outweigh
costs within a year for all males, but not for females. The analysis will include correlations between
the wage effect and the type and duration of training provided.
Policy Inlications
This evaluation suggests that the unemployed constitute a heterogenous group and that
eligibility criteria for program participation are important determinants of the cost-effectiveness of the
program. In the specific case of Mexico, the analysis suggests that the selection criteria should be
modified to target those demographic groups likely to benefit most from the program, namely
unemployed workers with prior work experience, slightly older workers (aged over 25), and those
with 6 to 12 years of schooling. For certain groups - the very young, new entrants into the labor
force, and those with low levels of schooling attainment - it may be more important to provide other
kinds of training, introduce apprenticeship programs to give new entrants some work experience, or
facilitate return to school. These conclusions have been used to redefine trainee selection criteria for
the new retraining program under the proposed project. To further strengthen the cost-effectiveness
of the PROBECAT program, Bank disbursements above US$ 25 million would be conditional upon
the outcome of the full evaluation of PROBECAT to be completed by March 31, 1995 (see Annex 5
and pam. 337).
More broadly, our study demonstrates that the results can be very sensitive to the way in
which training effects are measured. We identified two principal sources of bias that many manpower
training evaluations are likely to encounter: biases arising from (a) sample selectivity and (b) from
non-representativeness of the tratment group. Addressing these biases is essential in any program
impact evaluation. The Terms of Reference (Annex 5) for the program evaluations to be carried out
under the proposed project contain specific data and methodology requirements to address these
issues.
Our results strongly sugest that a wide variety of potential program impacts be investigated.
Training programs may have an impact on some labor market outcomes but not others. Focusing on
one outcome versus others may make the difference between continuation or trmination of the
program. A broad approach is also potentially more revealing about the direct and indirect ways in
which training programs affect target populations. These considerations have also been incorporated
into the Terms of Reference for the program evaluation (see Annex 5).
Finally, it is worth noting that this evaluation still has some limitations. One, our control
sample is quite small. Two, our duration data for controls had to be imputed by reconstructing



- 71 -                                Annex 3.1
PROBECAT & CIMO PROGRAMS
complot spells from observations on the same individuals in successive quarters. Three, some
important dimensions of PROBECAT -training duration, type of training, mix of theory and practice,
different training provides- were not studied. Future evaluations will attempt to address these
shortcomings. In particular, they will investigate the differential impacts of alternative training
modalities, and evaluate their relative cost-effectiveness.
