MEXICO'S SYSTEMM FOR ML%E SUPPORT, AND CIMO'S ROLE IN ITV
1.    Mexico has a complex system for technical and financial support to its about 1.5 million small
and medium-sized enterprises. Conceptually, this system of support is similar to MSME support
systems in other countries, but in practice, its effectiveness has been constrained by a scarcity of
resources and lack of operative coordination. These constraints have affected in particular
microenterprises of less than 15 persons.
2.    During the 1980s successive Governments, aware of the potential of MSMEs as major
creators of employment, equity, and wealth, have taken important new initiatives to reinforce the
MSME support system, especially to make it more responsive to the needs of microenterprises, and
also have acted to streamline administrative procedures to facilitate the creation and growth of small
enterprises. Among these initiatives are the Federal Law for the Development of the Microindustry,
the relevant parts of the National Program for Training and Productivity, 1991-94, the National
Program for the Modernization and Development of the Micro, Small and Medium Sized Industry,
1991-94, as well as the creation in 1987 of the CIMO program itself. As a result of these and earlier
initiatives, a division in principle of responsibilities for MSME support now exists. Table 1 shows
the main fields of action and the agents primarily responsible for each.
3.    In spite of these efforts, MSME support in Mexico is still not effective for various reasons,
including:
(a)   insufficient numbers of suitably trained staff in the agencies;
(b)    scarcity of information materials and resources, including lack of a system for
information, both regarding technical/management/ market information, and
concerning labor market conditions;
(c)   remaining unclear division of responsibilities for implementation of the programs,
which causes lack of coordination and overlap of activities particularly at the
operational level, and lack of implementation of some of the measures mandated by
the Federal Law;
(d)    difficulties in effectively reaching out to enterprises at the firm level, especially as
regards micro and small firms;
(e)   in many agencies, a supply-oriented and reactive approach, instead of offering
services guided by enterprises' needs and demands;
1/ This Annex is based on a study done for STPS as part of preparation for the proposed project and
documented in a report entitled "Evaluaci6n de los Mecanismos de Apoyo a la Micro, Pequefia y
Mediana Empresa en M6xico", April 1992, by Consultant Antonio Maza Pereda, and on other
feedback from enterprises obtained by CIMO, including that documented in the report "Tendencias de
Modernizaci6n en la Empresa Grande y Modalidades de Capacitaci6n en la Micro, Pequefia y
Mediana Empresa", 1992 (see Annex 9).



-75 -Annex 3.2
PROBECAT & CIMO PROGRAMS
(f)   too high a level of specialization in the technical assistance offered, to the detriment of
more basic needs of enterprises;
(g)   lack of systematic efforts to consolidate or combine demands for assistance from
different firms in order to achieve economies of scale; and
(h)    an absence of actions to obtain multiplier effects which could partly compensate for
the scarcity of resources, including dissemination of relevant information. The only
exceptions are NAFIN's effort to promote the creation of credit unions among
MSMEs, and CIMO's proposed information dissemination program and its program
for upgrading the supply of process consultants and trainers at the local level.
4.    In spite of the continued existence of these problems, substantial efforts have been made in
two areas: MSME finance, through NAFIN and 'Asesorfa Dinamica a la Micro EmpresaH ADMIC,
and training, mainly through CIMO (and NAFIN, through its incipient program of mass distribution
of management training materials for MSMEs); tens of thousands of firms have benefitted from
assistance in these two respects. Nevertheless, this is only a small fraction of the large number of
MSMEs; some needs such as systematic industrial and labor market information are hardly touched;
and there are inevitable biases which favor support to the larger or technically more advanced
enterprises to the detriment of the smallest and least technified. Overall, the shortcomings listed
above tend to have particularly serious consequences for the very small enterprises, which are most in
need of assistance and which require a very direct, individualized, and practical type of support.
5.    The results of a detailed survey of 115 enterprises participating in the CIMO program clearly
demonstrates that the scarcity of MSME assistance affects the smallest enterprises most severely. For
instance, while the medium-sized enterprises surveyed had all begun to develop human resource
uevelopment policies and programs to some extent, 90% of small enterprises with over 15 workers
had no structured training program before participating in CIMO; for microenterprises this number
was close to 100%.
6.    The fundamental problem is a lack of communication, in both directions: few of the support
agencies have the policy or the means to listen to firm owners and managers. Among all of the
agencies mentioned, only the ADMIC, essentially a financing agency for microenterprises, and
CIMO have the resources and mandate to visit enterprises to seek out their needs; and conversely,
there is an acute scarcity of information materials and systems adapted to the needs of small
enterprises. Under the project, an increased number of MSME promoters will be trained to provide a
broader range of services to MSMEs, including training, organizational strengthening and business
orientation. The project fully supports the decentralization of CIMO activities to UPCs, which will
be closer and more attentive to the needs of MSMEs.

-77-                                 Annex 3.3
PROBECAT & CIMO PROGRAMS
1CIMO PROGRAM AND IT C0OSHARING W1E EN TPISE
Purpose of the Program
1.    The CIMO program, as proposed for inclusion in the Labor Market and Productivity
Enhancement Project, is a program for demand-driven assistance to micro, small, and medium-sized
enterprises (MSMEs). It is designed specifically to support productivity enhancement activities in
these enterprises, and to catalyze access to other actors in Mexico's system for MSME support, for
instance technology development support agencies and MSME financing institutions such as NAFIN
and the Agency for Technical Assistance to Micro Enterprises (ADMIC). CIMO does not provide
financing for direct productive investment.
Program History
2.    CIMO grew out of the Pilot Inservice Training and Productivity Program supported under the
Manpower Training Project, and implemented during the period 1987-1992. This pilot program was
designed mainly as an effort to increase the awareness of MSME owners and managers of the benefits
of providing inservice training programs for workers, and documenting and disseminating the
outcomes of such programs in terms of increased productivity and profitability, improved product
quality, and strengthened competitive position of participating enterprises. This program was
implemented by means of a number (currently 28) of geographically dispersed professional groups
called UPCs (Training Promotion Units), each composed of 2 to 5 MSME specialists (promoters)
contracted by STPS as fixed-term consultants.
Organization of the Program
3.    The UPCs are all physically based in collaborating enterprise associations (mostly Chambers
of Commerce and Industry) which have formally agreed with STPS, as a condition for the
establishment of each UPC, to provide logistical and in-kind financial assistance (office space,
secretarial help, communications) and to participate in the local Executive Committee which guides
the work of each UPC at the local level. The program is highly decentralized, with only a light
structure for oversight, technical assistance and financial control which is located in STPS's
Directorate of Occupational Analysis (DAO). The program is also highly participative in terms of
substantive work; for instance, all assistance to MSMEs is based on systematic situation and problem
diagnoses carried out by enterprises themselves with the help of promoters.
Financing of the Program
4.    The Secretariat of Labor and Social Welfare finances the fixed costs of the promoter corps
(salaries and travel) and the central oversight and support functions in STPS, plus general information
and dissemination costs.
5.    CIMO-assisted improvement programs directly benefitting individual enterprises, such as
worker training, have been financed by the CIMO program on a declining basis. For regular
inservice training, the program started by financing about 60% of the training costs; it currendy
finances an average of 30 to 40% of the expenses for instructors only, with enterprises fully financing
consumable materials and rental of equipment and facilities. This percetage will be further reduced



- 78 -                               Annex 3.3
PROBECAT & CIMO PROGRAMS
(to about 23 % by the end of 1997) as a function of the time each UPC has been active in a locality
(and presumably, built up its credibility) thereby reducing the need for special incentives to
enterprises to use its services.
6.    The financing of the program has thus been shared from the beginning between STPS and
enterprises, with enterprises paying a grow portion of total costs over time. Figure 1 summarizes the
evolution of cost-sharing of inservice training under the pilot program between 1988 and 1991. This
Annex presents a more detailed study of total program costs and resulting effective financing during
calendar year 1991. The outcomes are as follows:
-     if only direct outlays for all program activities are counted, the total program cost was
US$8.1 million, financed 58% by STPS. 37% by enterprises, and 5% by training providers (through
rebates on facilities rentals, etc.);
-     if the time spent by workers in organized training activities is valued at the average wage of
participants in the PROBECAT retraining program (which is a very low valuation), the total cost rises
to USS10.1 million financed 46% by STPS. 50% by enterprises, and 4% by training providers; and
-     if the calculation is made to include also the time of MSME managers and supervisors spent
with CIMO promoters and in self-assessment and evaluation activities directly tied to the program,
and the cost of this managerial time is valued at a salary equal to that of the promotor (which again is
a low estimate), the total program co  increases to USS12.7 million, financed 37% by STPS. 60% by
enterurises, and 3 % by training providers.
7.    It should be noted that the above is probably an underestimate of the average enterprise
contribution to overall costs also for another reason: 1991 was a year during which the program still
had very substantial and exceptional start-up costs, notably for an intensive training program for all
promoters in the UPCs. The relative importance of these fixed costs is expected to decline over time.
8.    Despite high and increasing private sector participation in the financing of CIMO activities,
and thus favorable prospects for continuing this trend, it is likely that a small percentage of public
financing (between 15 and 20% of total costs) will be required beyond the life of the project. The
number of MSMEs in Mexico is very large, and the process of modernization of their forms of
production will take time. Ultimately, however, the Government would maintain only an information
and technical assistance role.

SECTOR ORGANIZATION
ORGANIZATION AND SRCUEOF THE NATIONAL EMPIdOYNMT SE3RVCE (SNIn
1.    Employment services and retraining are the responsibility of the National Employment Service
(SNE) and its network of State Employment Services (SEEs), set up in coordination with the State
Governments. Employment services offered by the SEEs include registration of job seekers and
vacancies, career counseling, referral, and placement in jobs or training. Programs are financed
mainly under the federal budget, supplemented with contributions from the state budgets. The system
has operated since 1983. As of December 1991, it comprised 99 operating units, organized into 32
SEEs, one for each state and the Federal District, and involved a staff of 851 individuals, divided
evenly between state and federal staff.

PROJECT DESCRIPI1ON
SCTON  RITRIAFOR TRADIIX
LABOR MARKET ADJUSTNMT COMPONENT
Rel_ for DW acend Ueamplozoe Workers
Eligible trainees would satisfy the following criteria:
(a) registered as a job applicant at the local state employment office;
(b) have prior work experience if entering classroom training (minimum 3 months);
(c) be between the ages of 18 and 55 years;
(d) have either complete primary or some secondary education; and
(e) all trainees without prior work experience would be eligible only for the in-plant training
modality.
The rationale for these criteria is based on the results of the evaluation of the retraining program
(classroom modality only) presented in Annex 3.1
Baic Edcao
This program would target those individuals who:
(a) register as a job applicant at the local employment office;
(b) have prior work experience (minimum 3 months);
(c) are between the ages of 16 and 40; and
(d) have no formal education or have not completed primary school.
The rationale for these criteria is that the training should benefit these seeking employment who are
under-qualified both from the point of view of specific skills an in terms of basic education.
Participants are largely, but not exclusively, anticipated to be rural migrants seeking more
remunerative employment in urban areas. Under the project, this is a pilot program which will be
fully evaluated.
In both programs, priority would be given to family heads with economic dependents and therefore
more urgent needs.



- 86 -                              Annex 5.2
PROJECr DESCRIPTION
TRAENING FOR YOUNG INEXPER3:CD WOREER
1.    Workers who lack the necessary skills to qualify for existing vacancies and who have no prior
work experience would qualify for training under the in-plant training modality supported by the
project.
2.    In-plant training has been successfully used under the Manpower Training Project (Ln. 2876-
ME). Under this training modality, enterprises communicate their needs for workers with specific
skills to their local employment office. SEE staff then select, among the unemployed, the most
appropriate candidates for training. Basic selection criteria included heads of household 18 or more
years old with economic dependents.
3.    Course content is determined by the enterprise and always includes a combination of
classroom and on-the-job training. Course duration varies between two and six months with the
average duration being three months. While enterprises are not required to hire all trainees upon
training completion, experience has shown that the majority of trainees are hired.
4.    Under the in-plant training modality enterprises cover the costs of instructors and materials,
provide the necessary physical training facilities and normally pay the trainees a small amount to
cover transportation and meal costs. The Government provides a stipend equal to the minimum wage
to each participant.
5.    The proposed project would target this type of training to those workers who lack work
experience and, thus, do not qualify for the retraining program. SEEs would actively promote the in-
plant training program and would encourage enterprises with skilled worker needs to participate in the
program. The program would have the dual-purpose of providing skills to the most disadvantaged
youth and unemployed and giving them the opportunity to obtain the necessary work experience to
become active labor force participants.
6.    Cost recovery options for this group of trainees would not be feasible because of the high
administration and transaction costs involved.
7.    Evaluations of all of PROBECAT'S training modalities would be carried out during the
project implementation period (the first one in 1994) to measure the impact of alternative training
programs and delivery systems on the employment and earnings of program participants and to assess
the cost effectiveness of the programs. Terms of reference for evaluation of the program were agreed
upon during appraisal and will be confirmed during negotiations (Annex 5.3).



- 87 -                               Annex 5.3
PROJECr DESCRIPTION

every six months to the central level.



- 88 -                              Annex 5.4
PROJECT DESCRITON
TE RMB OF REFEENCE FIOR PROBECAT EAUTO
OBJECTIVES OF THE STUDY. Th objectives are: (i) to design surveys for use in evaluations of
the PROBECAT program; (ii) to meaure the impact of alternative training programs and delivery
systems on the employment and earnings of PROBECAT participants; and (iii) to assess the cost-
effectiveness of PROBECAT and its different training modalities (classroom versus in-plant).
A. Swreys
The first part of the study is intended to develop data bases needed for evaluations of the
PROBECAT program. Samples of trainees should be administered a retrospective survey at least one
year after training completion. The survey should elicit information on respondents' demographic
attributes, household characteristics, and both pre-intervention and post-intervention labor market
histories. Similar data should be collected for a comparable group of unemployed individuals that did
not participate in PROBECAT, to serve as a control group for the PROBECAT trainees. The
following specific actions are needed:
(1) A retvspectve survey of trinees should be fidded In 1994 to the 1993 cohort of
trainees. This survey should be modeled on INEGI's 1992 PROBECAT survey of trainees who
completed training in 1990. That survey elicited a wealth of information on the individual, on all
jobs held since completion of training until February 1992, including start and end dates for each job,
monthly salary, weeldy hours worked, occupation, and industry of employment. The survey should
be augmented to elicit more pre-training information, in particular about the last job held prior to
entering the PROBECAT program, if any; this information should include, at a minimum, industry,
firm size, monthly salary, hours worked, and start and end dates for the job.
(2) The 1994 PROBECAT survey should be administered to a sample of trainees that is
representative of both training modalities; it should cover at least 5 % of the men and women who
completed training in the middle of 1993 - or about 2,000-3,000 individuals. This survey should
limit coverage of the post-training period to about one year. Whilst it would be desirable to monitor
these trainees over a longer period, in practice it is both difficult and costly to do so. The survey
would be completed between September and December 1994, ie. 12 months after the 1993 trainees
have completed their courses. This would enable the analysis and tabulation of results to be
completed by March 31, 1995 for a full review with the World Bank. A second PROBECAT
survey should be fidded In 1995 to a similar size sample of trainees completing training in 1994. A
third survey, possibly modified to address specific issues that arise during the program's
implementation, could be fielded in 1997.
(3) A control grwp sould be developed for the 1994 PROBECAT sample. In principle,
such a control group can be selected from the unemployed individuals in the quarterly urban labor
force survey (ENEU). As structued, each rotation group of households in the ENEU is followed
over five consecutive quarters. Each quarter, household members are asked a set of questions about
employment status, monthly salary, hours of work per week, firm size, and industry. Thus, the 1994
PROBECAT and ENEU surveys should be broadly comparable, both in their one year coverage of
the post-training/unemployed period and in the kinds of labor force questions asked. Four ENEU
rotation grups -households etering in the first, second, third and fourth quarters- should yield a
control group sample of about 2,000 unemployed men and women at existing unemployment rates.



- 89 -                              Annex 5.4
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
(4) Two minor modifications to the ENEU should be negotiated between STPS and INEGI
no later than December 31, 1993, to make the ENEU more useful for this evaluation. First,
information on the start dates of jobs should be elicited if an individual reports being employed in a
given quarter. As currently structured, information is available on the duration of the current
unemployment spell but not the current employment spell at the time of the survey. This presents
problems for determining when an individual leaves unemployment and enters employment. Second,
information should be elicited not only about the current job, if employed, but also about the last job
if currently unemployed. In its present form, the ENEU provides no information about the last job
held by an individual, a particularly acute omission for the unemployed people beginning the first
quarter of their five-quarter rotation, and for those who remained unemployed in subsequent quarters.
(5) Two altenatives exist for getting information on Ist job. INEGI can either ask all
respondents about current or last job (as in the U.S. Current Population Survey), or ask the
unemployed a limited set of questions about firm size, industry, hours of work, and monthly salary in
their last job. The first approach requires modification of ENEU's job-related questions and skip
patterns; the second approach, the addition of four questions about the last job. The second approach
generates a broader data set on the unemployed and thus is preferable.
B. Evaluation Studies
The evaluation studies are intended to measure the effects of program participation on labor
market outcomes, and to assess the cost-effectiveness of the PROBECAT program. Another objective
is to evaluate the impact of different training modalities and alternative training delivery systems so as
to improve the quality of services provided the unemployed. Design of the evaluation study should
reflect the following considerations:
-- (1) A range of labor market outcomes should be evalusted. These should include (but not
be limited to) the following outcomes: (a) time to first job, (b) employment probability over
progressively longer periods of time, (c) the proportion of time spent unemployed over one year, (d)
job stability, (e) monthly salary and hourly wage, and (f) hours worked per week.
(2) A control group should be used in the evaluation. Selection bias in measurement of
program impacts arises because PROBECAT participants rota random sample of the unemployed
population. As such, the effects of trferred by comparing outcomes to the
experiences of an appropriate control group.
(3) Three approaches exist for addressing the issue of selection bias. One approach uses a
control group with similar attributes to the trainee population. This technique uses program selection
criteria to identify an "eligible" group of non-trainees. A second approach is to correct for sample
selectivity using a two-stage econometric technique in which outcomes are measured conditional upon
selection into training. A third approach is to compare post-training outcomes (e.g., wages) to
comparable pre-training measures. This method recognizes that individuals are heterogeneous (e.g.,
because of unmeasured ability, tastes, and motivation), and that they are their best controls. To the
extent possible, the sensitivity of results using the various approaches should be compared.
(4) The surveys described in Section A will yield roughly equal numbers of trainees and
controls. Account should be taken of the oversampling of trainees in these data relative to their true
proportion in the population (about 10% in 1989). The data should be reweighed, and regression
models estimated using choice-based weights.



- 90 -                              Anne 5.4
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
(5) The cost-fecivenss of the PROBECAT program should be adused. A
methodology for assigning a monetary benefit to each of the different impacts of program
participation should be developed and justified by DGE prior to the commenen of the survey, ic.
before June 30, 1994. For example, the private benefit from shorter unemployment spells attributable
to the intervention may be valued at the average trainee's post-training wage. These benefits should
be compared to the per-trainee costs of PROBECAT to derive a benefit-cost estimate. Program costs
should include both direct and indirect costs.
(6) AlMternive training modalities and delivery system should also be evaluated. Issues to
be addressed include (a) the relative impacts of various kinds of training provided; and (b) the relative
efficacy of alternative training delivery systems, and whether outcomes vary across training
institutions once the background characteristics of trainees, training mix, type of course, gender of
trainee and regional effects are taken into account. Special attention would be placed on attempting to
identify the causes of different employment and wage effects of training for men and women.
C. Comultant Proposals
Evaluations should be conducted under the direction of the Directorate of Employment Studies
and Policies, in coordination with the DSNE, and with technical assistance, as required, by outside
consultants. Technical assistance needs are estimated at 8 to 9 person-months per evaluation study.
Consultants should have demonstrated expertise in manpower training evaluation studies,
familiarity with current developments in evaluation methods, and experience using the econometric
methodologies described in Section B.
D. Schedule
The retrospective survey of 1993 trainees would be fidded in the second and third quarters of
1994, and cover their whole labor marlet history for the year following training. Prior to the
commencement of the survey, the questionnaire would be submited to the Bank for approval. These
data would be available for analysis by December 1994. The first evaluation study would be
completed by March 31, 1995 (subject to Section 5.01(a) of the Leon Agreeennt). The results and
implications for program design would be discussed with the Bank at that time and would condition
further Bank's financial support to the program (for details see Annex 3.1).
A second PROBECAT survey would be fielded in he third and fourth quarters of 1995 in
parallel with the Basic Education survey to a sample of trainees completing training in 1994. The
corresponding evaluation would be completed by September 30, 1996, and results discussed with the
Bank during the 1996 annual review.
A third survey, slightly modified to address specific issues that arise during the program's
implementation would be fidded in 1996. The evaluation study would be completed by September
30, 1997, with results to be used for the overall project evaluation and project completion report.

PROJECT DESCRIPTION
CIMO EVALUATION
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR MONITORING AND E3VALUATION
OBJECTIVES. A first objective is to monitor the implementation of CIMO interventions on a
continuous system-wide basis, so as to assure the quality, timeliness and effectiveness of CIMO
services. A second objective is to measure the impact of CIMO interventions on the performance and
productivity of enterprises and to assess the efficacy and overall cost-effectiveness of the program,
and its different support modalities. To this end, the evaluation framework for CIMO comprises a
range of monitoring and evaluation instruments, namely: (i) program-wide annual operational
reviews; (ii) semi-annual progress reports at the UPC level; (iii) detailed case studies compiled at the
UPC level; and (iv) rigorous program impact-evaluation analyses of CIMO, including a cost-benefit
study of CIMO interventions. The Bank would review each of these evaluations at the appropriate
annual reviews, and agree with the Government on any action plans or project modifications to be
undertaken as a result.
1. MONITORING
A. Program-Wide Annual Operational Reviews
The annual operational reviews would analyze previous year performance, budget allocation
and expenditures for the program as a whole, and present the detailed set of monitoring indicators and
progress review criteria agreed with the Bank. Annual performance reports would be compiled at the
central level on the basis of program performance information generated through the financial and
administrative control system.
B. Semi-Annual Progress Reports at the UPC Levd
Each UPC would prepare semi-annual progress reports summarizing the key monitoring
indicators at the local level and contrasting actions programmed with actions actually undertaken over
the previous six-month period.
C. Detailed Case Studies
Each UPC would also document a limited number of case studies, aimed at illustrating both
quantitative and qualitative effects of CIMO interventions. The case studies would focus on one or
more areas of impact, documenting effects of CIMO actions on individual firms, groups of firms,
training suppliers and/or public institutions supporting MSMEs. The case studies would provide
important insights into the more qualitative affects of CIMO interventions, and contribute to CIMO's
dissemination/demonstration strategy.
2. PROGRAM IMPACT EVALUATION STUDY
The objectives of the impact evaluation study are: (i) to design a data collection strategy to
evaluate the effects of the CIMO program; (ii) to measure the impact of interventions on improving
the performance and productivity of enterprises and its workers; (iii) to assess the overall cost-
effectiveness of the CIMO program; and (iv) to develop improved productivity measures by relating
the qualitative improvements to quantitative outcomes such as output or inputs.



-92 -                               Annex S.5
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
A. Data Reqe       :
The first part of the study is intended to develop data bases needed to evaluate the CIMO
program and to improve the effectiveness of CIMO support for micro, small, and medium-sized
enterprises. This effort should be designed to institutionalize evaluation capabilities at the program
level and at the level of the individual UPC. It will require: (a) routine, and ongoing collection of
key economic data for each diagnosis; (b) use of a common format for recording data across UPCs;
(c) familiarizing all promoters with evaluation concepts and methodologies; (d) surveys of selected
enterprises to measure post-intervention outcomes; and (e) use of a control group of enterprises in
assessing the impact of CIMO interventions. Specifically,
(1) Background data on a time-series of key economic variables should be collected from all
enterprises at the diagnosis that precedes the intervention. This practice of colecing a common set
of variables across UPCs should be institutionalized for all interventions, beginning in 1993. The
kinds of data collected may vary by intervention type, being more limited for pure training services
(type A interventions) and more comprehensive for integrated services and support for groups of
firms (type B interventions).
(2) Diagnoses should include information on key variables for a minimum of the two most recent
yeas. For Type B interventions, the data should include year-end levels of production, inventories,
number of workers, new hires and quits, costs of raw materials, and estimated value of equipment
and machinery. For Type A interventions, where intrusive data requests may not be appropriate,
these data shoula include (at the minimum) year-end levels of production and the number of workers
(from which labor productivity is calculated). Contemporaneous data on the enterprise-average wage
rates, and other qualitative information-should also be collected for all interventions.
(3) In each UPC, two representative groups of r    should be sdected, one a group of firms that
experienced a CIMO intervention and one to serve as a control group. The first group of firms
should be administered a follow-up survey one year after the intervention to elicit information on
intervention outcomes. The control firms should be selected from diagnoses conducted in the same
year as the follow-up survey. The impact of CIMO on the first group of firms may be inferred from
comparing outcomes with pre-intervention data provided by the control group. Thus, survey costs
would only be necessary for the follow-up sample.
(4) Follow-up surveys should provide information on the levels and changes in key economic
outcomes one year after the inervention, as well as information on the qualitative changes and
improvements that are directly attributable to the intervention. The latter may include questions from
the original CIMO survey, as well as new questions on other outcomes. The design of this survey
instrument should begin when the original evaluation is completed, and its methodology, limitations,
and findings thoroughly discussed. A small pilot should be fielded to test and refine this survey
instrument.
(5) The first follow-up survey should be fielded in 1994 to a sample of enterprises that experienced
an intervention in 1993, and elicit retrospective data for the years 1992-1994. By 1993, all UPCs
would have adopted a common format for the diagnoses that will be used as a control group for the
follow-up survey. About 225 enterprises dxould be surveyed, including about 175 Type A
interventions (pure training services) and about 50 Type B interventions Qmtegrated and group
services). Each UPC will be responsible for fielding the survey to an average of 7 enterprises. A



- 93 -                    ~~~~~~Annex 5.5
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
similar-sized (225) sample of control firms should be selected from the universe of enterprises given a
diagnosis in 1994.
(6) A second survey should be fielded in 1996 to a similar sized sample of interventions in 1995.
Diagnoses already exist for these enterprises, so the follow-up survey need focus only on collecting
outcomes data for 1995-1996. The outcomes should be compared to the control group of enterprises
given a diagnosis in 1996. The objective of this second survey should be to evaluate new kinds of
CIMO interventions and to improve existing services.
B. Evaluation Analyses
The objective of the second part of the study is to evaluate the impact of CIMO interventions
on the performance of enterprises, using the data described in Section A. Analysis should focus on
evaluating the cost-effectiveness of the CIMO program, comparing the relative effectiveness of
different kinds of CIMO interventions, and developing better measures of productivity by relating
changes in physical output measures to improvements in various qualitative outcomes. The analyses
should be designed to yield concrete recommendations for improving the quality of CIMO support for
enterprises.
The evaluation should include the following tasks:
(1) Desgning a progrm  impact m     nt methodology. This methodology should take into
account the following considerations:
(a)    The impact of an intervention can be measured by comparing outcomes against the
pre-intervention measures of the same enterprise or that of a control group of firms.
The analyst should make an assessment of whether the alternative measures yield
different conclusions.
(b)   A control group should be selected from the data bases obtained through the initial
diagnoses. To the extent possible, the control group selected should resemble the
enterprises with an intervention, particularly with respect to industry and firm size.
(c)   In measuring impacts, checks for accuracy, consistency of responses across questions,
and avoidance of double-counting should be made. These concerns should be
reflected in the methodology for quantifying each outcome and summing across
outcomes to get a total program impact.
(d)    Both quantitative and qualitative outcomes should be analyzed. The quantitative
impacts include increased production (sales) and labor productivity, and reduced
inventories, materials use, and job turnover. The qualitative impacts may include
better product quality, systems for cost accounting, identification of new products and
markets, new forms of work organization, improved worker incentives, morale and
attitudes towards work, and work conditions.
(e)    Different measurement and evaluation strategies may be needed for Type A and Type
B interventions. The analyst should work closely with CIMO and UPC promoters to
tailor evaluations for each intervention type.



-94-                                 Annex 5.S
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
(2) Evaluation of the cost-effectiveness of CIMO should relate quantifiable benefits of the
intervention to the total direct and indirect costs associated with that support. The total cost of each
CIMO intervention has to be measured, taking into account the cost of all components (the UPC's
equipment, furniture, promoter's salaries, etc.) that may be justifiably allocated to it (e.g., on the
basis of promoter's time or cost of the intervention). Private costs should also be measured,
including all direct costs borne by the enterprise (tuition, wages and salaries of workers involved, and
materials use), and the opportunity costs incurred by workers when participation occurs outside
regular working hours (imputed using average hourly wage rates).
(3) Sensitivity-analysis should also be conducted. Cost-benefit estimates can be sensitive to
assumptions about both benefits and costs. The sensitivity of estimated cost-benefit ratios or internal
rates of return calculations should be assessed for a variety of alternative assumptions. These may
include assumptions about the period over which benefits are accumulated, the time path of such
benefits, and the inclusion of increasingly more comprehensive categories of direct and indirect costs.
(4) Different kinds of CIMO services should be analyzed for insights into the relative
effectiveness of altenative interventions. These analyses should be possible through a judicious
selection of firms provided different kinds of CIMO support. Separate estimates of the quantitative
and qualitative impacts of different kinds of CIMO interventions (e.g., Types A and B) should be
made, and their relative cost-effectiveness compared.
(5) An effort should be made to develop improved measures of productivity. The empirical
relationships between quantitative and more qualitative outcome measures should be studied (e.g.,
What impact does introducing a just-in-time inventory system have on changes in inventory levels?
How effective is it to train workers without parallel improvements in work organization or
supervisory control? Do improvements in product quality and timeliness of supply translate into
increased sales for the enterprise?) The separate, and joint, effects of qualitative improvements on
quantitative outcomes should be investigated using regression models. The results may be used to
infer the likely productivity or cost-saving effects of changes in qualitative outcomes that are the
target of integrated CIMO services.
C. Consultant Proposals
The UPCs will be responsible for data collection. The Directorate of Employment Studies
and Policies, in coordination with CIMO, will be responsible for the development of the data bases,
and the evaluation methodology and analysis. Technical assistance, as required, should be provided
by outside consultants. Consultants should have demonstrated expertise in evaluation studies,
familiarity with current developments in evaluation methods, and experience using the econometric
methodologies described in Section B.
D. Schedule
Enterprise performance data would be collected in all UPCs on a systematic and continuous
basis beginning in 1993. Prior to the com n     of the survey, the questionnaire would be
submitted to the Bank for approval. The first follow-up survey would be fielded in 1994 to a sample
of enterprises that experienced an intervention in 1993. The first evaluation study would be
completed by June 30, 1995 and results included in the mid-term review.

OBJECTIVES OF THE STUDY. The objectives are: (i) to design a data collection strategy for use
in the evaluation of the Basic Education program; (ii) to measure the impact of program participation
on employment, earnings and working conditions of trainees; and (iii) to assess the cost-effectiveness
of the Basic Education Program. The Bank would review the survey at the 1996 annual review, and
agree with the Government on any action plans or project modifications the be undertaken as a result.
A. Survey
The first part of the study is intended to develop the database needed for the evaluations of
the Basic Education program. Samples of trainees should be administered a retrospective survey at
least one year after training completion. The survey should elicit information on respondents'
demographic attributes, household characteristics, and both pre-intervention and post-intervention
labor market histories. Similar data should be collected for a comparable group of unemployed
individuals (with little or no formal education) that did not participate in the Basic Education
Program, to serve as a control group for the trainees. The following specific actions are needed:
(1) As part of the regular PROBECAT evaluation effort, a retrspective survey of Basic
Education Program trainees sbould be fielded in 1996 to the 1995 Cohbrt of trainees. This survey
should be modeled along the lines of the PROBECAT survey described in the PBCAT
Evaluation: Terms of Reference for Data Collection and Studies in thisi -amex. The survey should
elicit a wealth of information on the individual, including demographic and household characteristics;
on last job held prior to entering the PROBECAT program if any, including at a minimum, industry,
firm size, monthly salary, hours worked, and start and end dates for the job; and on all jobs held
since completion of training, including start and end dates for each job, monthly salary, weekly hours
worked, occupation, and industry of employment. The 1996 suz ey should be administered to a
representative sample of 1995 Basic Education trainees (it should cover at least 10% of the men and
women who completed Basic Education training in 1995).
(2) A control group should be devdoped for the 1996 Basic Education Trainee sample. In
principle, such a control group can be selected from comparable unemployed individuals -i.e., those
with no formal education or with just incomplete primary schooling- in the quarterly urban labor
force survey (ENEU). Four ENEU rotation groups -households entering in the first, second, third
and fourth quarters- should yield a control group sample of about 200 unemployed men and women
with no or little formal education at existing unemployment rates and schooling levels.
B. Evaluation Study
The evaluation study is intended to measure the effects of program participation on labor
market outcomes, and to assess the cost-effectiveness of the Basic Education program. Design of the
evaluation study should reflect the following considerations:
(1) A range of labor mket outcomes should be evaluated. These should include (but not
be limited to) the following outcomes: (a) time to first job, (b) employment probability over
progressively longer periods of time, (c) the proportion of time spent unemployed over one year, (d)

PROJECT DESCRIPION
job stability, (e) insertion into formal sector jobs, including size of firm and where employed (f)
changes in occupation and/or industry, (g) monthly salary and hourly wage, and (h) hours worked per
week. For the underqualified unemployed workers targeted by the Basic Education Program, the
impact of program participation on moving from informal sector jobs to formal sector employment is
likely to be of key interest. The evaluation should explicitly consider this issue.
(2) A control group should be used i the evaluat. Seection bias in measurement of
program impacts arises because program participants are not a random sample of the unemployed
population. As such, the effects of training should be inferred by comparing outcomes to the
experiences of an appropriate control group. Two approaches should be used in addressing the issue
of selection bias. One approach should use a control group with similar attributes to the trainee
population. This technique uses program selection criteria to identify an eligible" group of non-
trainees. The second approach should compare post-training outcomes to comparable pre-training
measures. This method recognizes that individuals are heterogeneous (e.g., because of unmeasured
ability, tastes, and motivation), and that they are their best controls. To the exte  pouible, the
sensitivity of results using the two approache should be compared.
(3) The sample data for trainees and controls should be reweighed to reflect the true
proportions of trainees and controls in the overall unemployed population, and regression models
estimated using choice-based weights.
(4) The cost-effectivene  of the Baic Educaion progrm  should be asessed. A
methodology for assigning a monetary benefit to each of the different impacts of program
participation should be developed and justified. These benefits should be compared to the per-trainee
costs of the program to derive a benefit-cost estimate. Program costs should include both direct and
indirect costs.

Evaluations should be conducted under the direction of the Directorate of Employment Studies

PROJECT DESCRIPTION
1. NATIONAL EIPLOYIMT SURVEY AND TRAPNING MODULE
OBJECTIVES. The objective of the survey is to elicit household-based information on employment
and unemployment characteristics of the population 12 years of age and over at the national level.
The survey is complemented by a module on education and training, which seeks to obtain
information on education and training of workers, and on the links between these investments and
worker earnings. By eliciting data on employment and unemployment with national coverage, the
survey would address some key gaps in Mexico's labor market data, and contribute to a better
understanding of the economic factors driving individual labor force behavior, and therefore to a
better evaluation of labor market policies and programs.
THE SURVEY. A National Employment Survey (ENE) was fielded in the second quarter of 1988,
and again in the second quarter of 1991. To ensure the availability of a continuous series of
comparable information, the new survey would be fielded in the second quarter of 1993, and every
two yeas thereafter (1995 and 1997). The Bank would review each of these surveys at the
appropriate annual review, and agree with the Government on any action plans or project
modifications to be undertaken as a result. The survey would have the following features:
A. SmRling. The sampling scheme, sample coverage and data collection methods would follow
those of the 1988 and 1991 ENEs. The survey would be collected from a probability, multi-stage,
stratified sample of approximately 50,000 households. It would cover two population universes,
urban areas and less-urbanized areas. The first universe would comprise the 32 main urban areas plus
all localities with 100,000 inhabitants or more and the state capitals. This sample would consist of
approximately 45,000 households. The second universe, for less urbanized areas (under 100,000
inhabitants), would comprise the rest of the country, grouped into nine regions according to a set of
income, health, education and employment indicators. This sample would consist of 5000
households.
B. Survey Instrument. The survey questionnaire would be modeled along the lines of the 1988 and
1991 ENEs, and would include a household characteristics section, a basic urban employment section
to be used in urban areas, a basic rural employment section to be applied in the less-urbanized areas,
and a module on training and education to be used for all households. The survey instrument for the
training module would follow that of the 1991 ENE trining module (which improved upon the
1988), but would attempt to distinguish between total amount of training received and amount of
training received with the current employer. The survey questionnaire would be sent to the Bank for
review prior to the fielding of each ENE.
RESULTS. Basic survey results would be published, and the microdata contained in these surveys
made available to external research institutes and academic institutions, within a year of data
collection.

TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE. The surveys would be conducted under the direction of the
CGPEET, in coordination with INEGI. Expert technical assistance, as required, would be used in

2. NATIONAL SURVEY OF EMPLOYMENT. COMPENSATION. T                 NOLOGY AND
llG
OBJECTIVES. The objective of the survey (Encuesta Nacional de Empleo, Salarios, Tecwlogfa y
Capacitation) is to elicit basic data on the characteristics of employment, compensation, technology,
and training in a representative sample of establishments in the manufacturing sector. Tbis
information will be important both for describing the current status of enterprises and their evolution
over time, and for developing government policies to modernize Mexico's manufacturing sector and
improve its productivity and international competitiveness. The Bank would review this survey at the
1994 and 1996 annual reviews, and agree with the Government on any action plans or project
modifications to be undertaken as a result.
SURVEY STRUCTURE. The structure of the national survey should be modelled broadly on the
survey developed with assistance from the ILO, that was fielded in the spring of 1992. Information
should be collected on each of the following six topics:
(1) Production: level of production and sales; main products; domestic and foreign
ownership; primary inputs including machinery and equipment, labor, raw materials, and energy;
changes in inputs and outputs over the past three years; capacity utilization; and estimates of capital
equipment by type of technology.
(2) Employment: current level and changes in employment over time; the stucture of
employment by occupation, full-time and part-time status, and sex; hours of work; job turnover and
recruitment policies.
(3) Compnation: average compensation by occupation, sex, and length of job tenure; the
level and structure of compensation, including salaries, bonuses, allowances, and severance pay;
factors that shape compensation policy.
(4) Technology: investments in technology acquisition, including spending on research and
development and technology licensing; the importance of quality control; problems and constraints on
improving technology and productivity.
(5) Organization: forms of work organization; consequences for employment; quality control
and promotion policies.
(6) Iraining: sources and type of training provided different groups of workers; frequency
of training; hours of training; consequences of training for productivity and product quality;
constraints and incentives to provide training; factors that shape training policies.

IMPLMENTATION. The first survey should be fielded in 1994 to a random sample of about
7,000 manufacturing establishments, and be followed by a second survey in 1996.
(1) The sample sdected should be representative of the underlying population of
manufacturing establishments, in terms of the establishment sizes, occupational categories, and
industries used in the System of National Accounts.
(2) Development of survey insuments for the 1994 and 1996 surveys should build upon
INEGI's experiences with the 1992 survey. STPS should work closely with INEGI to modify the

future surveys.
(3) The 1992 survey data should be analyzed extensively, both for checks of consistency in
the data and for the economic content of responses. For example, can sensible production functions
be estimated using three years of production data? Is productivity relatively higher in establishments
using more technologically sophisticated equipment? Is labor turnover lower in plants which pay
higher wages or provide more training? Answers to questions such as these should help refine or
augment questions to be asked in future surveys.
(4) A random sample of about 600-700 establishments in both the 1994 and 1994 surveys
should be selected to respond to a short worker survey (Encuesta a Trabajodores Manufacturros).
The experience with the 1993 worker survey should provide lessons on how this supplementary
worker-level survey may best be incorporated into the National Survey.

survey would be fielded Oointly with the survey of workers in manufacturing) in April-June, 1994.
Microdata would be available by December 31, 1994. The calendar would be similar for the 1996
survey.
3. SURVEY OF VWO          IN MNUFACTURING
OBJECTIVES. The objective of the survey (Encuesta a Trabojadores Mamofareros) is to elicit
basic information on a sample of workers employed in establishments that responded to the National

TM SURVEY. The first worker survey would be fielded in 1993 to a ten percent sample of
manufacturing firms that responded to the 1992 National Survey of Employment, Salaries,

- 101 -                              Annex 5.7
PROJECI DESCRIPTION
(1) A random sample of about 600 establishments would be selected to respond to the Survey
of Workers. This sample would be restricted to those establishment with relatively complete
responses for the establishment-level survey. Small establishments may be over-sampled to
compensate for potentially lower survey response rates. The resulting sample of establishments would
generally reflect the distribution of establishment sizes and industry in the National Survey.
(2) The Number of workers interviewed would vary with the size of the establishment: 10 in
establishments with less than 200 employees, 20 in establishments with 200-500 employees, and 25 in
establishments with over 500 employees. Workers to be interviewed would be selected randomly
from the payroll of the establishment. This sampling frame would yield a sample of about 10,000
workers.
(3) A pilot study would be fielded in early 1993 to determine the best approach for
administering the worker survey, as well as to refine the survey instrument. One approach is to mail
out a pre-interview survey instrument to selected establishments. Personnel managers will be asked to
(i) sdect a random sample of employees to respond to the survey, and (ii) provide basic data about
each worker's characteristics, and both their employment and wage histories. This survey will be
augmented by in-plant interviews with individual employees.
KEY VARIABLES. Workers in each establishment will be asked to provide additional data about
their employment, earnings, and training histories. This would include the following variables:
(1) Worker Chatstcs. These include age, sex, marital status (and dependents), years
and level of schooling attainment, date completed the highest level of schooling, and whether worked
anytime prior to completing school.
(2) Prior Work History. These include data on prior work experience, the attributes of the
most recent employer-industry and firm size-years of tenure, occupation, and compensation (wages,
non-wage benefits and bonuses), date left last job, and the main reason for leaving the last employer.
(3) Current Employer. These include date joined the current employer, attributes of first job
in the company (occupation, time needed to become fully competent, wages and non-wage
compensation), attributes of the job currently held (occupation, time in occupation, time needed to
become fully competent, and current wage and non-wage benefits).
(4) SkiDs and Training. Workers may get training from: (A) schools, vocational or
technical institutes; (B) industry association centers; (C) formal training in company centers; (D) on-
the-job instruction by supervisors and coworkers; or (E) informal learning on-the-job. Training data
would be elicited at three points in time, in all previous jobs, in the first year with the current
employer, and over the past one year. For training with the current employer, data would be
collected on the total hours of training received (1) at work or on company time, and (ii) not on
company time.
(5) Worker sueslions. Information on suggestions made by workers for improving (I)
materials and input use, (ii) product quality, and (iii) sales, and both the nunber of sugestions by
type, and how they were implemented.



- 102 -                              Annex 5.7
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
RESULT. Basic survey results would be published, and the microdata contained in these surveys
made available to exenal research institutes and academic institutions, within a year of data
collection.
TECNICAL ASSISTANCE. The surveys would be conducted under the direction of the
CGPEET, in coordination with INEGI. Expert technical assistance, as required, would be used in
survey design.
SCHEMULE. Preparation work for the 1993 survey on workers in manufacturing would begin in the
last quarter of 1992. The survey questionnaire would be sent to the Bank for review and approval by
January 31, 1993. The survey would be fielded between August and October of 1993 (to allow for
the ENE survey to be fielded in April-June, 1993). Microdata would be available by December 31,
1993. In 1994 and 1996, this survey would be fielded as a module to the National Survey of
Employment, Compensation, Technology and Training.
4. SURVEY ON ,.GRATION
OBJECTIVES. Tbe objective of the survey is to elicit timely information on the size, characteristics
and determinants of migration flows to the U.S.-Mexican border and to the U.S., with emphasis on
the links between these flows and labor market conditions. The Bank would review the survey at the
1993 annual review, and agree with the Government on any action plans or project modifications to
be undertaken as a result.
THE SURVEY. The first migration survey would be fielded in 1993. On the basis of this survey,
the Bank and STPS would revise and agree on the methodology and design of future migration
surveys (to be fielded in 1995 and 1997). The 1993 survey would have the following features:
(1) The survey would be collected from a sample of approximately 14,400 individuals drawn
from the population of migrants. Three types of migrant populations would be sampled: the
population comprised of those seeking to migrate to the U.S.; that comprised of those voluntarily
returning to Mexico; and that comprised of those being deported.
(2) The survey would be collected from each population in three Northern regions (East,
Central and West) and would be fielded at two different times corresponding to low and high
migration season. About 1600 individuals from each population would be interviewed in each region
(800 per season) during a 12 month sampling period, yielding sample sizes of 4800 per population
type.
(3) The sampling scheme, survey methodology, and survey instrument would be refined on
the basis of the results from the pilot recently applied in Tijuana, and of the needs of STPS.
KEY VARIABLES. Individuals would be asked to provide data about their demographic
characteristics, migration and labor market histories. This would include the following variables:
(1) Demographic Characteristics. These include age, sex, marital status (dependents), years
and level of schooling attainment. The survey would also elicit information on the characteristics of
the home at the point of origin.



- 103 -                              Anex 5.7
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
(2) Labor Market History. These include employment status, occupation, sector/industry,
hours worked, tenure, and earnings before and after migration. The survey would also include a
question on remittances to Mexico.
(3) Migration Characteristics and History. These include point of origin
Oocality/municipality), type of migration flow (individuals/family), reasons for migrating, type of
networks at the place of destination (family, individuals from same town of origin etc), and date of
migration. The survey would also include, depending on the type of population sampled, questions
on characteristics of the trip and crossing into the U.S., time spent in transition and in destination
point, and reasons for returning to Mexico.
RESULTS. Basic survey results would be published, and the microdata contained in these surveys
made available to external research institutes and academic institutions, within a year of data
collection.
TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE. The 1993 survey would be conducted under the direction of the
CGPEET, in coordination with the Colegio de la Frontera Norte. Expert technical assistance, as
required, would be used in future survey design.
SCHEDULE. Preparation work for the survey would begin in September, 1992. The survey
questionnaire would be sent to the Bank for review by June 30, 1993. The survey would be fielded
at two different times between September, 1993 and September, 1994 (to cover both low and high
migration seasons). Results would be generated in parallel with the fielding of the survey, beginning
in March, 1994. On the basis of the 1993 survey, the Bank and STPS would agree on the design,
methodology and calendar for future migration surveys (scheduled for 1995 and 1997).
5. SURVEY OF MICROENTERPRISES
OBJECTIVES. The purpose of the survey is to elicit basic information on employment, earnings,
training, financing and cost patterns in microenterprises. The Bank would review the survey at the
1994 and 1996 annual reviews, and agree with the Government on any action plans or project
modifications to be undertaken as a result.
THE SURVEY. A microenterprise survey was fielded in 1992. The new survey would be fielded in
1994, and again in 1996. The survey would have the following features:
(1) The survey would be applied to a probability subsample of the household-based National
Urban Employment Survey (ENEU). This subsample would comprise those individuals who reported
to be either self-employed or the owner of a firm employing 1 to 15 workers in manufacturing and 1
to 5 workers in other sectors.
(2) The survey would be collected from the last ENEU sample for the preceding year. For
example, in 1992 the survey comprised 17500 households selected from the ENEU December sample.
Sample sizes would remain similar in future surveys.
(3) The information collected on the self-employed, owners and workers in small
establishments could be matched with their household characteristics drawn from the household
registration module of the ENEU.



- 104-                               Anne 5.7
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
KEY VARBLES. Individuals would be asked to provide data about their employment, earings,
and training. This would include the following variables:
(1) Individual/household characteristics. These would include age, sex, education, marital
status, dependents, employment status, sector of employment and earnings.
(2) CharaceristIcs of the Microenterprise. These would include size, date established,
reasons for establishing miaote, sources of financing, type of building, accounting system,
equipment, saes and profit, inventories, breakdown of expenditure, suppliers, clients, work
schedules, characteristics of salaried workforce and of unpaid workers, including prior labor force
experience and training.
RESULTS. Basic survey results would be published, and the microdata contained in these surveys
made available to external research institutes and academic institutions, within a year of data
collection.
TECHNICAL ASSIWANCE. The survey would be conducted under the direction of the CGPEET,
in coordination with INEGI. Expert technical assistance, as required, would be used in survey
design.
SCHEDULE. Preparation work for the 1994 survey would begin in the last quarter of 1993. The
survey questionnaire would be sent to the Bank by January 31, 1994. The survey would be fielded
between July and September, 1994. Microdata would be available by February 28, 1995. The
calendar would be similar for the 1996 survey.
6. RETROSPCIVE SURVECY OF INDIVDUAS
OBJECTIVES. Many labor market phenomena are inherently dynamic in nature, and cannot be
adequately studied using cross-sectional data. Repeated surveys can help characterize the ways in
which particular phenomena evolve over time but, since different groups of respondents are used each
time, longitudinal data are seldom available on the same individuals. In particular, little is known
about the constraints and incentives that they face at different times, the way they respond to changing
economic conditions and different government policies, and the future outcomes of these earlier
decisions.
The objective of the Retrospective Survey is to develop longitudinal data on individuals in a
random sample of households covered by the Urban Labor Force Survey (ENEU). The first survey
in 1994 will elicit retrospective histories of individuals; subsequendy, individuals will be followed at
two-year intervals to got information about intervening labor market experiences. By tracking them
over time, policymakers and researhers will be better able to study the individual detmina   and
outcomes of a variety of key labor market issues-including labor force entry and exit, unemployment
and employment, job turnover, education d training, earnings and earnings growth, and migration-
and lead to improved progm evaluations and labor market policies. The Bank would review the
survey at the 1994 annual review, and agree with the Government on any action plans or project
modifications to be undaken as a result.
SURVEY   UCTUR. In developing the longitudinal survey for Mexico, STPS should review
and draw upon the expri       that many industrialized and developing countries have bad with



- 105 -                              Annex 5.7
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
fielding longitudinal surveys. Examples include retrospective surveys such as the Malyasian Family
Life Surey, conducted in 1978 and again in 1988, and prospective surveys such as the National
L&ngWidial Survys (NLS) and the Panel Survey of Income Dynamics (PSID) of the United States.
The Retrospective Survey of Individuals should include the following four components: (1)
family income and wealth; (2) retrospective histories of males; (3) retrospective histories of females;
and (4) migration. Information on each component should be updated in subsequent follow-up
surveys. Information to be collected in each component should include (but not be limited to) the
following key variables:
(1) Family Income and Wealth: Data on household structure and earnings collected as part of
ENEU should be supplemented by information on other forms of family income and wealth. These
include family ownership of businesses, houses, land, durables, and other assets; business earnings
and income from rental of rooms, houses, or land; other forms of family income including gifts,
interest income, and pensions.
(2) Retrospective Histories of Males: The questionnaire should elicit a life history from all
economically active males in the household. Retrospective information should be collected from age
15 to the present, and should cover schooling and training, marital status changes, all employment
and unemployment spells, earnings and amount of time worked in each job, occupations, and the
characteristics of employers.
(3) Retrospective Histories of Females: This questionnaire should be administered to all
economically active females in the household. In addition to the questions asked of male respondents,
retrospective information should be collected on all pregnancies, withdrawals from the labor force,
and the main activities while out of the labor force.
(4) Migration: This component elicits information on the extent of regional mobility and
ascertains the causes and consequences of families' migration. It should cover all moves made by the
family, and then focus on the most recent migration event. Questions should be asked about distance
moved, accompaniment of family members, reasons for moving, where lived on arrival, economic
support received, and employment status after moving.
IMPLEMINTATION
(1) The first survey should be fielded in 1994 to all economically active individuals in a
random sample of about 2,000 urban households. This sample will be drawn from one cohort of the
Urban Labor Force Survey (ENEU).
(2) In addition to the ENEU questions, male and female respondents will be administered
separate retrospective questionnaires about their life histories. Household heads, together with their
spouses (if present), will be responsible for answering questions about family income and wealth, and
migration.
(3) Respondents should provide start and end dates for the major events which occur in time.
Interviews should emphasize cross-referencing dates and ages of respondents across events in different
areas, and checking the consistency of the life history over time.



- 106 -                              Ar 5.7
PROJECr DESCRPTION
(4) The 1994 survey data should be analyzed extensively, both for checks of consistency in
the data and for the economic content of responses. Are trends over time in key indicators-such a
unemployment, labor force participation, or earnings-revealed by the retrospective data consistent
with trends developed from external sources? How serious is the recall problem? Are analyses based
on retrospective data capable of yielding additional insights into labor market issues? What other
kinds of data are needed to better investigate these issues? Answers to questions such as these should
help refine or augment questions to be asked in future surveys.
(5) The second survey should be fielded in 1996. Life histories elicited in the first survey
should be updated to include their labor market experiences over the intervening two years. Added
questions raised by the analyses in (4) should be incorporated at this time. In checking the
consistency of the 1996 survey, the 1994-95 labor market information collected by ENEU for this
sample should be compared to that provided by the 1996 survey for the same time period.
SCHEDULE. Preparation work for the 1994 survey would begin in the last quarter of 1993. The
survey would be sent to the Bank for review and approval by January 31, 1994. The survey would
be fielded by INEGI, as a module of the ENEU, in the second quarter of 1994. Microdata would be
available by December 31, 1994. The survey would be fielded again, to the same sample of
individuals, in the second quarter of 1996.

TERMS OF REFERENCE F'OR SITUDIESS
(TO BE COFRI AND COMILETE3D DURING NGlITOS
1. NEW FORMS OF ENTERPRISE MANAGEMENT AND ORGANIZATION, AND THEIR
IMPACT ON PRODUCTIVITY GOT
BACKIGROUND AND GENERAL OBJECI1VE
As set out in the recently signed National Agreement for Increased Quality and Productivity,
the Government's labor market strategy has as a key objective the modenization of traditional forms
of organization and management, as a means of facilitating the adoption of new technologies and
fostering productivity growth. STPS is expected to play an important role in this modernization
process through promoting, advising and assisting enterprises and unions on organization and
management practices. The Bank would review this study during the 1993 annual review, and agree
with the Government on any action plans or project modifications to be undertaken as a result. This
study would strengthen STPS capacity to provide this technical assistance by:
(a)    documenting recent developments in enterprise management and organization in a cross-
section of countries;
(b)   reviewing, through a series of case studies, the use of these new forms of management and
organization in Mexico, with emphasis on the more export-oriented and competitive sectors;
(c)    linking these developments with increases in productivity, product quality and
competitiveness.
DATA SOURCES
The study would draw principally on the existing literature and on studies for other countries.
It would also rely on case studies (some of which could be based on CIMO enterprises), and on the
analysis of basic data on organization, training, technology, and promotion and wage schemes from:
the National Employment Survey and Training Module;
the National Survey on Employment, Wages, Technology and Training; and
the National Survey on Microenterprises, among others.
RESULTS
The output would help strengthen STPS advisory capacity in the area of organization and
management. It would also provide information relevant to the CIMO program.



- 108 -                              Annex S.8
PROJECT DESCRIPIlON
PROJECr LENGTH
The study will be carried out during February-May, 1993. It is expected to involve
approximately 8 person-months of work.
TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE
The study would be conducted under the direction of the CGPEET, with technical assistance
support from an outside consultant with experience in the field of industrial organization and
management.
2. ENMPLOyYM IN THE AGRICULTURAL SECTOR
BACKGROUND AND GENERAL OBJECTTVE
In 1992, the Government initiated a radical reform of the regulations and institutions
governing the agricultural sector. These reforms are expected to have a significant impact on the
labor market through: (a) the appearance of new forms of salaried employment in rural areas; and (b)
via rural/urban and/or rural/rural migration flows. To respond appropriately in its policies and
programs to these possible labor market developments, STPS needs to identify, and to the extent
possible forecast, the potential labor market effects of the reforms.
To this end, the study would seek to:
(a)    document the current structure of employment in the rural areas, and examine recent changes
and their possible link to the reforms;
(b)   analyze existing rural migration patterns and mechanisms, examine their effects on
employment in the secondary and tertiary sectors, and in particular, their effects on export-
oriented industries and the maquiladora;
(c)   on the basis of (a) and (b), identify qualitatively and quantitatively the potential effects of the
reform process, and the populations and regions most likely to be affected.
DATA SOURCES
The study would review and compile all available information on the subject, drawing
especially on the information obtained by the Secretariat of Agriculture (SARH).
To examine land tenure patterns, it would draw on data from the Population Census, the
National Agriculture Census, the Economic Census and the data compiled through the SARH.
To analyze employment behavior in rural areas, the study would rely on the National
Employment Survey (1988 and 1991), and on the migration module of the National Urban
Employment Survey.



- 109 -                              Annex 5.8
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
RESULTS
The output would help strengthen STPS capacity to develop policies and programs to
adequately respond to the changes currently underway in the agricultural sector. It would also help
address some key gaps in existing Inowledge of rural labor markets.
PROJEC LENGTH
The study will be carried out during January-June, 1993. It is expected to involve
approximately 12 person-months of work.
TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE
The study would be conducted under the direction of the CGPEET, with technical assistance
support from an outside consultant.
3. THMEXICAN LABOR MARKET: PATTERNS OF BEHAVIOR
OBJECTIYE
The general objective is to develop an operational model which will help to identify and
estimate the effects of implementting different measures and economic policy tools. The model will
be based on a wide theoretical basis. Its main goal is to measure supply and demand variations in the
labor force caused by:
(a)    Specific economic policy actions such as price variations, goods and services
rates produced by the public sector, taxes, exchange rates, interet rates,
inflation control, fiscal deficit, foreign investment, salaries, etc.
(b)    Behavioral pattern of the national aggregate demand and by sector.
(c)    Trade policy, especially tariffs, trade balance and balance of payments
and foreign trade.
(d)    Adapting the legal framework in different areas: foreign investment,
technology transfers, rural, educational, labor and public sector, etc.
(e)    World economic trends, in particular strategic products prices, supply,
interest rates, exchange rates, employment, salaries and economic
growth.
MODEL IMPLICATIONS
The study will result in the formation of a simulation model, quantifying tie impact of
macroeconomic, regional or sector actions, as they relate to labor force demand. Through model



- 110 -                              Annex 5.8
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
development, the main variables and variable relationships will be identified to explain, changes in the
demand and supply of labor force. Different combinations of these variables will be tested, especially
those pertaining to economic policy and interactions with economic policy goals and objectives and
with the national and international economic behavior will be examined. It will also provide
macroeconomic consistency to that currently being carried out by the GEA Group, contracted by this
STPS, on the impact of NAFTA in the labor market.
SQURCES
To form the document base, the results obtained by several consulting firms and higher
education institutions dedicated to modeling, such as: GEA Group, Wharton, Macroasesor(a
Econ6mica, and El Colegio de Mcxco will be used.
From the review of the goals and objectives in the main Government programs and policies,
both on labor issues as well as general economics, dependency relationships between variables will be
derived. These will be the foundation upon which the model will be built. The different currents of
economic theory will be discussed to support the formation of the theoretical framework supporting
the model and which shall adhere to the behavior of the economy and to the general policy on
employment.
An analytical follow-up of changes and paths of current domestic and international economic
and labor issues will be developed. To support the development of the database, a review will be
made of the statistical information related to selected variables, of the time series that can be built and
of their consistency.
